Refutation of the Shi'a Claim of Textual Designation
Baqillani argues that a textual designation of the imam would have been widely known and transmitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Baqillani argues that the Shi'a claim of a textual designation (nass) for the imamate is invalid because such a designation would have been widely transmitted among the early community.

Core Idea
If the Prophet had designated an imam by name, it would be known by everyone, not just a few.

In this lesson, we examine al-Baqillani's powerful refutation of the Shi'a claim that the Prophet explicitly designated Ali as his successor. Using the principle that matters of such importance would be widely transmitted, he demonstrates the implausibility of the solitary reports cited by the Shi'a. This passage is a classic example of how kalam methodology applies the criterion of tawatur to settle foundational theological disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the flow and argument. Note the key terms like 'nass' and 'ahad'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key premise
Baqillani assumes that a real textual designation from the Prophet would inevitably become widely known. Find the sentence where he says it is 'not permissible to neglect it' and that it would be discussed by scholars.
This premise is central to the argument.
3
Contrast with actual transmission
Notice how he contrasts this expected widespread transmission with the actual situation: the claim is only narrated by a few individuals, specifically the Shi'a, and attributed to a handful of companions.
This contrast shows the weakness of the claim.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the claim is invalid because it should have been known even to non-Muslims and secluded women. Reflect on how this reasoning disproves the Shi'a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلاف من خالف خالف فيه، إلى نظائر ذلك مما رواه وقاله الآحاد وظهر واشتهر ظهور مثله على ما جرت العادة بمثل رواية الآحاد في الصدر الأول للنص من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم على إمام بعينه لا بد أن تتلقاه الأئمة بالقبول أو برده بأسرها أو ينكره بعضها ويصححه الآخرون منها ويقع التشاجر بينهم في ذلك لأنه ليس يجوز إغفاله وقلة الإحفال به وترك البحث والتأمل لروايته وحال راويه بل كان يجب أن نعلم ضرورة أن هذا قد ادعِي في صدر الإسلام واسْتُدِل عليه ببعض الاحتجاج وكيف جرى أمر الأمة في قبـولـه أو رده أو الاختلاف فيه وألا يرد ذلك وُرُوداً خاصاً تَفْتَعِله الشيعة بينهم وتضيفه إلى عمار والمقداد وغيرهم من أصحابه ويمنون أنفسهم الأباطيل فيـه بـل يجب أن تعلمه العذراء في خدرها من المسلمين ومن ليس من أهل الإسلام أيضاً من أهل السِّيّر وكل ذلك يدل على بطلان دعوى من ادعى منهم أن النص نقله واحد وآحاد في الأصل.
And the disagreement of those who disagreed is disagreement in it, and similar to that which was narrated and said by individuals (ahad) and appeared and became famous in a manner similar to what is customary with the narration of solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) in the early generation regarding the text (nass) from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning a specific imam: it is inevitable that the imams (scholars) would receive it with acceptance or rejection entirely, or that some of them would deny it and others would authenticate it, and disagreement would occur among them regarding that, because it is not permissible to neglect it, pay little attention to it, and abandon research and reflection on its narration and the state of its narrator. Rather, it was necessary that we know with certainty that this was claimed in early Islam and that some argumentation was used to support it, and how the affair of the community proceeded in accepting it, rejecting it, or disagreeing about it, and that it did not come down as something particular (special) that the Shi'a fabricate among themselves and attribute to Ammar, Miqdad, and others of his companions, and they delude themselves with falsehoods about it. Rather, it should be known by the veiled virgin among the Muslims and even by non-Muslims who study histories. All of this indicates the invalidity of the claim of those who claim that the text was transmitted by one person and as solitary reports in origin.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an argument refuting the Shi'a claim that the Prophet explicitly designated Ali as the imam (nass). The author, Imam al-Baqillani, argues that if such a textual designation truly existed, it would have been widely known and transmitted by multiple trustworthy narrators, not just a few individuals (ahad). He points out that in early Islam, any such important matter would have been received by the early scholars (al-a'imma) with either full acceptance or rejection, leading to widespread discussion and debate. It would not have been neglected or treated lightly. Instead, the fact that it is only narrated by a few, and specifically by the Shi'a who attribute it to figures like Ammar and Miqdad, shows that it is a fabricated, isolated report. The author stresses that even a secluded Muslim woman or non-Muslim historians would have known about such a claim if it were true. Therefore, the claim that the nass was transmitted by a single person and as a solitary report is false.

Key Terms
النص (al-nass)
الآحاد (al-ahad)
الشيعة (al-shi'a)
الإمامة (al-imama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a solitary report (khabar ahad) can be accepted in matters of imamate just as in legal rulings.
Correction: The author shows that imamate is a foundational matter requiring widespread transmission (tawatur), not ahad reports.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the author against the Shi'a claim of a textual designation (nass) for the imamate?
A) The nass would have been widely known and transmitted among the early community.
B) The nass is mentioned in the Qur'an explicitly.
C) The nass was unanimously accepted by all companions.
D) The nass was only narrated by trustworthy narrators.

Key Takeaway
A foundational claim like the designation of an imam must be proven by widespread transmission, not by solitary reports.

?
The veiled virgin would know if the Prophet had designated an imam. Are there things in your faith that you think are true but are not actually known to the general body of Muslims? How can you be sure that what you believe is not a fabrication of a small group?
?
Baqillani says that if the designation were true, it would have caused disagreement and debate. Do you avoid seeking truth because it might lead to uncomfortable discussions? Are you willing to investigate even beliefs that feel comfortable, to see if they truly come from the Prophet ﷺ?

Impossibility of Hiding the Alleged Designation
Baqillani refutes the Shia claim of nass by impossibility of concealment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's proof that if the Prophet had explicitly designated Ali as caliph, that announcement would have been widely known (mutawatir), and its absence refutes the Shia claim.

Core Idea
A momentous public claim cannot remain hidden; its obscurity proves its falsehood.

Baqillani addresses the Shia claim that the Prophet explicitly designated Ali as his successor through a textual designation (nass). He argues that such a crucial event would have been impossible to conceal. In this passage, he demonstrates that the very nature of the claim—its transmission as a solitary report—contradicts what we would expect if it were true. Let’s follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia claim
The Shia claim that the Prophet made an explicit textual designation (nass) for Ali to be imam. Baqillani is responding to this claim.
2
Identify expected outcomes if true
If such a designation occurred, it would have been a major event. The community's leaders would have either accepted or rejected it, and it would have been widely discussed and transmitted.
Think about how momentous news spreads.
3
Recognize the actual situation
Instead of widespread transmission, the Shia themselves admit the nass was transmitted only as a solitary report (khabar ahad) via a few individuals. This contradicts what we would expect.
The key is the contrast between expectation and reality.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the claim is false. The obscurity of the report proves it was never made publicly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الأمر كما زعمت الشيعة من النص على إمام بعينه لوجب أن تتلقاه الأئمة بالقبول أو برده بأسرها أو ينكره بعضها ويصححه الآخرون منها ويقع التشاجر بينهم في ذلك لأنه ليس يجوز إغفاله وقلة الإحفال به وترك البحث والتأمل لروايته وحال راويه بل كان يجب أن نعلم ضرورة أن هذا قد ادعِي في صدر الإسلام واسْتُدِل عليه ببعض الاحتجاج وكيف جرى أمر الأمة في قبـولـه أو رده أو الاختلاف فيه وألا يرد ذلك وُرُوداً خاصاً تَفْتَعِله الشيعة بينهم وتضيفه إلى عمار والمقداد وغيرهم من اصحابة ويمنون أنفسهم الأباطيل فيـه بـل يجب أن تعلمه العذراء في خدرها من المسلمين ومن ليس من أهل الإسلام أيضاً من أهل السِّيَر وكل ذلك يدل على بطلان دعوى من ادعى منهم أن النص نقله واحد وآحاد في الأصل.
And if the matter were as the Shia have claimed, of the textual designation (nass) for a specific imam, then the leaders (of the community) would necessarily have either accepted it unanimously or rejected it unanimously, or some of them would have denied it and others would have affirmed it, and disputes would have arisen among them concerning it, because it is not permissible to ignore it or pay little attention to it or to abandon researching and pondering its transmission and the state of its transmitter. Rather, it would have been necessary for us to know with certainty that this was claimed in early Islam and was argued with some evidence, and how the affair of the Ummah proceeded in accepting it, rejecting it, or disagreeing about it, and that it would not come as a private report that the Shia fabricate among themselves and ascribe to 'Ammar, al-Miqdad, and others of their (the Prophet's) Companions, and they delude themselves with false hopes concerning it. Rather, every virgin secluded in her tent from among the Muslims and also those not from the people of Islam, from the historians, must know it. And all of this indicates the falsehood of the claim of those among them who claim that the text (nass) was transmitted by a single person and is solitary (ahad) originally.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by reductio ad absurdum against the Shia claim that there was an explicit textual designation (nass) of Ali as caliph. Baqillani argues: If such a momentous announcement had truly been made by the Prophet, it would have been widely known and transmitted by many chains (mutawatir). It could not have been kept secret or known only to a few. The community's leaders would have either accepted it en masse or rejected it with clear arguments; there is no middle ground of ignoring such a crucial matter. In fact, the historical record shows no such widespread knowledge. The Shia themselves admit it was transmitted as a solitary report (khabar ahad) through specific individuals like 'Ammar and al-Miqdad. But a report of such significance cannot remain obscure. Even secluded women and non-Muslim historians would have known it. Therefore, the claim that the nass was transmitted by a single chain is false, proving the Shia doctrine invalid.

Key Terms
النص
خبر الآحاد
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Baqillani is against all solitary reports (khabar ahad) in legal matters.
Correction: Baqillani is not rejecting solitary reports entirely; he is arguing that a claim of this magnitude—a public announcement by the Prophet—requires multiple chains of transmission to be credible. Solitary reports are acceptable in some contexts but not for founding a major doctrinal claim.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would necessarily happen if the Prophet had explicitly designated Ali as imam?
A) It would have been transmitted as a solitary report.
B) It would have been widely known and discussed, not hidden.
C) Only the Shia would have known about it.
D) It would have been recorded only by historians.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the Shia claim of explicit designation for Ali by showing that the alleged event's obscurity contradicts the necessary widespread knowledge it would have generated.

How does this passage call you to humility in accepting that truth is usually public and accessible, not esoteric?

Why Shia Narrators Fail the Condition of Trustworthiness
Baqillani responds to the objection that Shia reports are like accepted solitary reports in Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that Shia narrators are not trustworthy narrators of solitary reports, based on the requirement of 'adala (moral probity) and their stance towards the Companions.

Core Idea
Narrator credibility depends on moral probity, not merely report transmission.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a specific Shia objection: why do you accept some solitary reports but reject ours? He responds by clarifying that the acceptance of solitary reports is conditional upon the narrator's trustworthiness. The Shia narrators, due to their beliefs about the Companions, fail this condition. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection starts with 'فإن قال منهم قائل'.
2
Identify the Shia objection
The objector argues that their report about the text (nass) for Ali should be treated like any solitary report accepted in Sharia. Identify the exact phrasing.
3
Identify Baqillani's two-part answer
First, he reiterates the conditions for accepting solitary reports. Second, he points out that Shia narrators do not meet the condition of trustworthiness ('adala) because of their beliefs about the Companions.
Look for the phrase 'قد قلنا فيما قبل' and 'ونحن لا نعرف أحداً'.
4
Analyze the concept of 'adala
Trustworthiness includes not only honesty but also moral probity. Baqillani lists specific beliefs that disqualify Shia narrators: cursing Abu Bakr, Umar, and other Companions, claiming they apostatized, etc.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Baqillani's argument is not sectarian bias but a consistent application of the rules of hadith criticism. A narrator's beliefs can affect their reliability.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال منهم قائل : فاجعلوا خبَرَ الشيعة عن النص بمنزلة أخبار الآحاد التي تعملون بها في الشريعة وإن لم تقطعوا على صحتها . قيل له : قد قلنا فيما قَبْلُ إنا إنما نعمل بأخبار الآحاد إذا كانت على صفات مخصوصة وعَرِيَتْ مما يدل على فسادها أو معارضتها وثبتت عدالة نقلتها ونحن لا نعرف أحداً قال بالنص على عليّ عليه السلام إلا وهو يتبرأ من أبي بكر وعمر وسائر أهل الشورى سوى عليّ ويَشتِمُ الصحابة ويَزْرِي على أفعالهم ويزعم أنهم ارتدوا بعد الإسلام على أعقابهم ويضيف إلى ذلك مذاهب أُخر نرغب عن ذكرها لئلا يظن قارىء كتابنا أنا نقصد الشناعة عليهم دون الاحتجاج على فساد قولهم .
If an objector from among them says: 'Regard the Shia report about the text (nass) as equivalent to the solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) that you act upon in the Sharia, even if you are not certain of its authenticity.' It is said to him: 'We have already stated previously that we only act upon solitary reports when they meet specific conditions and are free from anything indicating their corruption or contradiction, and the trustworthiness ('adala) of their transmitters is established. And we do not know anyone who asserted the text (nass) in favor of Ali, peace be upon him, except that he disassociates himself from Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and the rest of the people of the Shura (consultative council) besides Ali, and he insults the Companions, disparages their actions, claims that they apostatized after Islam, turning back on their heels, and adds to that other doctrines which we decline to mention lest the reader of our book think that we are aiming to vilify them rather than argue for the falsity of their statement.'
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector, representing the Shia, argues: 'Why do you reject our report about the nass? You accept solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) in matters of religious law even without certainty; why not accept ours?' The answer is twofold: (1) Solitary reports are only accepted when they meet strict conditions, especially that the transmitters are trustworthy ('adala). (2) The transmitters of the Shia nass reports are not trustworthy because they openly curse and disparage the Companions, including Abu Bakr and 'Umar, and even claim that most Companions apostatized. Such beliefs disqualify them from being accepted as credible narrators. Baqillani declines to detail all their heretical doctrines, noting that his aim is rational refutation, not mere vilification.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
العدالة
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani rejects all Shia reports due to sectarian bias.
Correction: Baqillani rejects them based on a consistent application of the conditions for accepting solitary reports, particularly the requirement of narrator trustworthiness ('adala), which Shia narrators fail due to their beliefs about the Companions.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the Shia reports about the nass?
A) Because all solitary reports are rejected in matters of leadership.
B) Because the narrators lack trustworthiness due to their beliefs against the Companions.
C) Because the reports contradict the Quran.
D) Because the reports are not transmitted by women.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends the Sunni rejection of Shia nass reports by appealing to the standard condition of narrator trustworthiness, which is absent due to Shia beliefs about the Companions.

8 min
Why the Shia Transmission of the Nass Fails the Condition of Trustworthiness
Baqillani concludes that the original reporters of the nass were also partisans, so the report is rejected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Baqillani argues that the Shia narrators of the nass are not trustworthy due to their doctrine of loyalty and disassociation, and how that invalidates their entire transmission chain.

Core Idea
Bias from doctrine disqualifies a narrator; trustworthiness is the foundation of reliable transmission.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani delivers the final blow to the Shia claim of a textual designation (nass) for Ali. He argues that the very doctrine of loyalty and disassociation (al-wala' wa al-bara') held by the Shia narrators renders them biased and untrustworthy. Since Islamic law requires a narrator to be 'adl (just) and mardiyy (pleasing), the Shia transmitters fail this condition, and thus their reports—from the first generation onward—are rejected. Let's examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Review the condition of 'adala
Recall that Islamic law requires a single narrator (khabar al-wahid) to be 'adl (trustworthy) and mardiyy (pleasing) for their report to be accepted. This is the standard criterion.
'Adala includes integrity, uprightness, and absence of bias.
2
Understand the Shia doctrine of al-wala' wa al-bara'
The Shia narrators hold a doctrine that requires loyalty to Ali and disassociation from his opponents. This doctrine, Baqillani argues, compels them to favor their allies and oppose others, undermining their impartiality.
This doctrine is central to Shia identity.
3
See the effect on trustworthiness
Because their religion is completed only through loyalty and disassociation, they will testify only for those who agree with them. This bias destroys the 'adala required for accepting their reports.
Baqillani emphasizes that even their own claim about the narrators shows they were partisans.
4
Trace the conclusion: rejection of both salaf and khalaf
Since the first generation (salaf) transmitters were biased, their reports are rejected. The later generation (khalaf) inherited from them, and additionally, they declared the majority of Muslims disbelievers or corrupt, further showing their lack of integrity. Thus, the entire chain is invalid.
This is a cumulative argument: both the early and late narrators are disqualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وببعض هذه الأمور تسقط العدالة وتزول الثقة والأمانة لأن هذا الدين لا عندهم يتم إلا بالولاء والبراء، ومنهم من يرى الشهادة لمُوَافِقة على خَصْمِـه والشريعة إنما أوجبت العمل بخبر الواحد إذا كان عَدْلاً مَرْضِيّاً وليس هذه صفة القائلين بالنص ولا صِفَةَ الآحاد الذين رَوَوْا لهم ذلك في الأصل، على دعواهم؛ لأنهم يزعمون أن راوي هذه الأخبار لهم كان من القائلين بولاية علي وممن يرى في الصحابة رأيهم ويدين بالبراءة فلا منهم يجب أن يُقبَلَ خَبَرُ هذا السلف ولا يُقْبَلَ الخَلَفُ لما قد ثبت من إيمان من دانوا بإكفاره وعدالة من دانوا بتفسيقه والبراءة منه .
And due to some of these matters, trustworthiness ('adala) falls away, and confidence and honesty disappear, because this religion, in their view, is only completed through loyalty and disassociation (al-wala' wa al-bara'). And among them is one who considers testifying in favor of someone who agrees with him against his opponent. And the Sharia only requires acting upon the report of a single person when he is trustworthy and pleasing (respectable). But these are not the description of those who assert the text (nass), nor the description of the individuals who originally transmitted that to them, according to their own claim; because they claim that the transmitter of these reports for them was among those who affirmed the guardianship (wilaya) of Ali and who held their view concerning the Companions and practiced disassociation (bara'a). Therefore, neither the report of this predecessor is to be accepted from them, nor is the report of the successor to be accepted, due to what has been established of the faith of those whom they declared to be disbelievers and the trustworthiness of those whom they declared to be corrupt and disassociated from.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage concludes the refutation. Baqillani argues that the Shia narrators of the nass fail the condition of trustworthiness ('adala) because their doctrine requires loyalty and disassociation (al-wala' wa al-bara') which leads them to bias. They will testify only for their allies. The Sharia requires a narrator to be 'adl (just) and mardiyy (pleasing). But the Shia who report the nass are not such: they are partisans of Ali and hold extreme views against other Companions. Therefore, the entire transmission chain — from the first generation (salaf) to later ones (khalaf) — is corrupted, because they have declared the majority of Muslims to be disbelievers or corrupt. The fact that they call Muslims infidels and declare them corrupt shows that they themselves lack the necessary integrity. Thus their reports are rejected.

Key Terms
العدالة
الولاء والبراء
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What final reason does Baqillani give for rejecting the Shia transmission of the nass?
A) The transmitters were unknown.
B) The transmitters' doctrine of loyalty and disassociation shows they are biased and not trustworthy.
C) The reports contradict each other.
D) The nass was only transmitted by women.

Key Takeaway
A narrator's trustworthiness is compromised if their doctrine obliges them to be partial to allies, thus their reports are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate who to trust for religious knowledge, do you objectively examine their character, or do you favor those who agree with your group's views? How might your own loyalties blind you to the truth?
?
The Shia narrators are rejected because their doctrine makes them partial. Can you identify any biases in your own heart that might affect how you receive or reject religious reports? What would it cost you to correct that?
?
Baqillani argues that declaring a believer a disbeliever (takfir) is a sign of corrupt character. Have you ever been quick to judge or condemn someone based on their affiliation rather than their actual beliefs? How can you guard your tongue and heart from this?

Refuting the Nass Claim by Transmitter Unreliability
Author argues the transmitters of the nass are unreliable due to their heretical beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Baqillani argues that the Shia transmitters of the nass for Ali are unreliable due to their heretical beliefs that destroy their moral integrity (adala).

Core Idea
A report's reliability depends on the transmitter's justice; heretical beliefs corrupt that justice.

Imam Baqillani launches a two-pronged refutation of the Shia claim of an explicit textual designation for Ali. He first reiterates the conditions for accepting a solitary report, then argues that the very transmitters of the nass narratives disqualify themselves through their beliefs about the Companions. The passage also addresses a potential counterargument about the original transmitters.

Guided Path
1
Recap conditions for accepting khabar al-wahid
The author begins by restating the requirements: the report must be free of corruption or contradiction, and the transmitter must be just (adl). This sets the standard against which the Shia transmitters are measured.
These conditions are standard in Sunni hadith methodology.
2
Identify heretical beliefs that destroy adala
The author lists the beliefs of those who claim nass: they dissociate from Abu Bakr and Umar, curse the Companions, and accuse them of apostasy. Such beliefs, according to Baqillani, remove their moral integrity and trustworthiness.
The author refrains from mentioning all their doctrines to avoid appearing malicious.
3
Follow the objection about original transmitters
A hypothetical objection is raised: what if the original transmitters of the nass were not themselves believers in the nass? Baqillani answers that in that case they are corrupt (fussaq) from both perspectives: according to the Shia they abandoned the nass, and according to the Sunnis they transmitted a falsehood.
This objection-answer strengthens the argument by covering both possibilities.
4
Understand the conclusive argument
The author concludes that neither the earlier nor later transmitters can be accepted because their beliefs or actions show them to be unreliable. Thus the nass claim is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النص فيه عمل من الأعمال في الشريعة فصيروا إلى العمل به قيل له : قد قلنا فيما قَبْلُ إنا إنما نعمل بأخبار الآحاد إذا كانت على صفات مخصوصة وعَرِيَتْ مما يدل على فسادها أو معارضتها وثبتت عدالة نقلتها ونحن لا نعرف أحداً قال بالنص على عليّ عليه السلام إلا وهو يتبرأ من أبي بكر (۱) وعمر (۲) وسائر أهل الشورى (۳) سوى عليّ ويَشتِمُ الصحابة ويَزْرِي على أفعالهم ويزعم أنهم ارتدوا بعد الإسلام على أعقابهم ويضيف إلى ذلك مذاهب أُخر نرغب عن ذكرها لئلا يظن قارىء كتابنا أنا نقصد الشناعة عليهم دون الاحتجاج على فساد قولهم . وببعض هذه الأمور تسقط العدالة وتزول الثقة والأمانة لأن هذا الدين لا عندهم يتم إلا بالولاء والبراء، ومنهم من يرى الشهادة لمُوَافِقة على خَصْمِـه والشريعة إنما أوجبت العمل بخبر الواحد إذا كان عَدْلاً مَرْضِيّاً وليس هذه صفة القائلين بالنص ولا صِفَةَ الآحاد الذين رَوَوْا لهم ذلك في الأصل، على دعواهم؛ لأنهم يزعمون أن راوي هذه الأخبار لهم كان من القائلين بولاية علي وممن يرى في الصحابة رأيهم ويدين بالبراءة فلا منهم يجب أن يُقبَلَ خَبَرُ هذا السلف ولا يُقْبَلَ الخَلَفُ لما قد ثبت من إيمان من دانوا بإكفاره وعدالة من دانوا بتفسيقه والبراءة منه . فإن زعموا أن رواة النص في الأصل لم يكونوا ممن يدين بالنص على علي عليه السلام قيل لهم : فهم إذا فُسَّاقٌ عندنا وعندكم؛ فـوجـه فسقهم عندكم تدينهم بترك ما علموه ورَوَوْه من النص وتوليتهم الظلمة والفساق؛ ووجه تفسيقهم عندنا رِوَايَتُهم لما لا أصل له عندهم ولما قد علموا بطلانه وترك العمل به وأوْهَمُوا الناس بطلانه فلا مُعْتَبَرَ بخبر من هذه صفته عندنا وعندكم فهذا هكذا
The text (nass) regarding Ali... we have already said that we only act upon khabar al-ahad when they have specific qualities and are free from indications of corruption or contradiction, and the reliability of their transmitters is established. And we do not know anyone who claimed the nass for Ali, peace be upon him, except that he disassociates from Abu Bakr and Umar and the rest of the people of the Shura except Ali, and curses the Companions, and disparages their actions, and claims that they apostatized after Islam on their heels, and adds to that other doctrines which we refrain from mentioning lest the reader of our book think that we intend to vilify them rather than to argue against the corruption of their statement. And by some of these matters, reliability (adala) falls away and trust and honesty disappear, because this religion, according to them, is only complete through allegiance (al-wala') and dissociation (al-bara'). And some of them consider testimony to be for agreement against an opponent. And the Shari'a only necessitates acting upon khabar al-wahid when the transmitter is just (adl) and approved (mardiyy). And these are not the description of those who claim the nass, nor the description of the individuals who transmitted that to them originally, upon their claim; because they claim that the transmitter of these reports to them was among those who affirmed the wilayah of Ali and among those who hold their opinion regarding the Companions and adhere to dissociation (bara'). So neither is the report of this predecessor to be accepted, nor is the successor to be accepted, because it is established the faith of those whom they declare unbelievers and the reliability of those whom they declare corrupt and disassociate from. If they claim that the transmitters of the nass originally were not among those who adhere to the nass for Ali, peace be upon him, it is said to them: Then they are corrupt (fussaq) according to us and according to you; the aspect of their corruption according to you is their adherence to abandoning what they knew and transmitted of the nass and their appointment of oppressors and corrupt people; and the aspect of their corruption according to us is their transmission of what has no basis for them and what they knew to be false, and their abandonment of acting upon it, and they gave people the impression of its falsehood. So no consideration is given to a report of one with this description according to us and according to you. So this is so.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shia claim that there was an explicit textual designation (nass) for Ali's succession. The author begins by reiterating the conditions for accepting khabar al-ahad: the transmitter must be just and the report free from corruption or contradiction. Then he points out that the only people who transmitted reports of such a nass are those who disassociate from Abu Bakr, Umar, and the rest of the Shura members (except Ali), curse the Companions, accuse them of apostasy, and hold other objectionable doctrines. Such beliefs destroy their adala (moral integrity) because, according to the author, the religion is built on allegiance to the righteous and dissociation from the wicked. Additionally, some of these narrators view testimony as a tool against opponents, which further undermines their trustworthiness. The Shari'a requires that a transmitter of a solitary report be just (adl) and approved (mardiyy), and these narrators do not meet that description. The author then addresses a potential objection: what if the original transmitters were not themselves believers in the nass? He answers that in that case they would be corrupt (fasiq) according to both parties: according to the Shia because they abandoned the nass they knew, and according to the Sunnis because they transmitted something baseless. Thus, in either scenario, their reports are unacceptable.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid)
العدالة (adala)
النص (nass)
الولاء والبراء (al-wala' wa al-bara')
تفسيق (tafsiq)
فساق (fussaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Shia reports of nass are transmitted by reliable narrators.
Correction: The author shows that the very beliefs of those narrators (disassociation from Abu Bakr and Umar, cursing Companions) disqualify them as trustworthy because they destroy their adala.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are the transmitters of the nass for Ali not considered trustworthy?
A) Because they were not Muslims.
B) Because they hold beliefs (disassociating from Abu Bakr and Umar, cursing Companions) that destroy their adala.
C) Because they were few in number.
D) Because they were not Companions.

Key Takeaway
The reliability of a report depends on the moral integrity of its transmitter; heretical beliefs such as cursing Companions destroy that integrity and render the report unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive religious teachings, do you examine the character and beliefs of the one conveying them with the same rigor the author applies here? Or do you accept them blindly? This lesson demands you ask: Who taught me this, and are they trustworthy according to the standard of 'adala?
?
The author states that certain beliefs—like cursing the Companions—destroy a person's reliability entirely. Are there beliefs you hold that might similarly undermine your moral integrity? How would you react if someone judged your trustworthiness based on a single doctrine you affirm?
?
If you discovered that a teacher you respect held secret beliefs that contradicted the core of Islam (like denying the justice of the Companions), would you still accept their teachings? This passage forces you to decide whether content or carrier matters more—and that decision has eternal consequences.

Consensus of Early Muslims Refutes Nass
Author argues that the nass report is invalid due to unanimous consensus against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus (ijma') of the early Muslim community proves the invalidity of the nass claim for Ali.

Core Idea
Early consensus invalidates the nass claim.

This passage presents a powerful second argument against the nass claim: even if the reports were technically reliable, they are rendered invalid by the unanimous consensus of the early Muslim community. Imam al-Baqillani points out that the entire ummah, including Ali himself and all those for whom nass was allegedly made, submitted to the leadership of Abu Bakr and Umar. This established historical fact cannot be denied, and it directly contradicts the claim of a nass for Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's structure
Read the passage carefully. Notice that the author presents a new reason against the nass: the consensus of the early Muslims. Look for how he supports this with examples.
The passage begins with 'And because...' indicating this is a continuation of a previous argument.
2
Examine the evidence of consensus
The author lists key Companions who submitted to Abu Bakr and Umar, including Ali, al-Abbas, and others. Consider why listing these names is significant.
These are individuals for whom the nass was allegedly made.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Although the passage cuts off, the implied conclusion is that the nass claim cannot stand because it contradicts the known consensus. Reflect on how unanimity invalidates a report.
The argument is that if a nass existed, the early Muslims would not have ignored it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن هذه الأخبار التي هي أخبار الآحاد التي تدعونها في النص على عَليّ أخبار قد عارضها إجماع المسلمين في الصدر الأول على إبطالها وترك العمل بها لأن الأمة كلها انقادت لأبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما، ودانت بوجوب طاعتهما والسكون تحت رايتهما، وفيهم علي والعباس وعمار والمقداد وأبو ذر والزبير بن العوام وكل من ادعي له النص وروي له وهذا الظاهر المعلوم من حال الصحابة رضي الله عنهم لا يمكننا ولا أحداً من
And because these reports, which are khabar al-ahad that you claim regarding the nass for Ali, are reports that have been contradicted by the consensus (ijma') of the Muslims in the first era upon their invalidity and abandoning acting upon them, because the entire nation submitted to Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, and affirmed the obligation of obeying them and being at rest under their leadership, and among them were Ali, al-Abbas, Ammar, al-Miqdad, Abu Dharr, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam, and everyone for whom the nass was claimed and transmitted. And this apparent and known state of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, cannot be [denied] by us or anyone.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents a second, independent argument against the claim of nass. The author argues that even if the reports were transmitted by reliable narrators, they are contradicted by the consensus (ijma') of the early Muslim community. The entire ummah—including Ali, al-Abbas, Ammar, al-Miqdad, Abu Dharr, al-Zubayr, and all those for whom the nass was allegedly made—submitted to the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar and obeyed them. This outward and well-known behavior of the Companions cannot be denied. Therefore, the claim that there was a nass for Ali is invalid because it conflicts with the unanimous consensus of the early Muslims. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear: the consensus disproves the nass.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma')
النص (nass)
الصحابة (al-sahaba)
أخبار الآحاد (khabar al-ahad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might claim that the early Muslims were coerced or that the consensus was not unanimous, so the nass might still be valid.
Correction: The author emphasizes that even those for whom the nass was claimed—like Ali and al-Abbas—were part of this consensus, willingly submitting to Abu Bakr and Umar. This external, well-known fact cannot be dismissed.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument the author uses to refute the claim of nass?
A) The transmitters were not Companions.
B) The reports are contradicted by the consensus of the early Muslims who accepted Abu Bakr and Umar.
C) The nass was not mentioned in the Quran.
D) The nass was known only to a few.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the early Muslims, including those for whom the nass was claimed, proves that the claim of a nass for Ali is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the entire early community—including Ali and his supporters—submitted to Abu Bakr and Umar, do you feel a tension with any narratives you may have inherited? Are you willing to let go of claims that contradict the actual, visible behavior of those companions?
?
The author calls this consensus 'apparent and known' and says it 'cannot be denied.' Is there any area of your belief where you are denying a clear historical fact because it challenges a cherished view? What would it cost you to submit to that truth?
?
If you were among the companions, would you have been one of those who submitted to Abu Bakr and Umar, or would you have resisted based on reports of nass? How does that question reveal your own attachment to authority versus evidence?

Refuting the Charge of Fisq Against Rejecters of Nass
Baqillani refutes the Shia charge of fisq by turning it back on them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani turns the Shia accusation of fisq back against them by applying the same criteria they use against others.

Core Idea
The Shia accusation of fisq is self-defeating when measured by their own principles.

Baqillani presents a dialectical refutation: he shows that if the Shia criteria for fisq are applied consistently, the Shia themselves are guilty. This passage illustrates the classical Sunni method of refuting sectarian claims by turning the argument back.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the pronouns: 'them' refers to the Shia, 'they' refers to the Shia themselves.
2
Identify the two aspects of fisq
The passage gives two reasons for considering the Shia transgressors: one from the Shia's own perspective and one from the Sunni perspective. Find each and note how they are structured.
The first aspect uses the Shia's own criterion (abandoning the nass), the second uses Sunni criterion (narration of false reports).
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that reports from such narrators are unreliable. Consider why this conclusion follows from the two aspects.
This is a tu quoque argument: if the accusers are themselves guilty, their accusation loses force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علي عليه السلام قيل لهم : فهم إذا فُسَّاقٌ عندنا وعندكم؛ فـوجـه فسقهم عندكم تدينهم بترك ما علموه ورَوَوْه من النص وتوليتهم الظلمة والفساق؛ ووجه تفسيقهم عندنا رِوَايَتُهم لما لا أصل له عندهم ولما قد علموا بطلانه وترك العمل به وأوْهَمُوا الناس بطلانه فلا مُعْتَبَرَ بخبر من هذه صفته عندنا وعندكم فهذا هكذا
Concerning Ali, peace be upon him, it was said to them: Then they are transgressors (fussāq) according to us and according to you. So the aspect of their transgression according to you is their adherence (tadayyun) to abandoning what they knew and narrated of the explicit designation (nass), and their appointing of oppressors and transgressors. And the aspect of our considering them transgressors is their narration of what has no basis with them, and of what they knew to be false, and their abandonment of acting upon it, and their making people believe it is false. So no consideration is given to a report (khabar) of this description according to us and according to you. This is how it is.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation. The author reports a Shia argument that those who reject the explicit designation (nass) of Ali are transgressors (fussāq). The author then turns the accusation back: according to the Shia's own criteria, the Shia themselves are transgressors because they abandoned the nass they claim to know and appointed oppressive rulers. Moreover, from the Sunni perspective, the Shia are transgressors because they narrate baseless reports while knowing they are false, and then act contrary to them. Therefore, such reports are unreliable. The passage establishes a tu quoque argument before proceeding to further refutations.

Key Terms
فُسَّاق (fussāq)
النص (nass)
خبر (khabar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Shia charge of fisq against rejecters of nass is one-sided and only applies to Sunnis.
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that the same charge applies to the Shia themselves based on their own actions, making the accusation self-defeating.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the Shia's own basis for considering themselves (or others) transgressors (fussāq)?
A) Their rejection of the nass for Ali
B) Their acceptance of Abu Bakr and Umar
C) Their narration of false reports
D) Their practice of taqiyya

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the Shia charge of fisq boomerangs back onto them, rendering their accusation and their reports invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accused someone of a fault that you yourself commit? How does the tu quoque argument in this passage from Baqillani challenge you to examine your own accusations before making them? What step can you take today to drop a judgment you hold against another that might boomerang back on you?
?
The Shia charged others with fisq, but the proof shows they were guilty themselves. Is there any standard you apply to others but not to yourself? What would it cost you to apply that same standard to your own actions starting today?
?
Baqillani shows that those who narrate falsehoods while knowing them to be false are transgressors. Are there any 'narrations' or beliefs you hold about others that you know are not fully true? What would it take for you to abandon them and seek the truth, even if it means admitting you were wrong?

Refutation of Nass Reports by Ijmāʿ and Taqiyya
Bāqillānī rejects nass reports due to contrary ijmāʿ and taqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bāqillānī's two-part refutation of the Shia claim of nass for ʿAlī using ijmāʿ and taqiyya.

Core Idea
Ijmāʿ and taqiyya together invalidate Shia nass reports.

Bāqillānī presents a crisp argument against the Shia claim of explicit designation (nass) for ʿAlī. He points to two devastating problems: the consensus of the early community contradicts the reports, and the Shia themselves admit to taqiyya, making their narrations unreliable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the two main parts.
The passage is a single paragraph with two distinct refutation points.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons: first, the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the companions opposes the reports; second, the Shia practice of taqiyya makes their narrations unreliable.
3
Examine the ijmāʿ argument
Notice that the author includes ʿAlī and other figures for whom nass is claimed among those who accepted Abu Bakr and ʿUmar. This consensus invalidates the reports.
This is a key point: even those allegedly designated submitted to the caliphs.
4
Understand the taqiyya argument
The Shia themselves say 'taqiyya is our religion', so their narrators may conceal or misrepresent. This makes solitary reports from them unreliable.
Thus, even if the reports existed, they are vitiated by the narrators' practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن هذه الأخبار التي هي أخبار الآحاد التي تدعونها في النص على عَليّ أخبار قد عارضها إجماع المسلمين في الصدر الأول على إبطالها وترك العمل بها لأن الأمة كلها انقادت لأبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما، ودانت بوجوب طاعتهما والسكون تحت رايتهما، وفيهم علي والعباس وعمار والمقداد وأبو ذر والزبير بن العوام وكل من ادعي له النص وروي له وهذا الظاهر المعلوم من حال الصحابة رضي الله عنهم لا يمكننا ولا أحداً من الشيعة دفعه؛ ولهذا قالوا إن التقية ديننا؛ ورواه عن ولد عليّ أنهم قالوا : إن التقية ديننا ودين آبائنا؛ فلا يجوز أن يعمل بخبر الواحد ونحن نتیقن ترك رواته لـه وإظهاره فوجب ترك العمل على هذه الأخبار لتدينهم بخلافه وترك من روى النص عليه وتكذيبه به لأن الإجماع الظاهر في المعلوم يترك له ما هو أقوى من هذه الأخبار فوجب أيضاً ترك العمل على هذه الأخبار ولولا كانت مروية
And because these reports (akhbār) which are solitary reports (akhbār al-āḥād) that you claim regarding the designation (nass) of Ali are reports that have been opposed by the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Muslims in the first generation, which invalidates them and abandons acting upon them. For the entire community submitted to Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, and adhered to the obligation of obeying them and being still under their banner, and among them were Ali, al-Abbas, Ammar, al-Miqdad, Abu Dharr, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam, and everyone for whom the nass was claimed and narrated. This is the apparent, known state of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, which we cannot, nor can anyone from the Shia, repudiate. And for this reason they said: 'Taqiyya is our religion.' And it was narrated from the children of Ali that they said: 'Taqiyya is our religion and the religion of our fathers.' So it is not permissible to act upon a solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid) while we are certain that its narrators abandoned it and concealed it. Therefore, it is obligatory to abandon acting upon these reports because of their adherence to the contrary, and because those who narrated the nass for him abandoned and belied it. For the apparent, known consensus overrides what is stronger than these reports, so it is also obligatory to abandon acting upon these reports, even if they were transmitted.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a two-part refutation. First, the author argues that the solitary reports (akhbār al-āḥād) claiming nass for Ali are contradicted by the manifest consensus (ijmāʿ) of the early Muslim community, including Ali himself and all others for whom nass is claimed, since they all accepted the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar. This consensus invalidates the reports. Second, the author notes that the Shia themselves admit that taqiyya (dissimulation) is their religion, and even the children of Ali said so. Therefore, their narrators are known to conceal and abandon what they narrate, making their solitary reports unreliable. Hence, these reports must be rejected, especially when contradicted by such a strong proof as ijmāʿ.

Key Terms
أخبار الآحاد (akhbār al-āḥād)
الإجماع (ijmāʿ)
التقية (taqiyya)
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Solitary reports from Shia sources are automatically reliable.
Correction: The author shows that these reports are vitiated by two factors: (1) they contradict the consensus of the early Muslim community, and (2) the Shia practice of taqiyya means their narrators may conceal or misrepresent, making their solitary reports unreliable.

Checkpoint
What two reasons does the author give for rejecting the Shia nass reports?
A) The reports are ḍaʿīf and munkar
B) Contradiction by ijmāʿ and Shia taqiyya
C) The reporters are all mughālūn and the chain is broken
D) The reports are from non-Muslims and impossible

Key Takeaway
The Shia nass reports are invalid because they contradict the known consensus of the Companions and because the Shia themselves practice taqiyya, which undermines their reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you think of a belief you hold that is contradicted by the clear, known practice of those you respect? What would it take for you to surrender that belief to the consensus of trustworthy people, as this lesson demands you do with the nass reports?
?
The Shia practiced taqiyya to protect themselves. In what subtle ways do you conceal or adapt your faith to fit in or avoid conflict? What would it cost you to live your deen openly and without pretense?
?
If Ali ibn Abi Talib himself submitted to the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar, what does that reveal about the purity of his heart? Are you willing to let go of partisan loyalties and follow the truth even when it means humbling yourself?

Conflicting Claims of Nass and the Proof of Ikhtiyar
Bāqillānī applies conflicting-reports principle to nass claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Bāqillānī uses the principle of conflicting solitary reports to refute the Shia claim of nass and establish the doctrine of ikhtiyār.

Core Idea
Conflicting nass reports cancel out, proving the caliphate was based on choice.

In this passage, Imam Bāqillānī applies a key principle of Islamic legal theory: when two equally strong solitary reports conflict, they cancel each other out, and we revert to the original ruling. He uses this to dismantle the Shia claim of a divine designation (nass) for Imam Ali. By showing that rival groups also claim nass for Abu Bakr and al-Abbas, he demonstrates that none of these reports can be acted upon, thus proving that the caliphate was established by choice (ikhtiyār).

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the first sentence: the author states that we only act on a solitary report if it is not opposed by a contrary report. This is the principle of non-contradiction for khabar al-wāḥid.
This principle is fundamental in both law and theology.
2
Spot the opposing reports
The Shia claim of nass is opposed by reports from the Bakriyya (nass for Abu Bakr) and the Rawandiyya (nass for al-Abbas). The author asserts these counter-reports are stronger.
These groups are historical sects that rival the Shia.
3
Consider the two outcomes
Either the stronger report abrogates the weaker, or if they are equivalent, both are abandoned, reverting to the original position of no nass.
This is a classic legal maxim: al-aṣl al-ʿadam.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the nass claims are mutually canceling, the doctrine of ikhtiyār (election) for the caliphate is proven beyond doubt.
Ikhtiyār opposes the Shia doctrine of nass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا إنما نعمل بخبر الواحد من الشريعة إذا لم يعارضه خبر بضد موجبه وهذا الخبر الذي ادعته الشيعة فقد عارضه خبر البكرية والراوندية وكل من قال بالنص على أبي بكر والنص على العباس، وروايتهم في ذلك أظهر وأثبت؛ والعمل في صدر الأمة موافق لرواية النص على أبي بكر عليه السلام فهو إذا أقوى وأثبت؛ فيجب إذا ترك الأضعف بالأقوى؛ فإن لم نفعل ذلك فلا أقل من اعتقاد تعارض هذه الأخبار وتكافئها وتعذر العمل بشيء منها ورجوعنا إلى ما كنا عليه من أن الأصل ألا نص إلى أن يثبت أمر نعود إليه وأنى لأحد بذلك وكل ما يستدل به على بطلان الخبر الواحد موجود في هذه الرواية وفي بعض هذه الجملة كفاية في إبطال النص وإذا بطل النص ثبت الاختيار ثبوتاً لا يمكن دفعه وإنكاره
However, we only act upon a solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid) from the Sacred Law when it is not opposed by a report contrary to its implication. And this report which the Shia claim has been opposed by the report of the Bakriyya and the Rawandiyya, and everyone who affirmed the nass for Abu Bakr and the nass for al-Abbas. Their narration in that regard is more apparent and more firmly established; and the practice in the first generation was in agreement with the narration of the nass for Abu Bakr, peace be upon him, so it is therefore stronger and more firmly established. So it is obligatory to abandon the weaker for the stronger. And if we do not do that, then at the very least we must hold that these reports are contradictory and equivalent, and that acting on any of them is impossible, and we revert to what we were upon: that the original position is that there is no nass until something is established which we might return to. And how could anyone achieve that? Everything that is used to demonstrate the invalidity of the solitary report is present in this narration. And in part of this statement there is sufficient to invalidate the nass. And if the nass is invalid, then choice (ikhtiyār) is established in an undeniable and irrefutable manner.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage applies the principle of preferring stronger evidence. Even if we accept the validity of solitary reports in law, the Shia's claim of nass for Ali is contradicted by other reports claiming nass for Abu Bakr (from the Bakriyya) and for al-Abbas (from the Rawandiyya). These counter-reports are actually more apparent and more firmly established, and the practice of the early community aligns with the nass for Abu Bakr. Therefore, the stronger evidence should prevail, and the Shia report is abandoned. If one argues that the reports are equally balanced, then they cancel each other out, and we revert to the original principle that there is no nass. This suffices to disprove the Shia claim. Consequently, the doctrine of ikhtiyār (election/choice) for the caliphate is proven beyond doubt.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد
البكرية
الراوندية
النص
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Shia nass is the only such claim and must be accepted if it is a solitary report.
Correction: The author shows there are rival claims of nass from the Bakriyya and Rawandiyya, and these counter-reports are actually stronger, so the Shia report cannot be acted upon.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what must be done if conflicting nass reports are regarded as equivalent?
A) Accept all of them as valid
B) Accept the one with the strongest chain
C) Revert to the original position that there is no nass
D) Accept the nass for Abu Bakr because it is stronger

Key Takeaway
Conflicting nass reports cancel each other out, proving that the caliphate was established by choice (ikhtiyār) and not by divine designation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the truth, but do you actually apply a consistent method when evaluating conflicting religious claims, or do you simply accept what agrees with your prior commitments? Would you be willing to abandon a cherished belief if stronger evidence contradicted it, as Bāqillānī demands?
?
The early community's practice tipped the scale in this argument. When you face a disagreement among Muslims, do you give weight to the lived tradition of the Salaf, or do you rely solely on texts? What claim in your own faith would collapse if you applied this same principle?
?
Bāqillānī concludes that conflicting reports about nass do not leave us in uncertainty but prove that choice is the divinely intended system. Are you comfortable living without absolute textual proof for every matter, trusting that the absence of evidence is itself evidence of a greater wisdom? Or do you secretly wish for more 'nass' in your life to remove ambiguity?

The Taqiyya Argument Against Shia Nass
Baqillani uses Shia taqiyya doctrine to invalidate nass reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the Shia practice of taqiyya makes their nass reports unreliable.

Core Idea
Taqiyya exposes the contradiction in Shia transmission of nass.

Baqillani begins his refutation by turning the Shia doctrine of taqiyya against them. He notes that the Shia themselves claim taqiyya as their religion, and that their imams' descendants affirmed this. Because the narrators of the nass reports were bound to conceal them, their own practice contradicts the content of the reports, making them untrustworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taqiyya
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the argument.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the Shia are reported to say taqiyya is their religion and that of their forefathers.
This is used as evidence against them.
3
Understand the logic
Baqillani argues that since the narrators practiced taqiyya (concealment) regarding the nass, they cannot be trusted to have transmitted it truthfully.
The argument relies on the contradiction between the report and the reported behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيعة دفعه؛ ولهذا قالوا إن التقية ديننا؛ ورواه عن ولد عليّ أنهم قالوا: إن التقية ديننا ودين آبائنا؛ فلا يجوز أن يعمل بخبر الواحد ونحن نتيقن ترك رواته له وإظهاره فوجب ترك العمل على هذه الأخبار لتدينهم بخلافه وترك من روى النص عليه وتكذيبه به لأن الإجماع الظاهر في المعلوم يترك له ما هو أقوى من هذه الأخبار فوجب أيضاً ترك العمل على هذه الأخبار ولولا كانت مروية. على أنا إنما نعمل بخبر الواحد من الشريعة إذا لم يعارضه خبر بضد موجبه وهذا الخبر الذي ادعته الشيعة فقد عارضه خبر البكرية والراوندية وكل من قال بالنص على أبي بكر والنص على العباس، وروايتهم في ذلك أظهر وأثبت؛ والعمل في صدر الأمة موافق لرواية النص على أبي بكر عليه السلام فهو إذا أقوى وأثبت؛ فيجب إذا ترك الأضعف بالأقوى؛ فإن لم نفعل ذلك فلا أقل من اعتقاد تعارض هذه الأخبار وتكافئها وتعذر العمل بشيء منها ورجوعنا إلى ما كنا عليه من أن الأصل ألا نص إلى أن يثبت أمر نعود إليه وأنى لأحد بذلك وكل ما يستدل به على بطلان الخبر الواحد موجود في هذه الرواية وفي بعض هذه الجملة كفاية في إبطال النص وإذا بطل النص ثبت الاختيار ثبوتاً لا يمكن دفعه وإنكاره.
The Shia defended it; therefore they said: 'Taqiyya is our religion.' It was narrated from the descendants of Ali that they said: 'Taqiyya is our religion and the religion of our forefathers.' So it is not permissible to act upon a solitary report while we are certain that its narrators abandoned it and did not manifest it. Consequently, acting upon these reports is obligatory to abandon because of their adherence to its contrary, and [it is necessary] to abandon the one who narrated the nass upon him and to declare him a liar because the manifest consensus known to all overrides what is stronger than these reports; thus acting upon these reports must also be abandoned, even if they were narrated. Moreover, we only act upon a solitary report from the Sharia when it is not opposed by a report contradicting its purport. This report that the Shia claim has been opposed by the report of the Bakriyya and the Rawandiyya, and everyone who asserted the nass upon Abu Bakr and the nass upon al-Abbas. Their narration in that regard is more apparent and more established; the practice of the early community accords with the narration of the nass upon Abu Bakr, peace be upon him, so it is stronger and more established. Therefore the weaker must be abandoned for the stronger. If we do not do that, then at the very least we must hold that these reports are conflicting and equivalent, and that acting upon any of them is impossible, and we return to what we were upon: that the original principle is no nass until a matter is established to which we revert. How could anyone do that? And every argument used to invalidate a solitary report exists in this narration; and in some of this discourse there is sufficient refutation of the nass. And if the nass is invalidated, the choice (ikhtiyar) is established with an establishment that cannot be repelled or denied.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a general refutation of the Shia claim of explicit designation (nass) of Ali as caliph. Baqillani begins by citing the Shia doctrine of taqiyya (dissimulation), which they consider their religion. Because Shia narrators themselves allegedly practiced dissimulation regarding nass reports, their transmission of such reports cannot be trusted; they themselves abandoned publicizing the nass, contradicting its content. Therefore those reports should be set aside. He then argues that even if the reports were narrated, a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) is only accepted when not opposed by a contrary report. Here, the Shia report of nass for Ali is opposed by the Bakriyya and Rawandiyya reports of nass for Abu Bakr and al-Abbas respectively. Those contrary reports are stronger because the early community (ṣadr al-umma) acted upon Abu Bakr’s caliphate, making them more established. Since the weaker report must yield to the stronger, the Shia claim fails. If one insists the reports cancel each other out, then we fall back to the original principle: no nass exists until proven. Thus the method of election (ikhtiyar) is firmly established.

Key Terms
تقية
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqiyya is only a defensive practice and does not affect the truth of transmitted reports.
Correction: Baqillani argues that if narrators were bound to conceal the nass, their transmission of it is inherently contradictory, so the reports cannot be trusted.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the Shia practice of taqiyya invalidate their nass reports?
A) Taqiyya is forbidden in Islam
B) The narrators were required to conceal the nass, so they cannot be trusted to have transmitted it truthfully
C) The reports were not found in reliable books
D) The Shia only used taqiyya in times of persecution

Key Takeaway
The Shia doctrine of taqiyya contradicts their own transmission of nass reports, making those reports unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani exposes a contradiction in the Shia claim: they taught taqiyya while transmitting reports they concealed. Is there any area of your life where you claim one thing but practice another—where your actions contradict your words? What would it cost you to align them, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
This lesson shows that the Shia narrators could not be trusted because they hid the truth they conveyed. Think of a time you hid a truth you were responsible for teaching or sharing. How did that concealment affect the trust others placed in you? What step can you take this week to rebuild integrity in that area?
?
Baqillani argues that manifest consensus overrides even strong reports. Consider a belief you hold that goes against the manifest consensus of the Muslim community (as you understand it). Could you be practicing a form of taqiyya by holding it privately? Are you willing to submit to the community's consensus if it contradicts your personal view?

Conflicting Reports and the Proof of Election
Baqillani presents conflicting nass reports and concludes for election.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses conflicting nass claims and the early community's practice to establish the legitimacy of election (ikhtiyar).

Core Idea
Conflicting nass reports cancel out, returning us to the principle of election.

Baqillani strengthens his refutation by showing that even if the Shia nass reports were somehow reliable, they are opposed by other reports claiming nass for Abu Bakr and al-Abbas. The early community's practice supports the latter, making them stronger. Since the reports conflict and cannot all be true, the only consistent position is to reject all nass claims and revert to the original principle that the caliphate was established by election (ikhtiyar).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conflicting reports
Read the Arabic and translation to see Baqillani's argument about counter-claims.
2
Identify the opposing reports
Note the mention of Bakriyya (asserting nass for Abu Bakr) and Rawandiyya (asserting nass for al-Abbas).
These are mentioned as counter-claims.
3
Analyze the strength
Baqillani says the Bakriyya report is stronger because the early community (ṣadr al-umma) acted upon Abu Bakr's caliphate.
This is an appeal to practice and consensus.
4
Understand the conclusion
If reports cancel out, we fall back to the original principle: no nass is proven, so ikhtiyar (election) is established.
This is the final conclusion.

Source Text

?

Linguistic Refutation of the Hadith 'Man Kuntu Mawlahu'
Baqillani refutes the Shia interpretation of the mawla hadith by distinguishing 'mawla' from 'awla'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's linguistic argument that the word 'mawla' in the hadith does not imply the same authority as 'awla'.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' does not mean 'awla'; thus the hadith does not establish succession.

In this passage, Baqillani tackles a central Shia argument for the caliphate of Ali. He demonstrates that the linguistic ambiguity of 'mawla' prevents the hadith from being an explicit designation. This refutation relies on a careful analysis of Arabic semantics and the principle that words do not carry implied meanings without proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia objection and initial response
Start by reading the Shia claim that the Prophet's words 'Am I not awla to you?' followed by 'Whoever I am his mawla' imply Ali has the same authority. Then read Baqillani's initial rebuttal that 'mawla' and 'awla' have different meanings.
Notice that Baqillani immediately points out the linguistic distinction.
2
Examine the analogy with 'prophet'
Baqillani provides an analogy: if the Prophet had said 'Am I not your prophet?' then 'Ali is my mawla', no one would claim prophethood for Ali. This shows that meanings do not transfer between different words.
The analogy illustrates a fundamental logical principle: words do not inherit meanings from adjacent speech.
3
Understand the linguistic principle
Baqillani concludes by stating that the Shia must prove that 'mawla' can mean 'awla' through authoritative transmission from Arabs. Without such proof, their interpretation is unfounded.
This principle is called 'tawqif' in Islamic sciences: linguistic claims require verification from native speakers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال من الشيعة المنكرين صريح النص على علي عليه السلام: ما أنكرتم أن يكون النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد نص على عليّ رضي الله عنه، بقوله: «من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه»؟ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قرّرهم على وجوب طاعته وأنه أولى بهم من أنفسهم ثم قال بعد قوله لهم: «ألست أولى بالمؤمنين من أنفُسِهم؟»، فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه»؛ فأوجب لعليّ من وجوب الطاعة والانقياد له وأنه أولى بهم ما أوجبه لنفسه. يقال لهم: يجب ما قلتم؛ لأن ما أثبته لنفسه من كونه أولى بهم ليس هو من معنى أوجبه لعليّ بسبيل؛ لأنه قال: «من كنت مولاه فعليّ مولاه»، فأوجب الموالاة لنفسه ولعليّ وأوجب لنفسه كونه أولى بهم منهم بأنفسهم وليس معنى أولى من معنى مولى في شيء؛ لأن قوله «مولى» يحتمل في اللغة وجوهاً ليس فيها معنى أولى فلا يجب إذا عقب كلام بكلام ليس من معناه أن يكون معناهما واحداً. ألا ترون أنه لو قال: ألست نبيكم والمُخبر لكم بالوحي عن ربكم وناسخ شرائع من كان قبلكم؟ ثم قال: فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه، لم يكن ذلك أن يكون قد أثبت لعلي من النبوة وتلقى الوحي ونسخ الشرائع على لسانه ما أوجبه في أول الكلام لنفسه ولا أمر باعتقاد ذلك فيه من حيث ثبت أنه ليس معنى نبي معنى مولى فكذلك إذا ثبت أنه ليس معنى أولى معنى مولى لم يجب أن يكون قد أثبت لعليّ ما أثبته لنفسه وإنما دخلت عليهم الشبهة من حيث ظنوا أن معنى مولى معنى أولى وأحق وليس الأمر كذلك. فإن قالوا: ولم أنكرتم أن يكون معنى مولى معنى أولى؟ قيل لهم: لأن هذا الذي تدعونه لغة ولا يجوز ثبوته إلا بتوقيف من أهلها عليه بنقل يوجب العلم مثله وينقطع العذر به ومتى لم نجد ذلك في اللغة كما ادعيتم بطل ما قلتموه كما أنه إذا لم يكن معنى مولى معنى نبي لم يجب إثبات النوبة العلي بمثل هذا الكلام.
If the Shia who deny the explicit nass for Ali, peace be upon him, say: 'Why do you deny that the Prophet, peace be upon him, explicitly designated Ali, may God be pleased with him, by his saying, “Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla”? Because the Prophet, peace be upon him, established for them the obligation of obedience to him and that he is more entitled (awla) to them than themselves, then he said after his statement to them, “Am I not more entitled (awla) to the believers than themselves?” [then] “Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla”; so he made obligatory for Ali the obligation of obedience and submission to him and that he is more entitled (awla) to them just as he made obligatory for himself.' It is said to them: Your statement is not necessary; because what He established for Himself of being more entitled (awla) to them is not of the same meaning as what He made obligatory for Ali in any way; for He said, “Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla,” so He made mawalat obligatory for Himself and for Ali, and He made obligatory for Himself that He is more entitled (awla) to them than themselves. And the meaning of 'awla' and the meaning of 'mawla' are not the same in any respect; because the word 'mawla' has in language multiple meanings, among which there is no meaning of 'awla'. So it is not necessary, when speech is followed by speech that is not of its meaning, that both meanings be identical. Do you not see that if He said, 'Am I not your prophet, the conveyer to you of revelation from your Lord, and the abrogator of the laws of those before you?' Then He said, 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla,' that would not be to have established for Ali prophethood, receiving revelation, and abrogating laws by his tongue as He made obligatory in the first part of the speech for Himself, nor would it command belief in that concerning him, because it is established that the meaning of 'prophet' is not the meaning of 'mawla'. Similarly, if it is established that the meaning of 'awla' is not the meaning of 'mawla', it is not necessary that what He established for Ali be what He established for Himself. The confusion only entered upon them because they thought that the meaning of 'mawla' is 'awla' and 'ahaqq' (more worthy), but it is not so. If they say: 'Why do you deny that the meaning of 'mawla' is 'awla'?' It is said to them: Because this that you claim is [a matter of] language, and it cannot be established except by a determination (tawqif) from its people through a transmission that produces knowledge like it and by which excuse is cut off. And if we do not find that in the language as you claim, your statement is invalidated, just as if the meaning of 'mawla' were not the meaning of 'prophet', the establishment of the supreme prophethood by such speech would not be necessary.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer refuting the Shia interpretation of the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Ali mawlahu'. The Shia object: the Prophet first reminded his companions that he is 'awla' (more entitled) to them than themselves, then said the mawla hadith, so he must be giving Ali the same authority. Baqillani responds by distinguishing the meanings: the Prophet established his own 'awla' status directly, but when he used 'mawla' for Ali, he was not using it in the sense of 'awla'. 'Mawla' has multiple linguistic meanings, none of which necessarily mean 'awla'. He gives an analogy: if the Prophet had said 'Am I not your prophet?' then 'Ali is my mawla', no one would claim Ali became a prophet. Similarly, using 'awla' first and then 'mawla' does not transfer the meaning. The Shia are mistaken because they assumed 'mawla' equals 'awla'. Then a follow-up question: why deny that 'mawla' can mean 'awla' in Arabic? Baqillani answers that this linguistic claim requires authoritative transmission (tawqif) from the Arabs, which the Shia have not provided; without such proof, their interpretation fails. The argument uses a logical parallel: just as 'mawla' does not mean 'prophet', it does not mean 'awla' in this context.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' means that Ali is the rightful successor (awla) because the Prophet first said he is awla.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'mawla' does not mean 'awla' in this context; the hadith only commands love and support (muwalat), not succession.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the Prophet's statement 'Man kuntu mawlahu' not establish the same authority for Ali as the Prophet's own 'awla'?
A) Because the Prophet never claimed to be 'awla'
B) Because 'mawla' has multiple meanings and does not necessarily mean 'awla'
C) Because the hadith is weak in transmission
D) Because the companions rejected the hadith

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's refutation hinges on the linguistic fact that 'mawla' does not mean 'awla'; thus the hadith does not prove succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that two different words in a religious text must mean the same thing because they appear close together? Where in your own understanding might you be guilty of this error, and how can you train yourself to check linguistic precision instead of relying on assumptions?
?
When you hear a hadith like 'Man kuntu mawlahu', does your heart immediately lean toward a certain interpretation because of what you've been taught? Would you be willing to set aside that leaning and examine the Arabic as if for the first time—even if it challenges a beloved view?
?
The Shia claim required linguistic proof they could not produce. Are there beliefs you hold that rest on an unverified 'proof' from tradition rather than solid textual evidence? What would it cost you to test them honestly against the source?

Refuting the claim that 'mawla' means 'awla' in the hadith
Refutation of Shi'a interpretation of 'mawla' as 'awla'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' does not establish Ali's authority equal to the Prophet's, according to Baqillani's linguistic argument.

Core Idea
Mawla does not mean awla; linguistic distinction prevents doctrinal conflation.

The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' is often cited to argue for Ali's authority. Baqillani, however, closely examines the wording of the Prophet and shows that the key term 'mawla' has a different range of meanings than 'awla' (more entitled). In this passage, he explains why the Shi'a interpretation is linguistically unsound, using an analogy to further clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a very logical and structured argument.
2
Identify the key terms and context
Identify the terms 'mawla' and 'awla' and notice how the Prophet first establishes his own status as 'awla' before saying 'Man kuntu mawlahu'. This sets up the issue.
3
Analyze the linguistic distinction
Baqillani argues that 'mawla' has multiple meanings, none of which is 'awla'. Understand why this distinction is crucial for the argument.
He uses an analogy with 'prophet' to show that if two words are not synonymous, the second cannot carry the implication of the first.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the hadith does not give Ali the same authority as the Prophet because the term used for Ali ('mawla') does not mean 'awla'.
The confusion arises from assuming synonymy where none exists.
