بقوله: «من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه»؟ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قرّرهم على وجوب طاعته وأنه أولى بهم من أنفسهم ثم قال بعد قوله لهم: «ألست أولى بالمؤمنين من أنفُسِهم؟»، فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه؛ فأوجب لعليّ من وجوب الطاعة والانقياد له وأنه أولى بهم ما أوجبه لنفسه، يقال لهم: يجب ما قلتم؛ لأن ما أثبته لنفسه من كونه أولى بهم ليس هو من معنى أوجبه لعليّ بسبيل؛ لأنه قال: من كنت مولاه فعليّ مولاه، فأوجب الموالاة لنفسه ولعليّ وأوجب لنفسه كونه أولى بهم منهم بأنفسهم وليس معنى أولى من معنى مولى في شيء؛ لأن قوله «مولى» يحتمل في اللغة وجوهاً ليس فيها معنى أولى فلا يجب إذا عُقب كلام بكلام ليس من معناه أن يكون معناهما واحداً. ألا ترون أنه لو قال: ألست نبيكم والمُخبر لكم بالوحي عن ربكم وناسخ شرائع من كان قبلكم؟ ثم قال: فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه، لم ذلك أن يكون قد أثبت لعلي من النبوة وتلقى الوحي ونسخ الشرائع على لسانه ما أوجبه في أول الكلام لنفسه ولا أمر باعتقاد ذلك فيه من حيث ثبت أنه ليس معنى نبي معنى مولى فكذلك إذا ثبت أنه ليس معنى أولى معنى مولى لم يجب أن يكون قد أثبت لعليّ ما أثبته لنفسه وإنما دخلت عليهم الشبهة من حيث ظنوا أن معنى مولى معنى أولى وأحق وليس الأمر كذلك.
By his saying: 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla'? Because the Prophet, peace be upon him, established for them the obligation of obedience to him and that he is more entitled (awla) to them than themselves, then he said after his statement to them: 'Am I not more entitled to the believers than themselves?', then 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla'; so he made obligatory for Ali the obligation of obedience and submission and that he is more entitled to them as he made obligatory for himself. It is said to them: What you claim must be [considered]; because what he established for himself of being more entitled to them is not of the meaning he made obligatory for Ali in any way; because he said: 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla', so he made obligatory the relationship of mawla (al-muwalah) for himself and for Ali, and he made obligatory for himself being more entitled to them than themselves, and the meaning of 'awla' is not related to the meaning of 'mawla' in anything; because his saying 'mawla' in language permits multiple aspects in which there is not the meaning of 'awla', so it is not necessary, if speech is followed by speech not of its meaning, that their meanings be one. Do you not see that if he said: 'Am I not your prophet and the one who informs you of revelation from your Lord and the abrogator of the laws of those before you?' then he said: 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla', that would not necessitate that he established for Ali prophethood, receiving revelation, and abrogating laws by his tongue, what he made obligatory in the first part of the speech for himself, nor did he command belief in that regarding him, since it is established that the meaning of 'prophet' is not the meaning of 'mawla'. So likewise, if it is established that the meaning of 'awla' is not the meaning of 'mawla', it is not necessary that he established for Ali what he established for himself. Rather, the confusion entered them because they thought that the meaning of 'mawla' is the meaning of 'awla' and more deserving, but the matter is not so.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Baqillani responds to the Shi'a claim that the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' establishes that 'Ali has the same authority over the believers as the Prophet himself. First, Baqillani reconstructs the context: the Prophet had already established his own status as being 'awla' (more entitled) over the believers than themselves. Then he said 'Man kuntu mawlahu...'. The Shi'a argue that this makes 'Ali just like the Prophet in authority. Baqillani argues that the word 'mawla' used for 'Ali does not have the same meaning as 'awla' used for the Prophet. In language, 'mawla' has several meanings, none of which is 'awla'. Therefore, when two clauses are joined but their key terms differ in meaning, one cannot conclude that the second clause carries the implication of the first. He provides an analogy: if the Prophet had said 'Am I not your prophet?' and then said 'Man kuntu mawlahu...', no one would think 'Ali becomes a prophet. Similarly, since 'awla' and 'mawla' are not synonymous, the hadith does not give 'Ali the same status as the Prophet. The confusion arises from erroneously conflating 'mawla' with 'awla'.

Correction: Baqillani argues that 'mawla' has multiple linguistic meanings, none of which is 'awla', so the hadith cannot establish that meaning.

B) Because 'mawla' does not mean 'awla' in language
C) Because the Prophet did not intend obedience to 'Ali
D) Because 'Ali was not present

Key Takeaway

Linguistic objection to 'mawla' meaning 'awla' and its refutation
Baqillani responds to linguistic evidence for 'mawla' meaning 'awla'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's response to the linguistic evidence that 'mawla' means 'awla', and why he argues that context determines meaning.

Core Idea
Context, not etymology, determines the meaning of 'mawla'.

In this passage, Baqillani faces an objection from opponents who cite Quranic verses, hadith, and poetry to claim that 'mawla' can mean 'awla'. He responds by arguing that these examples do not prove a fixed linguistic equivalence; each instance must be interpreted according to its context. This lesson examines his initial rebuttal and the first evidence he tackles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and general principle
Read the opening objection where opponents ask why deny 'mawla' means 'awla', and Baqillani's response about linguistic verification through reliable transmission.
Notice the methodological rule: language claims require certain knowledge from experts.
2
Examine the three pieces of evidence
Identify the Quranic verse (57:15), the hadith about marriage guardian, and the poetry of al-Akhtal that opponents bring as proof.
3
Focus on Baqillani's response to the Quranic verse
See how Baqillani explains that 'mawla' in the verse means 'abode', not 'more entitled'. This shows context-specific interpretation.
The response to the hadith and poetry is incomplete due to page cut-off.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: ولم أنكرتم أن يكون معنى مولى معنى أولى؟ قيل لهم: لأن هذا الذي تدعونه لغة ولا يجوز ثبوته إلا بتوقيف من أهلها عليه بنقل يوجب العلم مثله وينقطع العذر به ومتى لم نجد ذلك في اللغة كما ادعيتم بطل ما قلتموه كما أنه إذا لم يكن معنى مولى معنى نبي لم يجب إثبات النبوة لعلي بمثل هذا الكلام فإن قالوا: ما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى قوله «مولى» معنى أولى في اللغة؟ بدلالة قوله تعالى: (مَأْوَاكُمُ النَّارُ هِيَ مَوْلاكُمْ) يريد أولى بكم وبدلالة قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أيما امرأةٍ نكحت بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل» يريد بغير إذن مولاها المالك لأمرها وبدلالة قول الأخطل: فأصبَحْتَ مولاها من الناس كلهم وأحرى قُرَيْش أن تُهابَ وتُحْمَدَا يقول أصبحت وليها، فجعل مولاها بمعنى وليها؟ يقال لهم: ليس فيما ذكرتموه ما يدل على أن معنى مولى معنى أولى؛ لأن قوله: «هي مولكم» المراد به مكانهم وقرارهم وكذلك فسره الناس؛ وأما قوله: «بغير إذن وليها» فكان يجب أن
For if they say: Why do you deny that the meaning of 'mawla' is the meaning of 'awla'? It is said to them: Because this which you claim is language, and it is not permissible to establish it except by verification from its people through a transmission that produces certain knowledge similar to it and cuts off excuse, and whenever we do not find that in the language as you have claimed, what you say becomes invalid, just as if the meaning of 'mawla' is not the meaning of 'prophet', it is not necessary to establish prophethood for Ali by such speech. Then if they say: What makes you deny that the meaning of his saying 'mawla' is the meaning of 'awla' in the language, as indicated by His statement, the Most High: (Your abode is the Fire; it is your mawla) meaning 'more entitled to you', and as indicated by the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her wali, her marriage is invalid' meaning without the permission of her mawla, the one who has authority over her, and as indicated by the saying of al-Akhtal: 'You have become her mawla among all people, and the most deserving of Quraysh to be feared and praised' he says 'You have become her wali', so he made 'mawla' in the meaning of 'wali'. It is said to them: There is nothing in what you have mentioned that indicates that the meaning of 'mawla' is the meaning of 'awla'; because His saying: 'It is your mawla' is intended as their place and abode, and thus the people interpreted it; as for his saying: 'without the permission of her wali', it was necessary that...
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents counter Baqillani's argument by citing linguistic evidence that 'mawla' can mean 'awla'. They bring three pieces of evidence: first, the Quranic verse (57:15) where 'mawlakum' is interpreted as 'awla bikum' by some linguists; second, the hadith about marriage requiring a wali, where 'mawla' is used to mean the guardian; third, a line of poetry by al-Akhtal where 'mawla' is used to mean 'wali' (guardian). The opponents argue that since 'mawla' is used in these texts to mean 'awla' or 'first in right', the same meaning should apply in the hadith. Baqillani responds by denying that these examples prove the equation. For the Quranic verse, he explains that 'mawla' here means 'abode' or 'place of arrival', not 'more entitled'. For the hadith, he begins to explain that it means 'wali' (guardian) but the text cuts off. The argument seems to be that 'wali' is not the same as 'awla'. The passage ends incomplete.

Misconception: The word 'mawla' has a fixed meaning that applies everywhere.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'mawla' has multiple meanings depending on context; it does not always mean 'awla'.

C) The Fire is more entitled to you
D) The Fire is your ruler

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'mawla' means 'awla' by arguing that context determines meaning, and he demonstrates this with the Quranic example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran or a hadith, are you quick to assume a meaning because it's familiar, or do you pause to verify the context as Baqillani did? What step can you take this week to check the context of a text you recite often?

The opponents used sincere sounding evidence—Quran, hadith, poetry—but Baqillani showed each was misapplied. Can you think of a time when you used a proof text out of context to support a view you liked? How will you prevent that in the future?

Baqillani's Refutation of 'Mawlā' as 'Awlā'
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'mawlā' means 'awlā' using two linguistic arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the linguistic claim that 'mawlā' means 'awlā' by examining two contexts: marriage guardianship and classical poetry.

Core Idea
The word 'mawlā' has multiple meanings; it does not automatically imply priority or succession.

In this lesson, we follow Imam al-Baqillani as he dismantles a key argument used to justify political succession based on the Prophet's statement 'Man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu.' The opponents claimed that 'mawlā' here means 'awlā' (more entitled). Baqillani counters by examining two separate occasions where the word appears—in a marriage guardianship hadith and in a line of poetry—to show that the word can mean 'master,' 'guardian,' or 'helper,' but never 'priority.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how Baqillani structures his refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two examples
Baqillani uses two examples to show that 'mawlā' does not mean 'awlā'. The first is from a hadith about marriage guardianship. The second is from a line of poetry by al-Akhtal. Find where each is cited.
3
Analyze Baqillani's reasoning
For each example, Baqillani explains the intended meaning. For the hadith, he distinguishes between 'walī' and 'mawlā'. For the poetry, he explains that 'mawlā' here means 'helper' due to context. Summarize his reasoning in your own words.
This will help you grasp the refutation.

بغير إذن وَلِيّها فنكاحها باطل يريد بغير إذن مولاها المالك لأمرها وبدلالة قول الأخطل: فأصبَحْتَ مولاها من الناس كلهم وأحرى قُرَيْش أن تُهابَ وتُحْمَدَا يقول أصبحت وَلِيّها, فجعل مولاها بمعنى وليها. يقال لهم: ليس فيما ذكرتموه ما يدل على أن معنى مولى معنى أولى؛ لأن قوله: «هي مولكم المراد به مكانهم وقرارهم وكذلك فسره الناس؛ وأما قوله: «بغير إذن وليها» فليس وليها من مولاها في شيء لأن أبا المرأة وأخوتها وبني عمها أولياؤها وليسوا بمول لها وإن كان ولي الأمة مولى لها لأنه لم يكن مولى لها من حيث كان وليها لأن من ذكرناه ولي وليس بمولى وقول الأخطل: «فأصبحت مولاها»، إنما أراد ناصرها والحامي عنها لأن المولى يكون بمعنى الناصر وكان عبد الملك بن مروان إذ ذاك أقدر على نصرها وأشدها تمكناً من ذلك فلهذا قال وأحرى قريش أن تهاب وتحمدا :أي: إنك أقدرها على إعزاز ونصرة وإجلال وإهابةٍ وإذا كان ذلك كذلك بطل ما قلتم .
"Without the permission of her guardian (waliyyiha), her marriage is invalid" — he intends by it "without the permission of her master (mawlaha), the owner of her affair" and by the indication of the saying of al-Akhtal: "So you became her mawlā from all the people, and more deserving that Quraysh be feared and praised." He says "you became her walī," thus he made "mawlaha" in the meaning of "walīha." It is said to them: In what you have mentioned there is nothing indicating that the meaning of mawlā is the meaning of awlā; because His saying: "It [the Fire] is your mawlā" — what is meant by it is your place and your abode, and similarly the people interpreted it. As for His saying: "without the permission of her walī" — her walī is not from her mawlā in anything, because the father of the woman, her brothers, and her paternal cousins are her awliyā' (guardians) and they are not mawlā for her, even if the guardian of a slave-girl is a mawlā for her, but he was not a mawlā for her by virtue of being her walī, because those we mentioned are walī and not mawlā. And the saying of al-Akhtal: "So you became her mawlā" — he only intended by it her helper and defender, because mawlā can be in the meaning of nāṣir (helper). And Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan at that time was more capable of helping her and more strongly empowered for that, so for this he said "and more deserving that Quraysh be feared and praised," meaning: you are the most capable of them for honor and help and exaltation and fear. And if that is the case, what you have said is invalid.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a linguistic argument. The opponents (likely Shi'a) claim that in the hadith "Man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu," the word "mawlā" means "awlā" (more entitled, more worthy), thereby arguing that ʿAlī is the rightful successor. Baqillani begins by citing a hadith about marriage guardianship: "Without the permission of her walī, her marriage is invalid." He notes that some interpret "walī" here as "mawlā" meaning master. He then quotes a line from the poet al-Akhtal, where "mawlā" is explained as "walī." The opponents try to use such examples to equate "mawlā" with "walī" and then with "awlā." Baqillani refutes this by showing that "mawlā" and "walī" are not identical. He first explains that when the Qur'an says "al-Nār hiya mawlākum" (the Fire is your mawlā), it means "your final destination," not "your guardian." Then regarding the marriage hadith, he argues that a woman's father and brothers are her awliyā' (guardians) but they are not called mawlā. Only the master of a slave-girl is called both walī and mawlā, but the relationship is different. Thus, the terms are distinct. Next, Baqillani analyzes al-Akhtal's line: "So you became her mawlā from all the people." He explains that the poet meant "protector and helper" (nāṣir), not "priority." He provides context: Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan was the most capable of helping her, so the poet praised him as her mawlā in the sense of protector. Therefore, the opponents' claim that "mawlā" always means "awlā" is invalid.

Key Terms
مولى (mawlā)
أولى (awlā)
ولي (walī)
ناصر (nāṣir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'mawlā' in the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlāhu' necessarily means 'awlā' (more entitled) and implies political succession.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'mawlā' has multiple meanings; in the contexts of marriage guardianship and poetry, it means 'master' or 'helper', not 'priority'. Therefore, the hadith does not automatically establish succession.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use to argue that 'mawlā' does not always mean 'awlā'?
A) The consensus of the Companions
B) The marriage guardianship hadith and a line from al-Akhtal
C) The miracles of the Prophet
D) The Qur'anic verse about the Fire

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the linguistic claim by demonstrating that 'mawlā' can mean 'master' or 'helper', not just 'priority', using the marriage hadith and poetry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or recite the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu,' have you ever quickly assumed a political meaning without considering the full linguistic context? After this lesson, how will you hold yourself accountable to studying words more carefully before drawing conclusions?
?
Think of a time when you were convinced that a single word in a sacred text must mean exactly what you wanted it to mean, ignoring other possible interpretations. How does Baqillani's method of examining multiple usages challenge you to repent of that rigidity and seek a more comprehensive understanding?
?
If you have been quick to cite the 'mawlā' hadith in debates about leadership, what does this lesson reveal about your sincerity in following the Sunnah? Are you willing to admit that the evidence is not as clear-cut as you assumed, and to adjust your belief accordingly—even if it means changing a deeply held opinion?

Consensus Overrides Apparent Meaning: Baqillani's Argument
Baqillani argues that consensus overrides the apparent meaning of the Ghadir Khumm hadith even if 'mawlā' means 'awlā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the consensus of the Ummah (ijmāʿ) to disprove the Shi'i interpretation of the Ghadir Khumm hadith, even conceding that 'mawlā' could mean 'awlā'.

Core Idea
Consensus of the Ummah overrides any linguistic possibility.

Baqillani engages with the Shi'i argument that the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu' designates ʿAlī as the immediate political successor. Even if we grant that 'mawlā' can mean 'awlā' (more entitled), Baqillani shows that the unanimous practice of the Companions—who never understood the hadith as establishing ʿAlī's authority during the Prophet's lifetime—proves that the intended meaning is not political leadership. He reinforces his case with the incident where ʿUmar addressed the Prophet as 'my mawlā and the mawlā of every believer.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Baqillani starts with a concession ('even if it were established'), then presents the apparent meaning, and then introduces the evidence that diverts from it.
2
Identify the hypothetical premise
Baqillani concedes that 'mawlā' could mean 'awlā' for the sake of argument. Why does he do this? This shows his dialectical method.
He is not conceding the linguistic point; he is showing that even if it were so, the conclusion does not follow.
3
Understand the role of consensus
Baqillani argues that the Ummah unanimously agrees that the Prophet did not impose obedience to ʿAlī during his lifetime. If the hadith meant immediate political appointment, it would have been acted upon, but it wasn't. Therefore, the apparent meaning is overridden.
Consensus (ijmāʿ) is a primary source of evidence in Sunni theology.
4
Examine the supporting evidence from ʿUmar
Baqillani cites the narration that ʿUmar said to the Prophet: 'You have become my mawlā and the mawlā of every believer.' If 'mawlā' meant 'ruler,' then ʿUmar would be affirming the Prophet's authority, which is true, but it also shows that the word was used in a general sense and not to designate ʿAlī.
This further confirms that the hadith does not imply political appointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنه لو ثبت أن معنى مولاه معنى أولى وإن كان محتملاً لوجوه أخر، لم يجب أن يكون المراد بقوله: فمن كنت مولاه، فعلي مولاه: من كنت أولى به، وإن نُسِقَ بعض الكلام على بعض وكان ظاهره يقتضي ذلك لدليل صَرَفَه عما يقتضيه وهو أنّ الأمة مجمعة على أن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم أوجب ما أوجبه بقوله: «ما كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه في وقت وقوع هذا القول في طول أيام حياة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فلو كان إنما أثبت له الولاية عليهم وجعله أولى بهم وألزمهم طاعته والانقياد لأوامره لوجب أن يكون قد أثبته إماماً وأوجب الطاعة له آمراً وناهياً فيهم مع وجوده سائر مدته صلى الله عليه وسلم فلما أجمعت الأمة على فساد ذلك وإخراج قائله من الدين ثبت أنه لم يُرَدْ به «فمن كنت مولاه من كنت أولى به؛ ولم يرد بقوله «فعلي مولاه» أنه أولى به. ويدل على ذلك أيضاً ويؤكده ما يروونه من قول عمر: أصبحت مولاي ومولى كل مؤمن; فأخبر أنه قد ثبت كونه مولى له ولكل مؤمن فلم ينكر.
And furthermore, even if it were established that the meaning of 'mawlāhu' is the meaning of 'awlā'—although it is susceptible to other meanings—it would not be necessary that what is intended by His saying: 'For whomever I am his mawlā, ʿAlī is his mawlā' be 'for whomever I am more entitled (awlā) to him,' even if some speech is connected to other speech and its apparent meaning necessitates that, because of an evidence that diverted it from what it necessitates—namely that the Ummah is unanimously agreed that the Prophet, peace be upon him, obligated what he obligated by his saying: 'For whomever I am his mawlā, ʿAlī is his mawlā' at the time of the occurrence of this statement throughout the days of the Prophet's life. So if he had thereby established for him authority (wilāyah) over them and made him more entitled (awlā) to them and obligated them to obey him and submit to his commands, then it would have been necessary that he had established him as an imam and obligated obedience to him as a commander and prohibitor among them while he (the Prophet) was present for the rest of his term. But when the Ummah unanimously agreed upon the invalidity of that and the expulsion of its proponent from the religion, it is established that what was intended by 'For whomever I am his mawlā' is not 'for whomever I am more entitled (awlā) to him,' and it was not intended by his saying 'ʿAlī is his mawlā' that he is more entitled (awlā) to him. And that is also indicated and confirmed by what they narrate from the saying of ʿUmar: 'You have become my mawlā and the mawlā of every believer'; so he informed that his being a mawlā for him and for every believer is established, and he did not deny it.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument that proceeds even after conceding a hypothetical point. Baqillani says: even if we accept that 'mawlā' can mean 'awlā' (more entitled)—though the word has other meanings—it does not follow that the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu' means 'For whomever I am more entitled, ʿAlī is more entitled.' Why? Because a decisive evidence (dalīl) diverts it from that apparent meaning. That evidence is the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Ummah. The Prophet said this hadith during his lifetime, and if it meant that ʿAlī was to be the immediate ruler and commander over the people while the Prophet was still alive, then the Companions would have been obliged to obey ʿAlī's commands and prohibitions during the Prophet's lifetime. But the Ummah unanimously agrees that such an obligation is absurd and that anyone who claims it is a heretic. Therefore, the hadith cannot mean that. Baqillani then provides additional confirmation: the narration from ʿUmar ibn al-Khaṭṭāb, who said to the Prophet: 'You have become my mawlā and the mawlā of every believer.' If 'mawlā' meant 'awlā' in the sense of political authority, then ʿUmar would have meant that the Prophet was his ruler and the ruler of every believer, which is true in a general sense, but ʿUmar did not use it to claim that the Prophet's authority was limited to him or that ʿAlī was thereby designated. Thus, the word 'mawlā' retains its broader meaning of 'friend,' 'helper,' or 'protector,' not exclusive political leadership.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijmāʿ)
ولاية (wilāyah)
أولى (awlā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the word 'mawlā' can mean 'awlā' (more entitled), then the hadith necessarily designates ʿAlī as the political successor.
Correction: Baqillani argues that even if 'mawlā' can mean 'awlā', the consensus of the Ummah that the Prophet did not impose obedience to ʿAlī during his lifetime overrides that apparent meaning, proving the hadith does not imply political authority.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Baqillani cite to override the apparent meaning of the hadith even if 'mawlā' meant 'awlā'?
A) The hadith of Ghadir Khumm was abrogated
B) The consensus that the Prophet did not appoint ʿAlī as ruler during his own lifetime
C) ʿAlī's own rejection of the caliphate
D) The Qur'anic verse 'Today I have perfected your religion'

Key Takeaway
Consensus of the Ummah is a decisive evidence that can override the apparent meaning of a hadith, even when linguistic possibilities exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Companion who heard the Prophet say, 'For whomever I am his mawlā, ʿAlī is his mawlā', would you have immediately understood it as a political appointment? Why or why not? What does your answer reveal about your tendency to rely on personal understanding rather than the collective reasoning of the Ummah?
?
Consensus of the Ummah overrides even strong linguistic possibilities. When you encounter a Qur'anic verse or hadith whose apparent meaning seems to support a cherished belief, do you check whether the scholarly consensus has ruled otherwise? Are you willing to submit to that consensus even if it humbles your personal preference?
?
Baqillani uses the consensus of the Companions as decisive. Imagine yourself in the time of the Prophet: would you have been among those who understood the hadith correctly and submitted to the consensus, or would you have insisted on your own interpretation? What does that say about the role of humility in seeking truth?

Baqillani's Refutation of the Imamate Interpretation of Ghadir
Baqillani refutes the claim that mawla means awla using ijma and Umar's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the consensus of the ummah and Umar's testimony to refute the claim that 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith means 'awla' (more deserving), thereby undermining the argument for Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The ummah's consensus and Umar's words prove the hadith does not establish political imamate.

Baqillani tackles a key Shi'a proof: the Ghadir Khumm hadith. Opponents claim 'mawla' means 'awla' (more deserving), implying Ali's imamate. But Baqillani argues that internal evidence—the consensus of the community and a report from Umar—shows the Prophet did not intend that. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic original. Notice its logical flow: first a conditional argument, then a proof from consensus, then a supporting proof from Umar.
2
Understand the first argument from consensus
Baqillani says if 'mawla' meant imamate, then the Prophet would have required obedience during his life, but the ummah agrees that is invalid. This refutes the interpretation.
Key term: إجماع (ijma).
3
Analyze the second argument from Umar's statement
Umar called Ali 'mawla of every believer', and the Prophet did not correct him. This shows 'mawla' does not imply authority over the Prophet's community.
Notice Baqillani's use of a companion's action as proof.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Together, these two proofs divert the apparent meaning of the hadith away from imamate. Conclude that the Shi'a claim is refuted.
This is a classic Sunni response to the Ghadir argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنه لو ثبت أن معنى مولاه معنى أولى وإن كان محتملاً لوجوه أخر، لم يجب أن يكون المراد بقوله: فمن كنت مولاه، فعلي مولاه»: من كنت أولى به، وإن نُسِقَ بعض الكلام على بعض وكان ظاهره يقتضي ذلك لدليل صَرَفَه عما يقتضيه وهو أنّ الأمة مجمعة على أن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم أوجب ما أوجبه بقوله : «ما كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه في وقت وقوع هذا القول في طول أيام حياة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فلو كان إنما أثبت لـه الولاية عليهم وجعله أولى بهم وألزمهم طاعته والانقياد لأوامره لوجب أن يكون قد أثبته إماماً وأوجب الطاعة له آمراً وناهياً فيهم مع وجوده سائر مدته صلى الله عليه وسلم فلما أجمعت الأمة على فساد ذلك وإخراج قائله من الدين ثبت أنه لم يُرَدْ به «فمن كنت مولاه من كنت أولى به؛ ولم يرد بقوله «فعلي مولاه» أنه أولى به ويدل على ذلك أيضاً ويؤكده ما يروونه من قول عمر: أصبحت مولاي ومولى كل مومن فأخبر أنه قد ثبت كونه مولى له ولكل مؤمن فلم ينكر ذلك النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم فدلّ أنه قد أثبت له في ذلك الوقت ما أثبته لنفسه وليس هو الولاية عليهم ولزوم طاعتهم له فهذه دلالة تصرف الكلام عن مقتضاه لو كان معنى مولى معنى أولى وكان نسق الكلام يقتضي ذلك، فسقط ما تعلقوا به .
And upon the assumption that the meaning of mawlah is awla, even if it is possible for other meanings, it is not necessary that the intended meaning of his statement: 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla' is 'whoever I am more deserving of him' — even if some speech is juxtaposed and its apparent meaning requires that — due to a proof that diverts it from its requirement: namely that the ummah is agreed that the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, obligated what he obligated by his statement 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla' at the time of this utterance throughout the days of the Prophet's life. If he had only established for him authority over them and made him more deserving of them and obligated them to obey him and submit to his commands, then it would be necessary that he had established him as imam and obligated obedience to him as commander and prohibiter among them during the rest of his tenure. Since the ummah agreed on the invalidity of that and the exclusion of its proponent from the religion, it is established that it was not intended by it 'whoever I am his mawla, I am more deserving of him', and it was not intended by his statement 'Ali is his mawla' that he is more deserving of him. Also that which they narrate from Umar indicates and confirms: 'You have become my mawla and the mawla of every believer'; so he informed that it is established that he is his mawla and the mawla of every believer. The Prophet did not deny that. So it indicates that he established for him at that time what he established for himself, and that is not authority over them and the obligation of their obedience to him. So this is an indication that diverts the speech from its requirement even if the meaning of mawla were awla and the conjunction of speech demanded that. So what they clung to has fallen.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the word 'mawla' in the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' means 'awla' (more deserving), which would imply Ali's imamate. The argument proceeds in two main steps. First, Baqillani appeals to the consensus (ijma) of the ummah: if the Prophet had intended to establish imamate and make Ali the ruler over them, he would have required obedience to him during his own lifetime, but the ummah agrees that such an interpretation is invalid and that anyone who claims it is outside the religion. Therefore, the hadith cannot mean that. Second, he cites the testimony of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who said to Ali, 'You have become my mawla and the mawla of every believer.' The Prophet did not correct Umar or deny this statement, which confirms that the mawla-ship established for Ali is not a political authority requiring obedience, but something else. These two proofs together divert the apparent meaning of the hadith away from the imamate interpretation, even if the word 'mawla' could lexically bear the meaning of 'awla.' Thus, the argument of those who use this hadith to support Ali's imamate is refuted.

Key Terms
مولى
أولى
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ghadir Khumm hadith establishes Ali's political imamate.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the ummah's consensus and Umar's statement prove 'mawla' does not mean 'awla' (more deserving) in a political sense, thus the hadith does not establish imamate.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what evidence from the ummah's consensus refutes the claim that 'mawla' in the hadith means 'awla'?
A) The Prophet stated it only once.
B) The ummah agrees that the Prophet did not intend imamate, because if he had, he would have required obedience during his lifetime, which is unanimously rejected.
C) Umar denied it.
D) The word has many meanings.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the imamate interpretation of the Ghadir hadith by showing that the ummah's consensus and Umar's testimony prove 'mawla' does not mean 'awla'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Islam based on a single text without considering how the early community (the ummah) understood it? What would it take for you to submit your interpretation to the consensus of the scholars, even if it contradicts a cherished view?
?
If Umar ibn al-Khattab, a close companion, understood the Ghadir hadith as not establishing political leadership, are you willing to trust his understanding over later sectarian interpretations? What pride or bias might prevent you from doing so?
?

Is there any belief you hold that you would not abandon even if it were proven to contradict the consensus of the righteous predecessors?

Refuting 'Mawla' as 'Awla'
Baqillani refutes interpreting mawla as awla based on context and grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses context and grammar to refute the Shia interpretation of 'mawla' as 'awla' in the Ghadir hadith.

Core Idea
Context and grammar determine meaning, not sectarian preference.

Baqillani now turns to a key sectarian argument: the Shi'a claim that in the famous Ghadir hadith, the Prophet's word 'mawla' means 'awla' (more deserving), implying 'Ali's authority. Baqillani counters that the linguistic and contextual evidence does not support this meaning. Let us examine his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the key phrase 'تصرف الكلام عن مقتضاه' (the speech diverts from its implication).
2
Identify Baqillani's premise
Baqillani states that the Prophet established for 'Ali something similar to what he established for himself, but it is not authority over the community or obedience.
This sets up the context for the grammatical argument.
3
Understand the grammatical conditional
Baqillani uses a conditional: 'if the meaning of mawla were awla, and the sequence of speech required that, then the speech would divert from its implication.' Since the context does not require awla, the condition fails.
This shows how grammar and context work together.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Because the linguistic evidence does not support 'awla', the Shia argument collapses. The word must mean something else, like 'helper' or 'friend'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم فدلّ أنه قد أثبت له في ذلك الوقت ما أثبته لنفسه وليس هو الولاية عليهم ولزوم طاعتهم له فهذه دلالة تصرف الكلام عن مقتضاه لو كان معنى مولى معنى أولى وكان نسق الكلام يقتضي ذلك، فسقط ما تعلقوا به .
That Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, indicated that he established for him at that time what he established for himself, and it is not the authority over them and the necessity of their obedience to him. This is an indication that the speech diverts from its implication if the meaning of mawla were the meaning of awla, and the sequence of speech required that, so what they clung to fell away.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shia claim that in the Ghadir hadith, 'mawla' means 'awla' (more deserving) to establish 'Ali's authority. Baqillani argues that the context of the Prophet's statement shows he affirmed something like his own status, not the authority over the community or obedience owed to him. The grammatical structure does not demand the meaning 'awla'; therefore, the Shia interpretation collapses.

Key Terms
مولى
أولى
ولاية
طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith must mean 'awla' (more deserving), proving 'Ali's right to rule.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the context of the Prophet's speech and the grammatical structure do not require that meaning; thus the word likely means 'helper' or 'friend'.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue about the meaning of 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith?
A) It means 'awla' (more deserving of authority).
B) It means 'helper' or 'friend', not authority.
C) The Prophet appointed 'Ali as his successor.
D) The word 'mawla' has a single fixed meaning.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that linguistic and contextual analysis discredits the Shia interpretation of 'mawla' as 'awla'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever interpreted a verse or hadith to support a belief you already held, even when the context suggested otherwise? What did you do when you realized the truth?
?
This lesson shows that linguistic honesty can dismantle false claims. Is there an area of your faith where you are tempted to twist words to fit your desires? Will you commit to seeking the plain meaning, even if it challenges you?
?
Baqillani's argument required him to set aside sectarian bias. When you read the Quran or hadith, are you willing to let the text speak for itself, even if it means letting go of a cherished opinion? What will you do today to practice that discipline?

The Nine Meanings of Mawla
Baqillani enumerates nine possible meanings of mawla and denies the Shia interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nine possible lexical meanings of the word 'mawla' as enumerated by Imam Baqillani, and why the Shia interpretation of 'mawla' as 'imam' is linguistically unfounded.

Core Idea
Mawla has nine meanings in Arabic, none of which imply obligatory obedience.

Baqillani begins by directly addressing the Shia claim that the Prophet's statement at Ghadir Khumm established 'Ali as the imam of the Muslims. He first establishes the lexical possibilities of the word 'mawla', showing its semantic range, and explicitly denies that it carries the meaning of 'imam whose obedience is obligatory'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the opening question and Baqillani's list of nine meanings. Notice that he states the denial immediately after the list.
2
Identify the nine meanings
As you read, count the meanings. They are: helper, paternal cousin, loving friend, place/abode, emancipator, freed slave, neighbor, in-law, ally.
This is a comprehensive list.
3
Understand the denial
Note the clear statement: 'mawla' does not mean an imam whose obedience is obligatory. This is the core of the argument.
Baqillani will provide evidence for the meanings in subsequent sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى مولى عندكم؟ وما الذي أثبته النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بهذا الكلام لعليّ وقصد به؟ قيل لهم : أما معنى مولى فإنه يتصرف على وجوه : فمنها المولى بمعنى الناصر، ومنها المولى بمعنى ابن العم، ومنها المولى بمعنى الموالي المحب ومنها المولى بمعنى المكان والقرار ومنها المولى بمعنى المعتق المالك للولاء، ومنها المولى بمعنى المعتق الذي ملك ولاءه، ومنها المولى بمعنى الجار، ومنها المولى بمعنى الصهر، ومنها المولى بمعنى الحِلْفٌ فهذا جميع ما يحتمله قوله مولى (١) . وليس من معنى هذه اللفظة أن المولى إمام واجب الطاعة قال الله تعالى في المولى بمعنى الناصر: ﴿ وإن تظاهرا عليه فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين ) (٢) يعني ناصره (۳) ؛ وقال الأخطل : فأصبحت مولاها من الناس كُلّهم وأحرى قُرَيْش أن تُهابَ وتُحْمَدَا أي فأصبحت ناصرها وحامي ذمارها . وأما المولى بمعنى ابن العم فمشهور. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وإني خفت الموالي من ورائي ) (٤) يعني العم (۱) قال الفضل بن العباس بن عُتبة بن أبي لهب (٢) يخاطب بني امية : مهلاً بَني عَمِّنا مَهْلاً مَوَالِينَا لا تُنبوا بيننا ما كان مدفونا لا تَحْسَبُوا أَن تُهِينُونا ونُكْرمكم وأن نَكُفَّ الأذى عنكم وتُؤْذُونا اللهُ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّا لا نُحِبُّكُمُ ولا تَلُومُكُمُ الاَ تُحِبُّونَا وأما المولى بمعنى المُعْتِقِ والمُعْتَقِ، فأظهر من أن يكشف ؛ يقال : فلان يعني مُعْتِقَه ومالِكَ ولائه، وفلان مولى لفلان يراد به مُعْتَقٌ له وأما المولى بمعنى الموالى المُحِبِّ فظاهر في اللغة؛ يقال فلان مولى فلان أي مُحِبّ له وولي له ؛ وقد رُوِيَ في قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «مُزَيَّنَةُ وجُهَيْنةً وأسلمُ وغِفَارٌ مـوالي الله ورسوله»(۳) أي مُحِبُّون موالون لهما . وأما المولى بمعنى الجار فمعروف في اللغة ؛ قال مربع بن دعدعة ، وكان جاور كليب ابن يربوع فأحسنوا جواره : جزى اللهُ خَيْراً والجزاء بكَفِّه كُليب بن يربوع وزادُهُم حَمْدا هم خلطونا بالنفوس وأَلْجُمُوا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومةً جُردا أي إلى نصر جارهم. وأما المولى بمعنى الصهر فمعروف أيضاً ؛ قال أبو المختار يزيد بن قيس الكلابي في ظلامته إلى عمر في أمرائه : فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر وكان الرجل صهراً لبني بدر. وأما المولى بمعنى الحليف فمـذكـور أيضاً ; قال بعض الشعراء (٤) :
If they say: What does mawla mean for you? And what did the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, establish for 'Ali with this speech and intend? Say to them: As for the meaning of mawla, it takes on various aspects: among them mawla meaning the helper, among them mawla meaning the paternal cousin, among them mawla meaning the loving friend, among them mawla meaning the place and abode, among them mawla meaning the emancipator who owns the wala', among them mawla meaning the freed slave who owns his wala', among them mawla meaning the neighbor, among them mawla meaning the in-law, among them mawla meaning the ally. This is all that the word mawla can bear. And it is not among the meanings of this word that mawla is an imam whose obedience is obligatory. God Almighty said concerning mawla meaning the helper: 'But if you support one another against him, then indeed God is his mawla (helper) and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers' meaning his helper; and al-Akhtal said: 'I became her mawla from all people / And more deserving that Quraysh be feared and praised' i.e. I became her helper and defender. As for mawla meaning the paternal cousin, it is well known. God Almighty said: 'Indeed, I fear the mawali (relatives) after me' meaning the paternal uncle. Al-Fadl ibn al-Abbas ibn Utba ibn Abi Lahab addressed the Banu Umayya: 'Gently, O our cousins, gently our mawali / Do not stir up what was buried between us...' As for mawla meaning the emancipator and the freed slave, it is too clear to need explanation; it is said: So-and-so meaning his emancipator and owner of his wala', and so-and-so is a mawla of so-and-so meaning a freed slave of his. As for mawla meaning the loving friend, it is clear in the language; it is said: so-and-so is a mawla of so-and-so, i.e. a lover of his and a friend of his; and it has been narrated in the saying of the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace: 'Muzayna, Juhayna, Aslam, and Ghifar are the mawali of God and His Messenger' i.e. they are lovers and friends of them. As for mawla meaning the neighbor, it is known in the language; Muraqqish ibn Da'd'a said, and he had Kulaib ibn Yarbu' as a neighbor and they treated him well: 'May God reward Kulaib ibn Yarbu' with good...' i.e. to support their neighbor. As for mawla meaning the in-law, it is also known; Abu al-Mukhtar Yazid ibn Qais al-Kilabi said in his complaint to 'Umar about his officials: 'Do not forget both benefactors / And this one in the market is the mawla of Banu Badr' and the man was an in-law of Banu Badr. As for mawla meaning the ally, it is also mentioned; one poet said:
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Shia question about the meaning of 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith. Baqillani first lists all the possible lexical meanings of 'mawla' (nine in total): helper, paternal cousin, loving friend, place/abode, emancipator, freed slave, neighbor, in-law, and ally. He then states explicitly that 'imam whose obedience is obligatory' is not among them. He then provides evidence for each meaning, starting with the helper meaning using Quran (Surah al-Tahrim 4) and the poetry of al-Akhtal. Then he gives Quran (Surah Maryam 5) and poetry for the cousin meaning. He notes that the meaning of emancipator/freed slave is too obvious to need explanation. He then provides hadith evidence for the loving friend meaning (the Prophet praising certain tribes). He gives poetry for the neighbor meaning, and a story for the in-law meaning. Finally, he begins the ally meaning but the text cuts off. This comprehensive lexical argument shows that the Shia interpretation is linguistically baseless.

Correction: Baqillani shows it has many meanings and none imply authority.

?

Evidence for Helper and Cousin Meanings
Baqillani provides linguistic evidence for two meanings of mawla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Quranic verses and Arabic poetry to support the meanings of 'mawla' as helper and paternal cousin.

Core Idea
Quran and poetry confirm that mawla means helper and cousin.

Having listed the nine possible meanings, Baqillani now provides textual evidence. He begins with the meaning of 'helper' (nasser), citing the Quran and a verse by al-Akhtal, and then the meaning of 'paternal cousin', citing another Quranic verse and a poem by al-Fadl ibn al-Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence for 'helper'
Notice the Quran verse from Surah al-Tahrim: 'Indeed God is his mawla' meaning helper. Then read al-Akhtal's poetry.
2
Read the evidence for 'cousin'
See the Quran verse from Surah Maryam: 'I fear the mawali (relatives) after me' meaning cousins. Then the poem by al-Fadl.
The poetry shows the word 'mawali' used for cousins.
3
Compare the evidence
Both meanings are supported by primary sources of Arabic language (Quran and authoritative poetry).

Evidence for Emancipator, Loving Friend, Neighbor, In-law, and Ally
Baqillani provides evidence for the remaining six meanings of mawla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses hadith, poetry, and common usage to support the meanings of 'mawla' as emancipator, freed slave, loving friend, neighbor, in-law, and ally.

Core Idea
Mawla's meanings extend to social relationships like patronage, love, neighborhood, and alliance.

Baqillani continues his lexical argument by providing evidence for the remaining meanings of mawla. He notes that the meaning of emancipator/freed slave is too obvious to require proof, then cites a hadith for the loving friend meaning, and poetry for neighbor, in-law, and ally.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence for emancipator/freed slave
Baqillani says this meaning is clear and does not need elaboration. Note the linguistic usage.
2
Read the hadith for 'loving friend'
The Prophet said certain tribes are 'mawali of God and His Messenger' meaning they love them.
3
Read the poetry for neighbor and in-law
Two poems show mawla used for neighbor and in-law.
4
Read the beginning of ally evidence
The text cuts off, but Baqillani mentions the ally meaning and starts a poem.

فإن قالوا : فما معنى مولى عندكم؟ وما الذي أثبته النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بهذا الكلام لعليّ وقصد به؟ قيل لهم : أما معنى مولى فإنه يتصرف على وجوه : فمنها المولى بمعنى الناصر، ومنها المولى بمعنى ابن العم، ومنها المولى بمعنى الموالي المحب ومنها المولى بمعنى المكان والقرار ومنها المولى بمعنى المعتق المالك للولاء، ومنها المولى بمعنى المعتق الذي ملك ولاءه، ومنها المولى بمعنى الجار، ومنها المولى بمعنى الصهر، ومنها المولى بمعنى الحِلْفٌ فهذا جميع ما يحتمله قوله مولى (١) . وليس من معنى هذه اللفظة أن المولى إمام واجب الطاعة قال الله تعالى في المولى بمعنى الناصر: ﴿ وإن تظاهرا عليه فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين ) (٢) يعني ناصره (۳) ؛ وقال الأخطل : فأصبحت مولاها من الناس كُلّهم وأحرى قُرَيْش أن تُهابَ وتُحْمَدَا أي فأصبحت ناصرها وحامي ذمارها . وأما المولى بمعنى ابن العم فمشهور. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وإني خفت الموالي من ورائي ) (٤) يعني العم (۱) قال الفضل بن العباس بن عُتبة بن أبي لهب (٢) يخاطب بني امية : مهلاً بَني عَمِّنا مَهْلاً مَوَالِينَا لا تُنبوا بيننا ما كان مدفونا لا تَحْسَبُوا أَن تُهِينُونا ونُكْرمكم وأن نَكُفَّ الأذى عنكم وتُؤْذُونا اللهُ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّا لا نُحِبُّكُمُ ولا تَلُومُكُمُ الاَ تُحِبُّونَا وأما المولى بمعنى المُعْتِقِ والمُعْتَقِ، فأظهر من أن يكشف ؛ يقال : فلان يعني مُعْتِقَه ومالِكَ ولائه، وفلان مولى لفلان يراد به مُعْتَقٌ له وأما المولى بمعنى الموالى المُحِبِّ فظاهر في اللغة؛ يقال فلان مولى فلان أي مُحِبّ له وولي له ؛ وقد رُوِيَ في قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «مُزَيَّنَةُ وجُهَيْنةً وأسلمُ وغِفَارٌ مـوالي الله ورسوله»(۳) أي مُحِبُّون موالون لهما . وأما المولى بمعنى الجار فمعروف في اللغة ؛ قال مربع بن دعدعة ، وكان جاور كليب ابن يربوع فأحسنوا جواره : جزى اللهُ خَيْراً والجزاء بكَفِّه كُليب بن يربوع وزادُهُم حَمْدا هم خلطونا بالنفوس وأَلْجُمُوا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومةً جُردا أي إلى نصر جارهم. وأما المولى بمعنى الصهر فمعروف أيضاً ؛ قال أبو المختار يزيد بن قيس الكلابي في ظلامته إلى عمر في أمرائه : فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر وكان الرجل صهراً لبني بدر. وأما المولى بمعنى الحليف فمـذكـور أيضاً ; قال بعض الشعراء (٤) :
If they say: What does mawla mean for you? And what did the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, establish for 'Ali with this speech and intend? Say to them: As for the meaning of mawla, it takes on various aspects: among them mawla meaning the helper, among them mawla meaning the paternal cousin, among them mawla meaning the loving friend, among them mawla meaning the place and abode, among them mawla meaning the emancipator who owns the wala', among them mawla meaning the freed slave who owns his wala', among them mawla meaning the neighbor, among them mawla meaning the in-law, among them mawla meaning the ally. This is all that the word mawla can bear. And it is not among the meanings of this word that mawla is an imam whose obedience is obligatory. God Almighty said concerning mawla meaning the helper: 'But if you support one another against him, then indeed God is his mawla (helper) and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers' meaning his helper; and al-Akhtal said: 'I became her mawla from all people / And more deserving that Quraysh be feared and praised' i.e. I became her helper and defender. As for mawla meaning the paternal cousin, it is well known. God Almighty said: 'Indeed, I fear the mawali (relatives) after me' meaning the paternal uncle. Al-Fadl ibn al-Abbas ibn Utba ibn Abi Lahab addressed the Banu Umayya: 'Gently, O our cousins, gently our mawali / Do not stir up what was buried between us...' As for mawla meaning the emancipator and the freed slave, it is too clear to need explanation; it is said: So-and-so meaning his emancipator and owner of his wala', and so-and-so is a mawla of so-and-so meaning a freed slave of his. As for mawla meaning the loving friend, it is clear in the language; it is said: so-and-so is a mawla of so-and-so, i.e. a lover of his and a friend of his; and it has been narrated in the saying of the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace: 'Muzayna, Juhayna, Aslam, and Ghifar are the mawali of God and His Messenger' i.e. they are lovers and friends of them. As for mawla meaning the neighbor, it is known in the language; Muraqqish ibn Da'd'a said, and he had Kulaib ibn Yarbu' as a neighbor and they treated him well: 'May God reward Kulaib ibn Yarbu' with good...' i.e. to support their neighbor. As for mawla meaning the in-law, it is also known; Abu al-Mukhtar Yazid ibn Qais al-Kilabi said in his complaint to 'Umar about his officials: 'Do not forget both benefactors / And this one in the market is the mawla of Banu Badr' and the man was an in-law of Banu Badr. As for mawla meaning the ally, it is also mentioned; one poet said:
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Shia question about the meaning of 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith. Baqillani first lists all the possible lexical meanings of 'mawla' (nine in total): helper, paternal cousin, loving friend, place/abode, emancipator, freed slave, neighbor, in-law, and ally. He then states explicitly that 'imam whose obedience is obligatory' is not among them. He then provides evidence for each meaning, starting with the helper meaning using Quran (Surah al-Tahrim 4) and the poetry of al-Akhtal. Then he gives Quran (Surah Maryam 5) and poetry for the cousin meaning. He notes that the meaning of emancipator/freed slave is too obvious to need explanation. He then provides hadith evidence for the loving friend meaning (the Prophet praising certain tribes). He gives poetry for the neighbor meaning, and a story for the in-law meaning. Finally, he begins the ally meaning but the text cuts off. This comprehensive lexical argument shows that the Shia interpretation is linguistically baseless.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates through hadith and poetry that mawla can mean emancipator, loving friend, neighbor, in-law, and ally, further refuting the Shia exclusive interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Prophet called the tribes 'mawali of God and His Messenger' because of their love and loyalty. Do you truly love God and His Messenger in a way that would earn you that title? What area of your life shows the greatest gap between your claimed love and your actual choices?

?
If mawla can also mean 'ally', who are you allied with in this life? Are your alliances built on taqwa and love for Allah, or on worldly benefit? What does your answer reveal about your real loyalty?

Two Meanings of 'Mawla' in the Hadith of Ali
The author gives two possible meanings of 'mawla' in the famous hadith about Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of 'mawla' in the hadith and how Imam Baqillani interprets them to clarify Ali's status.

Core Idea
The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' has two valid interpretations, each highlighting different aspects of Ali's virtue.

Imam Baqillani examines the famous hadith about Ali's mawla-ship. He shows that the word 'mawla' is ambiguous and can mean both 'supporter' and 'beloved/guardian'. By analyzing these two meanings, he clarifies the hadith's implications for Ali's inner state and the obligations of believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Read the passage and find where the author presents the two meanings of 'mawla'. The first is about support (nusrah), the second about guardianship/love (wilayah).
Notice that the author begins by saying 'it can bear two meanings.'
2
Understand the first meaning
The first interpretation: 'mawla' means supporter. The Prophet declares that whoever he supports, Ali also supports in the same thorough manner—both outwardly and inwardly. This assures Ali's inner purity.
The author contrasts this with ordinary supporters who may only act outwardly.
3
Understand the second meaning
The second interpretation: 'mawla' means beloved/guardian. The Prophet says that whoever loves him outwardly and inwardly must also love Ali the same way. This makes love and loyalty to Ali obligatory in both outer and inner dimensions.
Unlike for other believers, we only judge their outer conduct.
4
Compare the two meanings
Both interpretations elevate Ali's status, but the first emphasizes his inner virtue, while the second establishes a unique obligation of love and loyalty from believers.
The author shows that both are plausible and consistent with Ali's high rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما قصد به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بقوله: «من كنت مولاه، فعلي مولاه فإنه يحتمل أمرين أحدهما من كنت ناصره على دينه وحامياً عنه بظاهري وباطني وسري وعلانيتي فعلي ناصره على هذا السبيل فتكون فائدة ذلك الإخبار عن أن باطن عليّ وظاهره في نصرة الدين والمؤمنين سواء والقَطعَ على سريرته وعلو رتبته ؛ وليس يُعتقد ذلك في كي ناصر للمؤمنين بظاهره ؛ لأنه قد ينصر الناصر بظاهره طلب النفاق والسمعة وابتغاء الرّفْدِ ومتاع الدنيا فإذا أخبر النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم أن نُصرة بعض المؤمنين في الدين والمسلمين كنصرته هو صلّى الله عليه وسلم قطع على طهارة سريرته وسلامة باطنه وهذه فضيلة عظيمة. ويَحْتَمِلُ أيضاً أن يكون المراد بقوله : «فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه»، أي من كنت محبوباً عنده وولياً له على ظاهري وباطني فعلي مولاه أي إنّ وِلاءَهُ ومحبته من ظاهره وباطنه واجب كما أن ولائي ومحبتي على هذا السبيل واجب فيكون قد أوجب مولاته على ظاهره وباطنه ولسنا نوالي كل من ظهر منه الإيمان على هذه السبيل بل إنما نواليهم في الظاهر دون الباطن .
As for what the Prophet (peace be upon him) intended by his saying: 'Whoever I am his mawla, then Ali is his mawla'—it can bear two meanings. One of them is: Whoever I am his supporter in his religion, and his protector outwardly and inwardly, secretly and publicly, then Ali is his supporter in this way. Then the benefit of that is the information that Ali's inner and outer [self] are equal in supporting the religion and the believers, and certainty about his innermost and high rank. And this is not believed about any supporter of the believers outwardly; because the supporter may support outwardly seeking hypocrisy and fame and seeking provision and worldly goods. So when the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed that the support of some believer in the religion and for the Muslims is like his own support, he affirmed the purity of his innermost and the soundness of his interior, and this is a great virtue. And it can also bear the meaning of his saying: 'Whoever I am his mawla, then Ali is his mawla,' i.e., whoever I am beloved to him and a guardian for him outwardly and inwardly, then Ali is his mawla, i.e., his guardianship and love from his outer and inner are obligatory, just as my guardianship and love in this way are obligatory. Thus he made his mawla obligatory in his outer and inner, and we do not ally with everyone who shows faith in this way, but rather we ally with them outwardly without the inner.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is an interpretative analysis of the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa Ali mawlahu'. The author explains that the word 'mawla' in this context can bear two meanings. The first meaning is 'supporter' (nasir): the Prophet declares that whoever he supports in religion and protects outwardly and inwardly, Ali is also his supporter in the same manner. The benefit is that it assures the listener that Ali's inner state matches his outer actions in supporting the faith, a virtue not predicable of others who may support only outwardly for worldly reasons. The second meaning is 'beloved/guardian' (wali): the Prophet says that whoever considers him a beloved and guardian both outwardly and inwardly must also consider Ali the same, making love and loyalty to Ali obligatory in both aspects. However, the author clarifies that this obligatory love and loyalty to Ali is not the same as the general love we have for other believers, which is only outward while their inner state remains unknown to us. Thus, this interpretation elevates Ali's status to requiring unconditional inner loyalty, unlike others.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa Ali mawlahu' has only one unambiguous meaning and is clear-cut in its implications.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the word 'mawla' is ambiguous and can bear two distinct interpretations, each with different theological implications.

Checkpoint
What is the first interpretation of 'mawla' given by Imam Baqillani?
A) Love and guardianship
B) Support and protection
C) Neighborly relations
D) Manumission

Key Takeaway
The word 'mawla' in the hadith can be interpreted as 'supporter' or 'beloved/guardian', both highlighting Ali's unique status but with different nuances regarding his inner virtue and the believers' duties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author distinguishes between outward support for worldly motives and inner purity. Examine your own heart: when you speak or act in support of Islam, are you seeking Allah's pleasure alone, or do you seek the praise of people? What specific habit can you change this week to align your inner intention with your outer actions?
?
If Ali is truly your mawla in the sense of beloved and guardian, what does that require of you in terms of your loyalty to him? Are there any areas of your life where you hesitate to follow the example of the Prophet's family? Make a concrete plan to increase your love and obedience to them, starting today.
?
This lesson shows that a single word can bear multiple valid meanings. How often do you approach ambiguous religious texts with a desire to understand rather than to confirm your own opinions? Commit to studying one hadith or verse this week with an open mind, seeking multiple interpretations before settling on one.

Why Was Ali Specifically Mentioned?
Objection-answer unit on Ali's specific mention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological reasoning behind the Prophet's specific mention of Ali, and how it preemptively refutes future accusations.

Core Idea
The Prophet's singular mention of Ali was a preemptive defense against future slander.

This passage addresses a natural question: why was Ali singled out when many companions were righteous? The answer reveals the Prophet's foreknowledge of hypocrites and rebels who would later attack Ali's faith and leadership. The argument ties the mention to divine knowledge and a theological safeguard, not mere favoritism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the structure: an imagined question followed by the author's response.
The objection assumes many companions were perfectly sincere outwardly and inwardly.
2
Identify the core reasoning
Focus on the phrase 'it is possible that it reached him...' – the answer speculates on the Prophet's motivation based on foreknowledge of slander.
3
Understand the theological argument
The answer argues that if Ali were destined to leave the religion, Allah would not command belief in his guardianship. This proves the slander false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument using divine knowledge.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the mention was a political appointment. The author reframes it as a theological defense against future accusations.
The passage explicitly mentions 'hypocrites and rebels' (المنافقين والشراة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما وجه تخصيصه بهذا القول وقد كان عندكم في الصحابة خَلق عظيم ظاهرهم كباطنهم؟ قيل له : يحتمل أن يكون بلغه صلّى الله عليه وسلم قدح قادح فيه أو ثلبُ ثالب أو أخبر أن قوماً من أهل النفاق والشراة سيطعنون عليه ويزعمون أنه فارق الدين وحكم في أمر الله تعالى الآدميين ويسقطون بذلك ولايته ويزيلون ولاءه فقال ذلك فيه لينفي ذلك عنه في وقته وبعده لأن الله تعالى لو علم أن علياً سيفارق الدين بالتحكيم أو غيره على مـا قُرِف به لم يأمر نبيه أن يأمر الناس باعتقاد ولايته ومحبته على ظاهره وباطنه والقطع على طهارته وهو يعلم أنه يختم عمله بمفارقة الدين لأن من هذه سبيله في معلوم الله تعالى فإنه لم يكن قط ولياً لله ولا ممن يستحق الولاية والمحبة،
If it is said: What is the reason for specifying him with this saying, when there were among the Companions a great many whose outward was like their inward? It is said to him: It is possible that it reached him (peace be upon him) that a critic would criticize him or a defamer defame him, or he was informed that a group of hypocrites and rebels would attack him and claim that he had left the religion and judged in the matter of Allah the Exalted [over] human beings, thereby dropping his guardianship and removing his alliance. So he said that concerning him to negate that from him in his time and after, because if Allah the Exalted knew that Ali would leave the religion through arbitration or otherwise, as he was accused of, He would not have commanded His Prophet to command the people to believe in his guardianship and love outwardly and inwardly and to be certain of his purity while knowing that he would end his life with leaving the religion. For anyone whose way is like that in Allah's knowledge, he was never a friend of Allah nor among those who deserve guardianship and love.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector asks: why was Ali specifically singled out for this declaration when many companions were genuinely righteous both outwardly and inwardly? The answer: the Prophet (peace be upon him) foresaw that hypocrites and rebels would later slander Ali, accusing him of abandoning the religion through arbitration (referring to the conflict with Mu'awiya and the arbitration at Siffin). Therefore, the Prophet made this statement to preemptively reject those accusations. The argument is backed by a theological point: if Ali were destined to apostatize, Allah would not have commanded the Prophet to demand that people believe in his inner purity and love. Since Ali was never a friend of Allah in that case, but the command was given, it proves the falsity of the slander.

Correction: The author explains it was a theological defense against future slander, not a mere political gesture.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's specific mention of Ali was a divinely guided precaution against future slander, proving Ali's ultimate sincerity and Allah's knowledge of his end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Prophet argued that if Allah had known Ali would apostatize, He would not have commanded love for him. Consider your own life: Are you living as though your end is good, or are you relying on a future repentance while persisting in sin? If Allah knows your end, what does your current state say about it?
?
The Prophet prepared a defense for Ali against accusations that hadn't even happened yet. Do you preemptively defend the honor of the scholars and righteous people of this Ummah, or do you only speak up after their reputation is already tarnished? What concrete action can you take this week to guard the honor of someone who is being attacked unjustly?

The Two Possible Meanings of 'Mawla' in the Hadith
Baqillani introduces the two meanings of 'mawla' in the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct interpretations that Imam al-Baqillani offers for the word 'mawla' in the Prophet's statement: supporter/helper and beloved/friend, and how these interpretations avoid the claim of political succession.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings; the hadith does not necessarily imply succession.

Imam al-Baqillani begins by clarifying the linguistic ambiguity of the word 'mawla'. He quotes a line of poetry to show that 'mawla' can mean client by alliance, not just kinship. Then he presents two interpretations of the Prophet's statement about Ali: as a supporter in religion or as a beloved friend whose love is obligatory. This lesson unpacks those two meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the poetry through the two meanings. Notice how Baqillani uses the poetry to set up the semantic range of 'mawla'.
The poetry is by a pre-Islamic poet; it illustrates that 'mawla' can refer to a client by alliance.
2
Identify the first meaning
Find where Baqillani says the first possible meaning is 'nasir' (supporter/helper). Note how he explains that this implies Ali's inner and outer support are equal to the Prophet's.
This meaning emphasizes Ali's sincerity and high rank.
3
Identify the second meaning
Find the second meaning: 'mahbub/wali' (beloved/friend). Notice that this interpretation makes love and friendship for Ali obligatory in both apparent and hidden, just as for the Prophet.
This meaning does not imply political authority.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: How do these two meanings counteract the Shiite claim that the hadith is an explicit appointment of Ali as successor?
Both interpretations are about support and love, not about leadership.
