No Fixed Number for the Imamate Contract
Baqillani rebuts the fixed-number requirement for imamate contract attendees, using 'Umar's shura as counterevidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects a fixed number of attendees for the imamate contract and how he uses 'Umar's shura as evidence for flexibility rather than a numerical rule.

Core Idea
The imamate contract does not require a fixed number of attendees; 'Umar's shura was about merit, not procedure.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a common objection: that a minimum of four attendees is necessary for the imamate contract, based on 'Umar's shura. He explains that 'Umar's selection of six was based on their virtue, not as a procedural rule, and that the contract can even be made in seclusion. This lesson clarifies the flexibility of the bay'ah process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: objection, response, analogy rejection, practical point.
2
Identify the objection
The objection says that four attendees are necessary, analogizing to 'Umar's shura. Find where Baqillani states this.
The phrase 'وقد قال قوم...' introduces the objection.
3
Understand Baqillani's rebuttal
Baqillani argues that 'Umar's shura was not about setting a number but about consulting those he considered most virtuous. He quotes 'Umar's statement about Salim to prove this.
The quote shows 'Umar cared about the person, not the count.
4
Note the practical concession
Baqillani adds that the contract can be done secretly to avoid false claims, showing that even one person could suffice.
This underscores that there is no fixed number.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this flexibility relates to the broader Sunni view of imamate: it's not a rigid process but one based on community consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فهل يجب عندكم أن يحضر العقد للإمام قوم من المسلمين؟ قيل لهم : أجل وليس يجب أن يكون لمن يحضر العقد نفر من المسلمين تمت البيعة. وقد قال قوم إن أقل ما يجب أن يحضر أربعة نفر بعد العاقد والمعقود له قياساً على فعل عمر في الشورى وهذا ليس بواجب لم يقصد بجعلها شورى في ستة تحديد عدد الحاضرين للعقد وإنما جعلها فيهم دون غيرهم لأنهم أفاضل الأمة وقد أخبر بذلك عن نفسه بقوله : «أما إنه لو حضرني سالم (۱) مولى أبي حذيفة (۲) ، لرأيت أني قد أصبت الرأي وما تداخلني فيه الشكوك، يريد من أخذ رأيه ومشورته فبطل ما قالوه وإنما أن يعقد الرجل لغيره مستسراً للعقد وخالياً به لئلا يدعي ذلك كل أحد يمنع وأنه قد كان عقد له سراً فيؤدي ذلك إلى الهرج والفساد.
If they said: 'Is it obligatory in your view that for the contract of the imam a group of Muslims be present?' It is said to them: Yes, but it is not necessary that for those who attend the contract there be a number of Muslims for the pledge to be complete. Some people have said that the minimum that must attend is four persons after the contracting party and the one for whom the contract is made, by analogy with the action of ‘Umar in the shura. But this is not obligatory, because he did not intend by making it a shura of six to determine the number of those present for the contract; rather, he made it among them and not others because they were the most virtuous of the ummah. And he informed about that by his statement: 'Indeed, if Salim, the client of Abu Hudhayfa, were present for me, I would think that I have hit upon the opinion and what enters me of doubts' – meaning, from whose opinion and consultation I take. So what they said is invalid. And that a man may contract for another while concealing the contract and in seclusion with him, lest everyone claim and prevent it, and that it may have been contracted in secret, leading to confusion and corruption.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of how many people must be present for the imamate contract. The author affirms that some Muslim attendees are required, but not a specific number. Some argued for a minimum of four, citing ‘Umar's shura of six, but the author rejects this because ‘Umar's selection of six was based on their merit (afādil al-ummah), not as a procedural requirement. To prove this, the author quotes ‘Umar saying that if Salim had been present, he would have considered his opinion; this shows that ‘Umar valued talent over a fixed count. The author then adds a practical point: the contract can be done in seclusion to prevent false claims and chaos, as was done secretly in some cases.

Key Terms
بيعة
شورى
أفاضل الأمة
عاقد
معقود له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A fixed number (e.g., four) is necessary for the imamate contract.
Correction: Baqillani shows that no specific number is required; the process is flexible and based on merit and circumstances.

Checkpoint
What is Baqillani's main argument against requiring exactly four attendees for the imamate contract?
A) Four is the minimum stated in the Qur'an
B) ‘Umar's shura was based on merit, not number
C) The Prophet specified three as the minimum
D) Four people are impossible to gather

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that the imamate contract does not require a fixed number of attendees; what matters is the legitimacy of the contract, not a specific count.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make important decisions, do you prioritize consulting the most capable and wise people, or do you hide behind fixed procedures and numbers to avoid responsibility? How would adopting Baqillani's principle of merit over number change your approach to leadership or seeking advice?
?

?
If you were in a position of authority, would you trust your own judgment to appoint someone based on merit even if it meant bypassing a set number of witnesses? What fears might hold you back from doing what Baqillani described as permissible and wise?

Why the ummah cannot revoke the imam's contract without cause
Baqillani argues that the ability to contract the imamate does not imply the ability to annul it, using analogies from marriage, sale, and ritual acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the ability to appoint an imam does not imply the ability to remove him without cause, using analogies from marriage, sale, and acts of worship.

Core Idea
In Sharia, the right to contract does not include the right to annul.

Baqillani addresses an objection: if the ummah can select an imam, why can't they dismiss him at will? He responds by showing that Islamic law consistently separates the power to initiate a contract from the power to dissolve it. This lesson unpacks his argument and its analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and the series of examples.
The author uses a concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objector argues that the ummah's ability to contract the imamate should include the ability to annul. Find how Baqillani responds.
The response is a firm 'No' followed by evidence.
3
Examine each analogy
List each analogy: guardian/marriage, seller/sale, fasting, kitaba/tadbir, voluntary worship. Notice how each shows that contracting does not imply annulling.
These are drawn from different areas of Islamic law.
4
Connect to the principle of imamate stability
Reflect on why this matters for the imamate: it protects the imam from arbitrary removal and emphasizes that removal requires a valid cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فهل تملك الأمة فسخ العقد على الإمام من غير حدث يوجب خلعه كما أنها تملك العقد له؟ قيل له : لا . فإن قيل : فكيف يملك لا يملك فسخه؟ قيل له : هذا في الشريعة أكثر من أن ترى أن العاقد على وليته لا يملك فسخ النكاح من حيث كان يملك عقده وكذلك العاقد البيع على سلعته لا يملك حله وإن ملك عقده وكذلك يملك عقد الصيام إلى مدة ولا يملك فسخه وكذلك يملك كتابة عبده وتدبيره، والمتطوع بالصيام والصلاة إذا دخل فيهما لا يملك حل شيء من ذلك؟ فبطل ما سألتم عنه .
If a speaker said: 'Does the ummah have the right to annul the contract of the imam without a cause that necessitates his removal, just as they have the right to contract for him?' It is said to him: No. If it is said: 'How can they have the right to contract but not to annul?' It is said to him: This is in the Sharia more than you see: that the one who contracts for his female ward cannot annul the marriage even though he could contract it, and similarly the one who contracts the sale of his commodity cannot dissolve it even though he could contract it, and he can contract fasting for a specified period but cannot annul it, and he can contract the kitaba of his slave and his tadbir, and the one who volunteers for fasting and prayer, if he enters into them, cannot dissolve any of that. So your query is invalid.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that since the ummah can contract the imamate (select an imam), they should also be able to revoke it without cause. The author responds with a series of analogies from Islamic law: (1) A guardian (al-‘āqid ‘alā waliyyatihi) can contract a marriage for his ward but cannot annul it. (2) A seller can contract a sale but cannot dissolve it unilaterally. (3) One can vow to fast for a period but cannot break it voluntarily. (4) A master can make a kitaba (contract of manumission) or tadbir (freeing after death) for his slave, but cannot undo them. (5) One who voluntarily enters supererogatory fasting or prayer cannot exit at will. Thus, the lack of symmetry between contracting and annulling is well-established in Sharī‘ah. Therefore, the ummah does not have the power to revoke the imam's contract without a legitimate cause (like apostasy or injustice).

Key Terms
فسخ العقد
وليته
عاقد البيع
كتابة
تدبير
متطوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The power to appoint an imam implies the power to remove him at will.
Correction: Islamic law often separates the right to contract from the right to annul, as shown by multiple analogies.

Checkpoint
Which analogy does Baqillani use to show that the ability to contract does not imply the ability to annul?
A) A guardian can marry his ward but cannot divorce her
B) A ruler can make a treaty but cannot break it
C) A buyer can purchase but cannot return
D) A slave can be freed but not re-enslaved

Key Takeaway
The ummah's power to appoint an imam does not grant them the power to remove him without a valid cause, as consistently demonstrated by similar principles in Islamic law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that having the right to do something gives you the right to undo it? In your personal commitments or promises, do you treat them as binding as these contracts in Sharia? What would it mean to honor your word even when it becomes difficult?
?
The ummah's limited power over the imam reminds us that authority is not arbitrary. Where do you hold authority—as a parent, leader, or friend? Do you exercise it with the understanding that it carries responsibilities and limits? How can you implement this lesson today?
?
This passage shows that entering a contract is serious and cannot be undone lightly. Are you careful in making commitments, or do you enter them expecting an easy exit? What does this lesson teach you about the weight of your words and promises before Allah?

Why a Person Cannot Appoint Himself as Imam
Baqillani refutes the possibility of self-appointment to the imamate using legal analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and legal basis for why a member of ahl al-ḥall wa al-‘aqd cannot contract the imamate for themselves, as argued by Baqillani using clear analogies from Islamic law.

Core Idea
Self-appointment in authority is invalid because Islamic law consistently separates the contractor from the beneficiary.

Baqillani addresses a potential objection: if a qualified person can appoint others as imam, why can't he appoint himself? He responds by drawing on well-known legal principles from marriage, sale, and manumission, showing that self-appointment contradicts the very structure of such contracts. This lesson unpacks his argument and the analogies he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objection-and-answer structure come through.
Notice that Baqillani starts with an objection (fa in qālū) and then responds with a clear negative (lā).
2
Identify the three analogies
The passage presents three legal examples: marriage guardianship, sale of goods, and manumission contracts. Find each one and note the pattern: the contractor acts for others but not for himself.
In each example, the person cannot contract for themselves in the same capacity.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Ask yourself: what common principle do these examples share? They show that contracts of authority require a separation between the contracting party and the beneficiary. Self-appointment would collapse that distinction.
This principle is rooted in Sharia and recognized by the ummah.
4
Apply to the imamate
Reflect on why Baqillani's analogies apply to the imamate. The imam's authority comes from the delegation of the community (ahl al-ḥall wa al-‘aqd). A person cannot delegate authority to himself.
This reinforces that the imamate is a communal responsibility, not a personal right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فهل يملك الرجل من أهل الحل والعقد عقد الإمامة لنفسه كما يملك ذلك لغيره؟ قيل لهم : لا فإن قالوا كيف يُعقل هذا؟ قيل : من حيث عُقل أمثاله من الشريعة وعقلته الأمة ألا ترى أن الإنسان يملك العقد على وليته لغيره ولا يملك العقد عليها لنفسه وكذلك العاقد على سلعته يملك عقد بيعها على غيره ولا يملك عقد بيعها على نفسه وكذلك الإنسان يملك كتابة عبده وتدبيره وعتقه ولا يجب أن يملك تدبير نفسه وكتابتها وعتقها مع نظائر لذلك؟ فسقط ما سألتم عنه .
If they said: 'Does a man from the people of loosening and binding have the right to contract the imamate for himself just as he has that right for others?' It is said to them: No. If they said: 'How can this be rational?' It is said: From where rational examples of it in the Sharia are understood, and the ummah has understood them. Do you not see that a man can contract for his female ward for someone else but cannot contract for himself? Similarly, the one who contracts for his commodity can contract its sale for another but cannot contract its sale for himself. Similarly, a man can contract the kitaba of his slave and his tadbir and his manumission, but he cannot contract the tadbir of himself, nor its kitaba, nor its manumission, along with similar cases? So your query falls.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a member of the ahl al-ḥall wa al-‘aqd can appoint himself as imam. The author answers no, and explains by analogy: (1) A guardian can marry his female ward to someone else but cannot marry her to himself. (2) A seller can sell his goods to others but cannot sell them to himself. (3) A master can make a contract of kitaba or tadbir for his slave, but cannot do so for himself. These legal examples show that the role of contracting party is distinct from the beneficiary; one cannot act on one's own behalf in a contract of authority. Therefore, a person cannot unilaterally assume the imamate; it must be granted by others.

عقد الإمامة
وليته
عاقد على سلعته
كتابة
تدبير
عتق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because a qualified person can appoint others as imam, he can also appoint himself.
Correction: Islamic law consistently prohibits self-appointment in contracts of authority, as shown by the analogies of marriage, sale, and manumission.

Checkpoint
Which analogy does Baqillani use to show that a man cannot contract the imamate for himself?
A) A guardian can marry his ward to others but not to himself
B) A buyer can buy for himself but not for others
C) A judge can judge his own case
D) A ruler can appoint his own successor

Key Takeaway
A person cannot assume the imamate unilaterally; it must be conferred by the community of qualified representatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the leaders of your community, but have you ever appointed yourself as the judge of someone else's intentions? This lesson shows that even the most qualified cannot assume authority over others without their consent. In which relationships—at home, at work, or among friends—have you unilaterally taken a role of authority that should have been granted by others? Will you repent and seek proper delegation?
?
The analogies in this passage reveal a deep principle: you cannot be both the contractor and the beneficiary. Where in your spiritual life have you tried to appoint yourself as the one who defines right and wrong for your own soul, bypassing the revelation and the community? How does this lesson force you to submit your judgments to a higher authority?
?
Baqillani uses examples of marriage and slavery to show that self-appointment is invalid. Consider your own tendency to 'appoint' yourself as the guardian of others' morals or the 'owner' of your time and resources. Do you act as if you have the sole right to decide how to use your wealth or talents, ignoring the rights of others? Will you recognize that even your own abilities are a trust, not a possession, and that you cannot appoint yourself as their sole beneficiary?

The Binding Nature of the Imam's Contract
Baqillani answers the objection that the community can revoke the imam's contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the community cannot revoke the imam's contract without cause based on Baqillani's analogies from Islamic contracts.

Core Idea
The power to contract does not imply the power to revoke.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a common objection: if the community can appoint an imam, why can't they remove him at will? He responds with a firm negative and supports his answer with multiple analogies from Islamic law, showing that the ability to initiate a contract does not automatically grant the ability to dissolve it. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and the analogies he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the objection from the questioner and Baqillani's initial 'No'. Notice the structure of the dialogue.
The objection assumes that contract power implies revocation power.
2
Examine the analogies
Identify each analogy (marriage, sale, fasting, manumission, voluntary worship). For each, note how the contract is binding once entered.
These are all drawn from everyday Islamic legal practice.
3
Connect to imamate
Reflect on how these analogies apply to the imamate contract. Why is the imamate similarly binding?
The imamate is a contract for the community's benefit, like others.
4
Conclude the argument
Understand Baqillani's conclusion: 'So what you asked about is invalidated.' The objection fails because it ignores the binding nature of contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال لهم
فإن قال قائل : فهل تملك الأمة فسخ العقد على الإمام من غير حدث يوجب خلعه كما أنها تملك العقد له؟ قيل له : لا . فإن قيل : فكيف يملك لا يملك فسخه؟ قيل له : هذا في الشريعة أكثر من أن ترى أن العاقد على وليته لا يملك فسخ النكاح من حيث كان يملك عقده وكذلك العاقد البيع على سلعته لا يملك حله وإن ملك عقده وكذلك يملك عقد الصيام إلى مدة ولا يملك فسخه وكذلك يملك كتابة عبده وتدبيره، والمتطوع بالصيام والصلاة إذا دخل فيهما لا يملك حل شيء من ذلك؟ فبطل ما سألتم عنه .
Their question: And if someone says: Does the community have the power to revoke the contract of the imam without an event that necessitates his removal, just as it has the power to contract for him? It is said to him: No. And if it is said: How can it have the power to contract but not have the power to revoke? It is said to him: This is more common in the Sharia than you might see: the one who contracts marriage for his daughter does not have the power to revoke the marriage even though he had the power to contract it; likewise, the one who contracts a sale for his commodity does not have the power to dissolve it even though he had the power to contract it; likewise, he has the power to contract fasting for a specified period but does not have the power to revoke it; likewise, he has the power to write a contract of kitaba (manumission) for his slave and to make him mudabbar, and the volunteer in fasting and prayer, when he enters into them, does not have the power to dissolve any of that. So what you asked about is invalidated.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. An objector asks: Since the community has the authority to appoint an imam, can it also remove him without any valid reason? Baqillani answers with a firm 'no'. The objector then presses further: How can the community have the power to contract but not to revoke? Baqillani provides several analogies from Islamic law to demonstrate that the power to initiate a contract does not necessarily entail the power to dissolve it. He lists examples: a father who marries off his daughter cannot unilaterally dissolve that marriage; a seller cannot rescind a sale; a person who vows to fast for a set period cannot break it; a master who enters into a kitaba or tadbir contract cannot cancel it; a volunteer in prayer or fasting cannot exit at will. In all these cases, the contract is binding once concluded. Similarly, the contract of imamate is binding and cannot be revoked except for a legitimate cause. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
العقد
الإمامة
فسخ
الأمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the community's power to appoint an imam implies an equal power to remove him arbitrarily.
Correction: As Baqillani shows, Islamic law recognizes many contracts where the right to contract does not include the right to revoke without cause.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why cannot the community revoke the imam's contract without cause?
A) Because the imam is infallible and cannot be removed.
B) Because the power to contract does not imply the power to revoke, as seen in other contracts like marriage and sale.
C) Because the community has no authority over the imam after the contract.
D) Because revoking the contract would lead to civil war.

Key Takeaway
The imamate contract is binding like other Sharia contracts and cannot be revoked without legitimate cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a commitment you entered into willingly—like a promise, a contract, or a role. Have you ever considered breaking it simply because you felt like it, without a serious reason? How does Baqillani's argument that 'initiation does not imply revocation' challenge your attitude toward that commitment? What concrete step will you take this week to honor a binding obligation you have been tempted to neglect?
?
The community cannot remove the imam without cause. How does this make you examine your own response to legitimate authority—whether in your family, community, or country? Have you ever dismissed, criticized, or rebelled against someone in authority over trivial matters? What would it look like to support a leader you disagree with, unless they clearly violate a higher command? Name one specific attitude you need to change.
?
Baqillani uses analogies from marriage, sale, and worship to show that revoking contracts is not always permitted. Look at your own life: are there 'contracts' you've made with Allah—like a vow, a commitment to pray, or a promise to give charity—that you have broken or neglected? What does it say about your seriousness in dealing with Allah? Will you renew that contract today and resolve not to break it?

Why a Qualified Person Cannot Contract Imamate for Himself
Baqillani answers the objection using analogies from marriage, sale, and manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that a person from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd cannot contract imamate for himself, based on analogical reasoning from Islamic law.

Core Idea
Just as a father cannot marry his daughter to himself, a qualified person cannot appoint himself imam.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to an objection: if the people of loosening and binding can contract imamate for others, why can't a qualified person do so for himself? He answers by drawing analogies from marriage, sale, and manumission, showing that the contracting party and the beneficiary must be distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Begin by reading the objectors' question and Baqillani's brief 'No' response. Notice the directness.
The objectors assume symmetry: if you can contract for others, you can contract for yourself.
2
Identify the three analogies
Baqillani provides analogies from marriage, sale, and manumission. For each, note how the contracting party cannot be the beneficiary.
These are well-established cases in Islamic law that the audience would accept.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider the common thread: the agent and the recipient of a contract must be distinct persons. Imamate follows this rule.
This principle prevents conflicts of interest and maintains the integrity of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال آخر لهم
فإن قالوا : فهل يملك الرجل من أهل الحل والعقد عقد الإمامة لنفسه كما يملك ذلك لغيره؟ قيل لهم : لا فإن قالوا كيف يُعقل هذا؟ قيل : من حيث عُقل أمثاله من الشريعة وعقلته الأمة ألا ترى أن الإنسان يملك العقد على وليته لغيره ولا يملك العقد عليها لنفسه وكذلك العاقد على سلعته يملك عقد بيعها على غيره ولا يملك عقد بيعها على نفسه وكذلك الإنسان يملك كتابة عبده وتدبيره وعتقه ولا يجب أن يملك تدبير نفسه وكتابتها وعتقها مع نظائر لذلك؟ فسقط ما سألتم عنه .
Another question for them: And if they say: Does a man from among the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) have the power to contract imamate for himself just as he has the power to contract it for others? It is said to them: No. And if they say: How can this be understood? It is said: From how similar examples are understood in the Sharia and the community understands them. Do you not see that a man has the power to contract marriage for his daughter to someone else but does not have the power to contract marriage upon himself? Likewise, the one contracting on his commodity has the power to contract its sale to someone else but does not have the power to contract its sale to himself. Likewise, a man has the power to write a contract of kitaba for his slave, to make him mudabbar, and to free him, but he should not have the power to manage himself, write a kitaba contract for himself, or free himself, along with similar examples to that. So what you asked about falls away.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objectors ask: Can a qualified person from the ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd (the people who contract imamate) appoint himself as imam, just as he can appoint someone else? Baqillani answers clearly: no. The objectors then ask for the rationale. Baqillani explains that this is understood from analogous cases in Islamic law that the community already accepts. He gives examples: a father can marry his daughter to another man but cannot marry her to himself; a seller can sell his goods to someone else but cannot sell them to himself; a master can contract kitaba for his slave or free him, but he cannot do these things for himself. The point is that the role of the contracting party and the recipient are distinct; one cannot act as both. Similarly, in the imamate, the person who has the authority to contract cannot simultaneously be the object of that contract. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

العقد

Misconception: A qualified person can simply declare himself imam because he has the authority to contract imamate.
Correction: Baqillani shows that analogically, the contracting party and beneficiary must be distinct, so one cannot contract imamate for oneself.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say a person cannot contract imamate for himself?
A) Because imamate is a divine appointment, not a human contract.
B) Because the contracting party and the recipient must be different, as in marriage and sale.
C) Because no one is qualified to be both imam and contractor.
D) Because the community must unanimously agree.

Key Takeaway
A person cannot contract imamate for himself because the contracting party and the recipient must be separate, as in other legal contracts.

?

Resolving Multiple Imamate Contracts in Different Regions
Baqillani explains how to handle multiple imamate contracts in different locations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani resolves conflicts arising from multiple imamate contracts in different locations, including the role of precedence and the analogy to marriage contracts.

Core Idea
When multiple imamate contracts conflict, precedence decides; if unknown, all are void.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a practical problem: what happens when different groups in different cities each appoint a qualified imam simultaneously? The answer requires careful scrutiny of the contracts, a hierarchy of solutions, and an analogy from marriage law. This lesson will walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
The objectors present a hypothetical: multiple legitimate contracts in different regions. Notice the conditions: all candidates are qualified, no previous imam.
2
Follow Baqillani's Resolution
He gives a sequence: check precedence, if known, confirm the first; if unknown, nullify and recontract; if simultaneous, also nullify. See how each scenario is handled.
3
Examine the Marriage Analogy
Baqillani draws a parallel to marriage contracts with multiple guardians. This helps solidify the principle of precedence in Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال آخر لهم
فإن قالوا : فما تقولون إذا عقد جماعات من أهل الحل والعقد لعدة أئمة في بلدان متفرقة وكانوا كلهم يصلحون للإمامة وكان العقد لسائرهم واقعاً مع عدم إمام وذي عهد من إمام ما الحكم فيهم عندكم ومن أولى بالإمامة منهم؟ قيل لهم : إذا اتفق مثل هذا تصفّحت العقود وتؤملت ونظر أيه السابق فأقرت الإمامة فيمن بُدىء بالعقد له وقيل للباقين : انزلوا عن الأمر فإن فعلوا وإلا قوتلوا على ذلك وكانوا عصاة في المقام عليها ؛ وإذا لم يعلم أيها تقدم على الآخر وادعى كل واحد منهم أن العقد سبق له أبطلت سائر العقود واستؤنف العقد لواحد منهم أو من غيرهم وإن أبوا ذلك قاتلهم الناس عليه فإن تمكنوا وإلا فهم في غلبة وفتنة وعذر في ترك إمامة الإمام وإن تُمكِّنَ من العقد لغيرهم فعل ذلك وكان الإمام المعقود له حرباً لسائر هؤلاء حتى يذعنوا ويرجعوا إلى الطاعة والسداد وإن تؤملت العقود ووجدت كلها وقعت في وقت واحد ابطل أيضاً جميعها واستؤنف العقد لرجل منهم أو من غيرهم ونظير ذلك من الشريعة عقد ولاة المرأة عليها ووجوب تسليمها إلى من سبق بالعقد له فإن أشكل ذلك وتنازع الأزواج وعُدمت البينة أبطلت العقود بأسرها وإن انكشف أن أوليائها عقدوا عليها في حالة واحدة فسخت أيضاً وكذلك القول في الإمامة .
Another question for them: And if they say: What do you say when groups of the people of loosening and binding contract for several imams in different lands, and all of them are qualified for the imamate, and the contract for all of them occurs in the absence of an imam and without a covenant from a previous imam? What is the ruling with you regarding them, and who among them is more deserving of the imamate? It is said to them: When such a situation occurs, the contracts are examined and scrutinized, and it is seen which one is earlier. Then the imamate is confirmed for the one for whom the contract was initiated, and it is said to the rest: Relinquish the affair. If they do, fine; otherwise, they are fought against for that, and they are disobedient in persisting in it. And if it is not known which one preceded the other, and each of them claims that the contract was earlier for him, all contracts are nullified and the contract is renewed for one of them or someone else. And if they refuse that, the people fight them over it. If they (the people) are able, (they do so); otherwise, they are in a state of overpowering and tribulation, and they have an excuse in abandoning the imamate of an imam. And if it is possible to contract for someone other than them, that is done, and the imam for whom the contract was made is at war with the rest until they yield and return to obedience and rectitude. And if the contracts are examined and found to have all occurred at the same time, all of them are also nullified, and the contract is renewed for a man from among them or from others. An analogy for that from the Sharia is the contract of the guardians of a woman over her and the obligation to hand her over to the one whose contract preceded. If that is problematic and the husbands dispute and evidence is lacking, all contracts are nullified. And if it is discovered that her guardians contracted for her in a single instance, they are also rescinded. And the same applies to the imamate.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer addressing the scenario of multiple simultaneous or overlapping imamate contracts. The objectors ask: If groups in different regions each contract a different imam, and all are qualified, and there is no previous imam, what is the ruling and who is the rightful imam? Baqillani answers by detailing a procedure. First, the contracts are examined to determine precedence. The one for whom the contract was initiated first is confirmed, and the others are asked to step down; if they refuse, they are fought and considered disobedient. If precedence cannot be determined, all contracts are nullified and a new contract is made for one of them or someone else. If they refuse this, fighting proceeds, and if the people are overpowered, they have an excuse for not having an imam. If all contracts occurred at the same exact time, they are also all nullified and a new contract is made. Baqillani draws an analogy to marriage: if multiple guardians contract a woman to different suitors at the same time, and precedence is unclear, all contracts are void. This shows the method for resolving such conflicts in the imamate.

Key Terms
أهل الحل والعقد
الإمامة
العقد
البيعة
السابق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple simultaneous imamate contracts can all be valid.
Correction: Baqillani rules that they must be resolved by checking precedence; if unknown or simultaneous, all are nullified and a new contract is made.

Checkpoint
What is done if multiple imamate contracts occur simultaneously?

B) The eldest candidate is chosen.
C) All contracts are nullified and a new one is made.
D) The most powerful candidate becomes imam.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani resolves conflicts over multiple imamate contracts by prioritizing precedence; if precedence is unknown or simultaneous, all contracts are void and a new one must be made.

?
The analogy of marriage contracts shows that Allah has prescribed clear procedures for resolving disputes. In conflicts you face in your personal life—whether over family matters, business, or community issues—do you actively seek the rulings of the Shari'ah, or do you rely on your own opinions and emotions? ?
Baqillani states that those who persist in their claim after being shown the earlier contract are disobedient and should be fought. Ruling on Conflicting Sects Claiming Imamate
Baqillani addresses the case of sects with conflicting beliefs all claiming the right to appoint the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani resolves the question of which sect has the right to appoint the imam when the ummah is divided.

Core Idea
The right to imamate belongs only to Ahl al-Haqq when fundamental errors are involved.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a critical challenge: if the Muslim community is split into conflicting sects, each claiming authority, how do we determine the true imam? He distinguishes between disagreements over subsidiary legal matters, where multiple opinions may be tolerated, and fundamental errors that entail disbelief or sin. This distinction shapes the entire ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the question carefully: the ummah is divided into various sects, each claiming the right to appoint the imam. Notice that Baqillani presents this as a challenge to his position.
The question presupposes that only one sect is correct.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Baqillani's answer splits the situation based on the nature of the disagreement. Identify the first scenario (subsidiary legal matters where error is excusable) and the second (matters entailing takfir/tadlil).
The second scenario is the one that concerns us, as it involves fundamental errors.
3
Trace the consequences
For each scenario, note the consequences: who can contract the imamate, whether obedience is obligatory, and what happens if the sects oppose each other or if the people of truth are weak.
Baqillani compares the situation to the Prophet's early struggle in Mecca.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال لهم آخر
فإن قالوا : فما تقولون إذا كانت الأمة مفترقة على مذاهب مختلفة وآراء متضادة والحق منها في واحد وادعى كل واحد منهم أنهم ولاة هذا الأمر دون غيرهم وتمانعوا فيه ما الحكم فيهم ، ومن أولى منهم بعقد هذا الأمر؟ قيل لهم : إن كان ما اختلف فيه من المسائل الشرعية التي الحق عندنا في جميعها، والإثم موضوع عن المخطىء فيها على قول غيرنا فكلهم ولاة هذا الأمر فأيهم سبق بالعقد لرجل تمت بيعته ولزمت طاعته وصار المخالف عليه باغياً يجب حربه وإن كان ما اختلفت فيه الأمة مما يوجب التكفير والتفسيق والتضليل فعقد الإمامة لأهل الحق منهم دون غيرهم ممن كفر أو فسق وضل بتأويله الخطأ في الدين وقد قام الدليل على أن هذه الفرقة هم أصحابنا دون المعتزلة والنجارية وغيرهم من الفرق المنسوبة إلى الأمة فإن تمكنا من ذلك حملناهم على الانقياد لمن نعقد له فإن دفعونا عنه وعقدوا لبعض موافقيهم فليس له إمامة ثابتة ولا طاعة واجبة وكنا نحن في دار قهر وغلبة وإن تقاومت الفرق وتمانعت فتلك فتنة يقوم العذرُ بها في ترك العقد وإن انحاز أهل الحق إلى فئة ونصبوا حرباً وراية وعقدوا لرجل منهم كان هو الإمام دون غيره من أهل الضلال فليس هذا التمانع إن اتفق أكبر من تمانع اليهود والنصارى والمسلمين إذا حصلوا في دار واحدة وتمانعوا وحاول أهل كل دين منهم إقـامـة الـريـاسـة لهم وتنفيذ أحكامهم في الدار ولا بأعجب من غلبة النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم وصحابته بمكة وتعذر إقامة الحق فيها قبل الفتح والهجرة فذلك حكم تغالب فرق الأمة وقهرهم الفرقة الهادية إن اتفق ذلك وبالله التوفيق .
Another question for them: If the ummah is divided into different schools and conflicting opinions, and the truth among them is one, and each of them claims that they are the guardians of this matter (imamate) to the exclusion of others, and they contend over it, what is your ruling concerning them, and who among them is most entitled to contract this matter? It was said to them: If what they differ over is from the legal matters (al-masa'il al-shar'iyya) concerning which the truth is with all of them in our view, and sin is removed from the mistaken one according to the statement of others, then all of them are guardians of this matter. Whichever of them precedes in contracting for a man, his oath of allegiance is complete and obedience to him is obligatory, and the opponent to him becomes a rebel whose war is obligatory. If what the ummah differs over is from what necessitates takfir (declaring disbelief), tafsiq (declaring sinfulness), and tadlil (declaring error), then the contract of imamate is for the people of truth among them, to the exclusion of others who have disbelieved, sinned, or erred due to their mistaken interpretation in religion. Proof has established that this group is our companions (ashabuna) to the exclusion of the Mu'tazila, the Najjariyya, and other sects attributed to the ummah. If we are able to do so, we force them to submit to the one for whom we contract. If they repel us from him and contract for one of their own supporters, then he has no established imamate and no obligatory obedience, and we are in a land of compulsion and domination. If the sects confront and contend with each other, that is a turmoil (fitna) which excuses the omission of the contract. If the people of truth gather as a group and set up war and a banner, and contract for a man from among them, then he is the imam to the exclusion of the people of misguidance. This contention, if it occurs, is not greater than the contention of Jews, Christians, and Muslims when they are in one land and contend, and the people of each religion attempt to establish leadership for themselves and implement their rulings in the land. Nor is it more astonishing than the domination of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his Companions in Mecca and the difficulty of establishing truth there before the conquest and the hijra. That is the ruling on the overwhelming of the sects of the ummah and their subjugation of the guided group, if that occurs. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning a situation where the Muslim ummah is divided into various sects with conflicting views, each claiming the right to appoint the imam. The answer distinguishes between two types of disagreements: (1) In matters of ijtihad (legal questions where error is excusable according to some), all sects are considered valid guardians, and the first to contract for an imam establishes a binding allegiance; opponents are rebels. (2) In matters that entail takfir, tafsiq, or tadlil (declaring disbelief, sinfulness, or error), only the people of truth are entitled to contract the imamate. Baqillani asserts that the correct group is 'our companions' (the Ash'aris/Sunnis), excluding the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and other deviant sects. If the people of truth are able, they enforce submission to their chosen imam; if they are pushed aside, the imam set up by the others has no validity. If the sects are balanced and oppose each other, it constitutes fitna that excuses the postponement of the contract. Baqillani compares this to the situation of Jews, Christians, and Muslims in one land, and to the Prophet's initial weakness in Mecca before the hijra, emphasizing that such contention does not negate the right of the guided group to establish the imamate when possible.

Key Terms
التكفير
التفسيق
التضليل
أهل الحق
المعتزلة
النجارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sects have an equal claim to imamate regardless of the nature of their disagreement.
Correction: Baqillani shows that only Ahl al-Haqq have the right to imamate when the disagreement involves fundamental errors that necessitate takfir, tafsiq, or tadlil.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, when the ummah is divided into sects, who is entitled to appoint the imam in matters that necessitate takfir?
A) The majority
B) All sects equally
C) Only Ahl al-Haqq (the correct sect)
D) The strongest sect

Key Takeaway
In fundamental disagreements, only the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) have the right to appoint the imam, and their imam is the legitimate one.

When you encounter a Muslim who believes differently from you on a fundamental issue, do you automatically regard their leadership as illegitimate? The passage says that if Ahl al-Haqq are overpowered, they live in a 'land of compulsion' where obedience to the false imam is not obligatory. The Imamate Belongs to the People of Truth
Baqillani states that the imamate belongs to the people of truth and explains rulings when they are overpowered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defines the rightful imamate as exclusively belonging to the saved sect and the practical rulings under different scenarios of dominance and conflict.

Core Idea
The imamate belongs to the saved sect, and its legitimacy depends on power dynamics and the presence of fitna.

Baqillani begins by asserting that the imamate is for the people of truth alone, then outlines the rulings when the believers are in power, when they are overpowered, and when there is general conflict. This passage shows how classical theology merges doctrinal truth with practical political reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the conditional structure starting with 'فإن' in multiple places.
2
Identify the four scenarios
The passage presents four distinct situations for the people of truth: (1) when they are able to force submission, (2) when they are overpowered, (3) when there is fitna, (4) when they gather and raise a banner. Find each one.
These scenarios lead to different rulings about the imam's legitimacy and obedience.
3
Understand the proof for the saved sect
Baqillani claims proof has established that the saved sect (his group) is the people of truth, excluding Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, etc. Ask: How does this proof function? Is it purely theological or also historical?
The proof is not elaborated here; it's presumed from earlier arguments.
4
Reflect on the analogy with the Prophet
Baqillani compares the situation of the people of truth in conflict to the Jews, Christians, and Muslims in one territory, and to the Prophet in Mecca before the conquest. Consider how these analogies justify delay or exception in establishing the imamate.
This shows that the imamate's practical implementation can be postponed due to circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمامة لأهل الحق منهم دون غيرهم ممن كفر أو فسق وضل بتأويله الخطأ في الدين وقد قام الدليل على أن هذه الفرقة هم أصحابنا دون المعتزلة والنجارية وغيرهم من الفرق المنسوبة إلى الأمة فإن تمكنا من ذلك حملناهم على الانقياد لمن نعقد له فإن دفعونا عنه وعقدوا لبعض موافقيهم فليس له إمامة ثابتة ولا طاعة واجبة وكنا نحن في دار قهر وغلبة وإن تقاومت الفرق وتمانعت فتلك فتنة يقوم العذرُ بها في ترك العقد وإن انحاز أهل الحق إلى فئة ونصبوا حرباً وراية وعقدوا لرجل منهم كان هو الإمام دون غيره من أهل الضلال فليس هذا التمانع إن اتفق أكبر من تمانع اليهود والنصارى والمسلمين إذا حصلوا في دار واحدة وتمانعوا وحاول أهل كل دين منهم إقـامـة الـريـاسـة لهم وتنفيذ أحكامهم في الدار ولا بأعجب من غلبة النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم وصحابته بمكة وتعذر إقامة الحق فيها قبل الفتح والهجرة فذلك حكم تغالب فرق الأمة وقهرهم الفرقة الهادية إن اتفق ذلك وبالله التوفيق.
The imamate belongs to the people of truth among them, not to others who disbelieved, or sinned, or went astray through erroneous interpretation in religion. And the proof has been established that this group is our companions, not the Mu'tazila, the Najjariyya, and others among the sects attributed to the community. If we are able to do that, we force them to submit to the one we appoint. But if they repel us from him and appoint one of their own partisans, then he has no established imamate and no obedience is obligatory, and we are in a house of coercion and domination. And if the groups resist and oppose each other, that is a fitna that excuses refraining from appointment. And if the people of truth turn to a faction and set up war and a banner and appoint a man from among them, he is the imam to the exclusion of others from the people of error. This opposition, if it occurs, is not greater than the opposition of Jews, Christians, and Muslims when they gather in one house and oppose each other, and each religion attempts to establish leadership for themselves and enforce their rulings in the house. Nor is it more astonishing than the domination of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his companions in Mecca and the difficulty of establishing truth there before the conquest and the emigration. So that is the ruling of the mutual overcoming of the sects of the community and the overpowering of the rightly guided faction if that occurs. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage defines the imamate as belonging exclusively to the people of truth, who are the author's school (the Ash'aris). Proof is presumed establishing that this saved sect is distinct from the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and other deviant groups. The author then outlines the practical ruling for the people of truth if they have power: they force others to submit to their appointed imam. If the opposing group appoints their own imam, that imam has no legitimacy and obedience to him is not obligatory; the people of truth remain under duress. If there is a general conflict between groups, it is a fitna that excuses the people of truth from appointing an imam altogether. But if the people of truth rally together, raise a banner, and appoint a leader, that leader is the true imam, regardless of opposition from the people of error. The author argues that this situation is no more extraordinary than the conflicts among Jews, Christians, and Muslims in a single territory, or the Prophet's situation in Mecca before the conquest, where truth could not be established. Thus, the ruling for the mutual struggle of the Muslim sects and the eventual triumph of the rightly guided faction is as described.

Key Terms
الإمامة
أهل الحق
الفرقة الهادية
دار قهر وغلبة
فتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imamate is a purely political office that can be held by any capable Muslim.
Correction: Baqillani ties the imamate to religious truth, asserting that only the saved sect (the people of truth) has a legitimate claim, regardless of power.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the ruling on the imamate when the people of truth are overpowered and there is conflict?
A) They must still appoint an imam by force
B) They are excused from appointing an imam
C) They must follow the imam of the dominant group
D) They must appoint a temporary imam until peace

Key Takeaway
The imamate is a religious institution belonging exclusively to the saved sect, with practical legitimacy depending on the believers' ability to enforce it and excusing conditions during fitna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the imamate belongs only to the people of truth, and you claim to be among them, how do you concretely support the legitimate leadership of truth even when it is weaker than the prevailing order? Are you content to stand with the powerless truth, or do you gravitate toward the powerful falsehood?
?
Baqillani says fitna excuses you from appointing an imam—but does it also excuse you from striving to establish truth? When you witness chaotic disagreements among Muslims, do you use 'fitna' as an excuse to disengage entirely, or do you actively seek to gather the people of truth even in small ways?
?
Reflect on your own heart: when the people of truth are oppressed and their imam is not in power, do you still maintain inward loyalty and outward readiness to obey him if he calls? Or does your allegiance to truth fade when truth is not victorious?

Required Qualities of the Imam
Baqillani enumerates the five required qualities of the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five qualities that Baqillani considers necessary for the imam to be appointed.

Core Idea
The imam must be Qurayshi, knowledgeable, a capable military leader, firm in justice, and among the best.

Baqillani presents a structured list of five essential qualities for the imam. These qualities refute both overly expansive views that require infallibility and overly restrictive views that limit the imamate to a specific lineage. The following passage enumerates these requirements.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the five required qualities introduced by 'منها أن يكون'.
The passage begins with a question-and-answer format.
2
Identify each required quality
List each of the five conditions: Qurayshi lineage, judicial knowledge, military insight, firmness in punishment, and being among the best in these areas.
3
Understand the exception
Note that if an obstacle prevents appointing the best, a less excellent candidate may be appointed. This shows practicality.
This exception is at the end of the required qualities list.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Think about why Baqillani includes these particular qualities and what sectarian views he might be addressing.
These qualities counter both Shi'a and Khariji extremes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في صفة الإمام الذي يلزم العقد له فإن قال قائل : فخبرونا ما صفة الإمام المعقود له عندكم؟ قيل لهم : يجب أن يكون على أوصاف منها أن يكون قرشياً من الصميم ومنها أن يكون من العلم بمنزلة من يصلح أن يكون قاضيا من قضاة المسلمين ومنها أن يكون ذا بصيرة بأمر الحرب وتدبير الجيوش والسرايا وسد الثغور وحماية البيضة وحفظ الأمة والانتقام من ظالمها والأخذ لمظلومها وما يتعلق به من مصالحها ومنها أن يكون ممن لا تلحقه رقة ولا هوادة في إقامة الحدود ولا جزع لضرب الرقاب والأبشار ومنها أن يكون من أمثلهم في العلم وسائر هذه الأبواب التي يمكن التفاضل فيها إلا أن يمنع عارض من إقامة الأفضل فيسوغ نصب المفضول وليس من صفاته أن يكون معصوماً ولا عالماً بالغيب ولا أفرس الأمة وأشجعهم ولا أن يكون من بني هاشم فقط دون غيرهم من قبائل قريش .
Chapter on discussing the quality of the imam whose appointment is obligatory. If someone says: Inform us what is the quality of the imam appointed among you? It is said to them: He must possess qualities: among them that he be a Qurayshi of pure lineage; among them that he be of a level of knowledge fitting to be a judge among the judges of the Muslims; among them that he have insight into warfare, management of armies and raiding parties, guarding borders, protecting the community, preserving the ummah, taking vengeance from its oppressor, and supporting its oppressed, and what pertains to its interests; among them that he be one whom no softness or leniency affects in establishing punishments, nor fear of striking necks and skins; among them that he be among the best of them in knowledge and all these areas where preference is possible, unless an obstacle prevents establishing the best, then the appointment of the less excellent is permissible. And it is not among his qualities that he be infallible, nor knower of the unseen, nor the most discerning and bravest of the ummah, nor that he be only from the Banu Hashim to the exclusion of other Quraysh tribes.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This is a classification passage enumerating the required and non-required qualities of the imam. The author structures it as a question-and-answer. The five required qualities are: (1) Qurayshi descent with pure lineage, (2) sufficient knowledge to serve as a judge, (3) military and administrative insight encompassing warfare, border defense, protection of the community, and justice, (4) firmness in executing punishments without leniency or fear, (5) being among the best of the community in knowledge and these areas, unless an obstacle prevents appointing the best, in which case a less excellent candidate may be appointed. The author then clarifies what is not required: (a) infallibility, (b) knowledge of the unseen, (c) being the most discerning or bravest, (d) being exclusively from Banu Hashim rather than any Quraysh tribe. This refutes exaggerations about the imam's status.

Key Terms
قرشي
قضاة المسلمين
الحدود
المفضول
الأفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The imam must possess five key qualities: Qurayshi lineage, judicial knowledge, military insight, firmness in punishment, and being among the best, with allowance for appointing a less excellent candidate if necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to be considered for leadership among Muslims, which of these five qualities would you be most lacking in, and what are you doing today to address that deficiency?
?
The imam must be among the best—yet we often settle for less in our own choices of leaders. Where in your life have you accepted a 'less excellent' option when you knew the 'best' was possible, and what did that cost you?
?
Baqillani says the imam must have no softness in executing punishments. Is there a matter of truth or justice in your life where your 'softness' has prevented you from doing what is right? Will you commit to changing that this week?

What the Imam Does Not Need to Be
Baqillani lists four qualities not required of the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four qualities that Baqillani explicitly denies as necessary for the imam.

Core Idea
The imam is not required to be infallible, know the unseen, be the bravest, or be exclusively from Banu Hashim.

After establishing the required qualities, Baqillani clarifies what is not needed. This refutes extreme views that ascribe supernatural attributes to the imam or restrict the imamate to a specific clan. The passage lists four non-requirements.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial list
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وليس من صفاته' and note each negation.
The passage is a list of four things the imam does not need to be.
2
Identify each non-required quality
List the four denied qualities: infallibility, knowledge of the unseen, being the most discerning/bravest, and being exclusively from Banu Hashim.
3
Understand the refutations
Consider which sectarian views each denial targets. For example, infallibility is claimed by Shi'a, and Banu Hashim exclusivity by some Hashimites.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about how these denials complete the picture of the imam's qualifications, emphasizing practical leadership over supernatural attributes.
The imam is a fallible human leader, not a divinely guided prophet.

Evidence for the Quraysh Condition from Hadith and Early Practice
Baqillani provides hadith evidence and argues for its authenticity using early practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the hadith 'al-a'immatu min Quraysh' and the acceptance of the Ansar in Saqifa to prove that the imam must be from Quraysh.

Core Idea
The Ansar's acceptance in Saqifa confirms the authenticity of the Quraysh hadith.

Baqillani presents two intertwined arguments for the Quraysh condition: direct hadith evidence and the historical practice of the early Companions. He cites the Prophet's statement that leaders are from Quraysh and then shows how Abu Bakr and Umar used it in the Saqifa, with the Ansar submitting. This lesson unpacks that reasoning, demonstrating how custom and consensus support the hadith's authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow from a question to the answer with multiple evidences.
The passage is a proof passage with two main parts: direct hadith and historical precedent.
2
Identify the two arguments
First, Baqillani cites the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' and the sermon to al-Abbas. Second, he appeals to the Saqifa incident where the Companions accepted this hadith. Trace how each supports the Quraysh condition.
The hadith are said to be mutawatir in meaning, and the Saqifa acceptance is used to confirm their authenticity.
3
Understand the custom-based reasoning
Baqillani argues that if the hadith were false, the Ansar would have rejected them. Since they did not, and the dispute was intense, the principle of custom ('adah) proves the reports are genuine. Reflect on this logical move.
This reasoning ties the historical consensus to the authenticity of the transmitted reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : وما الدليل على ما وصفتم قيل له : أما ما يدل على أنه لا يجوز إلا من قريش فأمورٌ منها قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «الأئمة من قريش ما بقي منهم اثنان» وقوله للعباس حيث وصى بالأنصار في الخطبة المشهورة وكانت آخر خطبة خطبها لما قال للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: «توصي لقريش»، فقال له : «إنما أوصى قريشاً بالناس وبهذا الأمر وإنما الناس تبع لقريش ؛ فبَرُّ الناس تبع لبرهم وفاجرهم تبع لفاجرهم» في نظائر هذه الأخبار أو الألفاظ التي قد استفاضت وتواترت واتفقت على المعنى وإن اختلفت ألفاظها . ويدل على ذلك وعلى صحة هذه الأخبار أيضاً احتجاج أبي بكر وعمر على الأنصار في السقيفة بها، وما روي عن العباس من ذكره لها والأمر باعتماد عليها وما كان من إذعان الأنصار ورجوعهم لموجبها عند سماعها وإدكارِهم بها والاستشهاد عليهم بها ولولا علمهم بصحتها لم يلبثوا أن يقدحوا فيها ويتعاطوا ردّها ؛ ولا كانت قريش بأسرها بالتي تقر كذباً يُدعى عليها ولها لأن العادة جارية فيما لم يثبت من الأخبار أن يقع الخلاف فيه والقَدْحُ عند التنازع والحِجَاج لا سيما إذا احتج به في مثل هذا الأمر العظيم الجسيم مع السيوف واختلاط القول ومحاولة الإمْرَةِ والميل إلى الرياسة والعادة أصل في الأخبار فصح بذلك ثبوت هذا الأمر.
If someone says: What is the evidence for what you have described? It is said to him: As for what indicates that it is not permissible except from Quraysh, then there are matters: among them the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The leaders are from Quraysh as long as two of them remain.' And his saying to al-Abbas when he advised regarding the Ansar in the well-known sermon, which was the last sermon he delivered, when he said to the Messenger of Allah: 'You recommend to Quraysh?' So he said: 'I only recommend Quraysh to the people regarding this matter, and the people are followers of Quraysh; the righteous of the people follow their righteous, and the sinful of them follow their sinful.' — among the like of these reports or expressions that have become widespread and mutawatir, agreeing in meaning though differing in wording. And also on that and on the authenticity of these reports is the argument of Abu Bakr and Umar against the Ansar in the Saqifa by them, and what has been narrated from al-Abbas of his mentioning them and commanding reliance upon them, and what came of the submission of the Ansar and their return to their import when they heard them and their being reminded by them and being argued against by them. Had they not known their authenticity, they would have quickly impugned them and sought to reject them. And Quraysh as a whole would not have acknowledged a lie alleged against them and for them; because the custom is current regarding reports that have not been established that disagreement and impugnment occur at times of dispute and argument, especially when argued with in such a great and serious matter amidst swords and confused speech and attempts at rule and inclination to leadership. And custom is a basis for reports. So the establishment of this matter is thereby correct.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This is a proof passage. The author first provides direct textual evidence: the hadith 'al-a'immatu min Quraysh' and the hadith of the Prophet's last sermon where he tells al-Abbas that people are followers of Quraysh. He notes these reports are widespread and mutawatir in meaning. Then he argues for the authenticity of these reports by appealing to the historical practice of Abu Bakr and Umar in Saqifa, who used these hadiths to argue against the Ansar. The Ansar accepted them without objection, which would not have happened if they were false. Moreover, the author invokes the principle of custom ('adah): when a report is used in a critical situation like the Saqifa, if it were false, it would have been immediately challenged. The absence of widespread rejection confirms its authenticity. Thus, the Quraysh condition is firmly established.

Key Terms
الأئمة من قريش
السقيفة
إطباق
العادة
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some doubt the authenticity of the hadith 'al-a'immatu min Quraysh' because it is not narrated through continuous chains.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the widespread acceptance of the hadith by all Companions in the Saqifa, even by the Ansar who would have opposed it, proves its authenticity. This practical consensus outweighs technical chain issues.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani cite as evidence for the authenticity of the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش'?
A) The consensus of later scholars
B) The acceptance of the Ansar in Saqifa
C) The Quran
D) Rational argument

Key Takeaway
The Quraysh condition is established by both explicit hadith and the silent consensus of the Companions in the Saqifa incident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ansar submitted immediately when the hadith was cited. Is there a command from the Prophet that you know but have not fully submitted to in your life? What would it take for you to act on it like the Ansar did?
?
Baqillani uses the principle of custom—arguing that false reports would have been challenged. Think of a belief or practice you hold that has never been questioned by your community. Are you willing to test it against the scripture as the Ansar were tested?
?
The Prophet said people are followers of Quraysh. Do you find yourself following leaders based on their lineage, status, or worldly influence rather than their piety and adherence to the truth? What does this lesson demand you change in how you choose whom to follow?

Consensus of the Early Community on the Quraysh Condition
Baqillani cites consensus and Sa'd's response as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the early Muslim community serves as evidence for the condition that the imam must be from Quraysh.

Core Idea
The early community's agreement after initial dispute affirms the Quraysh condition.

Baqillani presents a second line of evidence: the consensus of the first generation. He notes that despite initial disagreement at Saqifa, both Muhajirun and Ansar ultimately agreed that the imamate can only be held by Quraysh. He also references the response of Sa'd ibn Ubada when reminded of prophetic reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is a brief fragment; the full quote of Sa'd is not included.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Find the consensus (إطباق الأمة) and the saying of Sa'd ibn Ubada. Understand how each supports the Quraysh condition.
The consensus is presented as 'additional evidence' (ويدل).
3
Reflect on consensus after disagreement
Consider why Baqillani emphasizes 'after the disagreement' – this strengthens the argument that the final agreement is authoritative.
The initial disagreement at Saqifa is well-known; the subsequent consensus resolves it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على ما قلناه إطباق الأمة في الصدر الأول من المهاجرين والأنصار بعد الاختلاف الذي شَجَرَ بينهم على أن الإمامة لا تصح إلا في قريش وقول سعد بن أبي عبادة لأبي بكر وعمر عند الاحتجاج بهذه الأخبار وادكارِه بها :

Commentary

The author also references the response of Sa'd ibn Ubada to Abu Bakr and Umar when they presented the hadith evidence. The passage is cut off, but the argument is that this consensus reinforces the previous evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The consensus of the early community is presented as decisive proof. When you study Islamic teachings, do you seek out the settled consensus of scholars, or do you prefer opinions that are comfortable or popular? How can you train yourself to submit to the weight of collective evidence over personal inclination? What is one area where you need to change your approach?

The Imamate is Established by Consensus
Baqillani affirms the authority of consensus in the imamate and rejects dissenting opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni theology, the legitimacy of the imamate is determined by communal consensus (ijma), and later individual opinions cannot overturn that consensus.

Core Idea
Consensus settles the imamate; later dissent does not matter.

This passage from Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il concludes a discussion on the imamate by citing an early saying and then asserting the authority of consensus. He explicitly dismisses the views of Dirar and others who came after the consensus was formed. This illustrates a key principle in Sunni political theology: the decisive role of communal agreement in establishing leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening saying
Start by reading the Arabic phrase: 'نحن الوزراء وأنتم الأمراء' (We are the ministers and you are the princes). This is likely a quote from early Islamic history, perhaps addressed to the caliph or community.
The exact context is not provided, but it sets up a relationship between rulers and subjects.
2
Identify the main conclusion
After the quote, Baqillani states: 'فثبت أن الحق في اجتماعها' meaning 'it is established that the truth lies in their consensus.' Note the pronoun 'ها' refers back to the community or the scholars.
This is the core doctrinal claim.
3
Understand the rejection of dissent
He then adds: 'وأنه لا معتبر بقول ضرار وغيره ممن حدث بعد هذا الإجماع' meaning no consideration is given to the statement of Dirar and others who came after this consensus. This explicitly rejects later opinions.
Dirar ibn Amr was a Mu'tazili scholar, but the principle applies broadly.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why consensus is given such authority. In Sunni thought, the community's agreement is a source of legal and political legitimacy. This passage emphasizes that once a consensus is reached, later views cannot overturn it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نحن الوزراء وأنتم الأمراء فثبت أن الحق في اجتماعها وأنه لا معتبر بقول ضرار وغيره ممن حدث بعد هذا الإجماع.
We are the ministers and you are the princes. So it is established that the truth lies in their consensus and that no consideration is given to the statement of Dirar and others who came after this consensus.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage provides a concluding remark on the method of establishing the imamate. The author quotes a statement (likely from a hadith) and then asserts that the truth is in the consensus (ijma) of the Muslim community regarding the imamate. He specifically rejects the opinions of Dirar (possibly Dirar ibn Amr) and others who held dissenting views after the consensus was formed. This emphasizes the authority of communal consensus in determining the legitimacy of the imam and disregards later innovations.

Key Terms
إجماع
ضرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that individual scholarly opinion can override established consensus on the imamate.
Correction: This passage shows that once consensus is reached, later individual opinions—like that of Dirar—are disregarded. Consensus is the final authority.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani assert about the truth regarding the imamate?
A) It is determined by the best individual.
B) It lies in the consensus of the community.
C) It is established by the Quran alone.
D) It is derived from the Prophet's designation.

Key Takeaway
Consensus of the community is the authoritative basis for the imamate; later individual opinions are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an established Islamic ruling that goes against your personal preference, do you seek out weak or minority opinions to justify yourself? How does the dismissal of Dirar's later opinion challenge you to submit to the collective wisdom of the scholars?
?
The saying 'We are the ministers and you are the princes' places the consensus above the individual. In your own life, do you allow the consensus of the righteous to govern your practice, or do you elevate your own reasoning above theirs? What would it take for you to humbly accept that the truth is with the majority of the early community?
?
Are there any beliefs or practices you hold today that are contradicted by the historical consensus of the Salaf? What is holding you back from abandoning them, and how does this lesson about the finality of consensus strengthen your resolve to align with the truth?

Three Proofs for the Imam's Knowledge
Baqillani provides three proofs that the imam must be a knowledgeable scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs from Baqillani that the imam must be a knowledgeable scholar.

Core Idea
The imam must be a scholar to lead, appoint judges, and judge rightly.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani demonstrates that the imam's role requires not just political authority but deep religious knowledge. He provides three distinct proofs, drawing on consensus and practical responsibilities. Let's examine each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Proofs
Read the passage and identify the three separate proofs Baqillani lists. Notice how each begins with 'among them is'.
The proofs are consensus, the imam's role in appointing judges, and his ability to judge personally.
2
Understand Each Proof's Logic
For each proof, ask: what is the core reasoning? The first relies on consensus, the second on the need to evaluate judges, the third on the imam's direct judicial role.
The second proof implies the imam must be at least as knowledgeable as the judges he appoints.
3
Reflect on the Conclusion
Notice how the author concludes 'So what we have said is established.' Consider why these proofs together are compelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه يجب أن يكون من العلم بمنزلة ما وصفناه فأمور: منها إجماع الأمة على ذلك ممن قال بالنص والاختيار. ومنها أنه الذي يولى القضاة والحكام وينظرُ في أحكامهم وما يوجب صرفهم وجَرحَهم ونقض أحكامهم ; ولن يملك علمه بذلك وتمكّنه منه إلا بأن يكون كَهُمْ في العلم أو فوقهم. ومنها إجماع الأمة على أن للإمام أن يباشر القضاء والأحكام بنفسه ولا يستخلف قاضياً ما استغنى بنفسه ونظره ; ولن يصلح للحكم إلا من صلح أن يكون قاضياً من قضاة المسلمين فصح بذلك ما قلناه.
As for what indicates that he must possess knowledge to the degree we described, there are matters: among them is the consensus of the community on that, from those who support designation and choice. And among them is that he is the one who appoints judges and governors and examines their rulings and what necessitates their dismissal, impeachment, and annulment of their rulings; and he cannot possess knowledge of that and be capable of it unless he is like them in knowledge or above them. And among them is the consensus of the community that the imam may personally undertake judgment and rulings and not appoint a deputy as long as he can manage by himself and his own examination; and no one is fit for judgment except one who is fit to be a judge among the judges of the Muslims. So what we have said is established.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a proof text establishing that the imam must be a person of knowledge. The author presents three distinct evidences: First, the consensus of the Muslim community (including both those who advocate appointment by prophetic text and those who advocate choice) holds that the imam must be knowledgeable. Second, because the imam appoints judges and governors and must be able to evaluate their rulings, he must be at least as knowledgeable as they are. Third, there is consensus that the imam may personally judge cases himself, which requires that he be qualified as a judge. The conclusion is that the imam must be a scholar.

Key Terms
إجماع
علم
قضاء
نص
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the imam only needs political shrewdness.
Correction: This lesson proves he needs scholarly competence as well, as shown by consensus and his roles.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a proof for the imam's knowledge?
A) Consensus of the community.
B) The imam appoints judges and must evaluate them.
C) The imam must be a scholar of hadith.
D) The imam may judge personally.

Key Takeaway
The imam must be a knowledgeable scholar, as proven by consensus, his oversight of judges, and his ability to judge personally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose to follow a leader—whether in your community, workplace, or family—do you examine their knowledge and competence as this passage requires, or do you follow based on other factors? Will you commit to evaluating leaders by their qualification to judge rightly?
?
The imam is held to a standard of knowledge so he can personally judge cases. In matters of justice that you face, do you rely on your own understanding or delegate to others when you could learn and act? What step will you take this week to grow in knowledge so you can judge rightly in your sphere of responsibility?
?
Consensus of the community here affirms a necessary quality of leadership. Are you part of a community that upholds knowledge as essential for its leaders, or do you settle for less? How can you actively support leaders who embody the knowledge required to judge with justice?
