The Imam Must Be Brave and Composed
Baqillani argues the imam must possess courage and emotional steadiness to execute punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the imam must possess sternness, calmness, and strength of heart to effectively carry out prescribed punishments.

Core Idea
Justice demands an imam who is fearless in executing the law.

Baqillani now turns to the personal qualities required of the imam. Beyond being just and knowledgeable, the imam must possess inner strength and composure. This passage explains why bravery and emotional steadiness are not just virtues but necessities for the one who enforces Islamic penal law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic carefully, noting the key terms: صرامة, سكون الجأش, قوة النفس.
The passage is a conditional argument: if not brave, then failure.
2
Understand the necessity
The imam must execute hudud and capital punishments. Without courage, he will shrink from his duty.
This links directly to the purpose of his office.
3
Reflect on the consequences
Baqillani warns that failure in this duty harms the very purpose for which the imam was established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه لا بد أن يكون من الصرامة وسكون الجأش وقوة النفس والقلب بحيث لا تَرُوعُه إقامة الحدود ولا يهوله ضرب الرقاب وتناول النفوس فهو أنه إذا لم يكن بهذه الصفة قصَّرِ عمـا لأجله أقيم من إقامة الحد واستخراج الحق وأضر فشله في هذا الأمر بما نُصِبَ له.
As for what indicates that he must possess sternness, calmness of demeanor, strength of soul and heart, such that the execution of prescribed punishments does not frighten him nor does striking necks and taking lives terrify him, it is that if he is not of this characteristic, he will fall short in what he was established for, namely executing the prescribed punishment and extracting the right, and his failure in this matter will harm his position.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the imam must be courageous and emotionally stable. The author argues that because the imam is responsible for implementing fixed punishments (hudud) and capital penalties, he must be someone who is not intimidated by these severe actions. If he lacks such bravery, he will fail in his duty, causing harm to the purpose of his office. The qualities required are sternness (saramah), calmness (sukun al-jash), and strength of soul and heart.

Key Terms
صرامة
جأش
قوة النفس
حدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the imam should be mild and lenient at all times.
Correction: Baqillani shows that firmness and courage are necessary to carry out prescribed punishments, without which justice fails.

Checkpoint

A) To lead prayers in times of fear.
B) To effectively carry out prescribed punishments.
C) To inspire awe in his subjects.
D) To negotiate with enemies.

Key Takeaway
The imam must be courageous and composed to execute the fixed punishments without hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson demands that even the imam must not be terrified by striking necks and taking lives. Are you willing to enforce difficult responsibilities in your own sphere—whether as a parent, leader, or friend—even when it costs you peace or reputation? What is holding you back?

The Imam Must Be Skilled in War
Baqillani proves the imam must be knowledgeable in military affairs for the protection of the state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the imam must be knowledgeable in military affairs to prevent harm to the community.

Core Idea
The imam's military expertise is essential for protecting the community.

Baqillani presents a rational argument for why the imam cannot be ignorant of military matters. He lists key responsibilities and warns of the cascading consequences of ignorance. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if he is not knowledgeable... then...'
2
Identify the responsibilities
Find the list of matters the imam must be knowledgeable about: war, army management, securing frontiers, protecting the community.
These are all specific aspects of military leadership.
3
Understand the argument
Trace the chain of consequences: ignorance leads to defects, harm to the community, enemies emboldened, internal conflict, halted rulings, and ultimately nullification of the imamate's purpose.
This is a logical argument from necessary conditions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that because of these consequences, the imam must be knowledgeable. Ask yourself: Could any of these consequences be avoided without military expertise?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على وجوب كونه عالماً بأمر الحرب وتدبير الجيوش وسد الثغور وحماية البيضة وما يتصل بذلك من الأمر، فهو أنه إذا لم يكن عالماً بذلك لحِقَ الخَلَلُ في جميعه وتعدى الضرر بجهله بذلك إلى الأمة وطمع في المسلمين عدوهم وكثر تغالبهم ووقفت أحكامهم وأدى ذلك إلى إبطال ما أقيم لأجله فوجب بذلك ما قلناه.
As for what indicates the obligation of his being knowledgeable in the matter of war, management of armies, securing frontiers, protecting the community (baydah), and what is connected to that of affairs, it is that if he is not knowledgeable in that, defect will occur in all of it, and harm due to his ignorance will extend to the community, and their enemy will covet the Muslims, and their mutual conflicts will increase, and their rulings will be halted, and that will lead to the nullification of what he was established for. So what we have said is obligatory.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage provides evidence for the imam's necessary expertise in military strategy. The author lists several related responsibilities: war, army management, fortification of borders, protection of the community (baydah). The argument is that ignorance in these matters will cause widespread harm: it will cause defects in the military system, harm the community, embolden enemies, increase internal strife, and halt the implementation of rulings. Ultimately, the purpose of the imamate would be defeated. Therefore, the imam must be a military expert.

Correction: Baqillani shows that the imam must be a military expert because ignorance leads to the collapse of the community and the failure of the imamate's purpose.

Key Takeaway

?
The purpose of the imam is protection of the community. What is the purpose of your role in your family or workplace? Are you personally prepared to fulfill it, or are you relying on others while neglecting your own growth? Commit to one specific action you will take to become more knowledgeable in that area.

The Imam Must Be the Best (Afdhal)
Baqillani uses hadith and consensus to prove the imam must be the most excellent person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Baqillani uses hadith and consensus to prove the imam must be the most excellent person.

Core Idea
Leadership belongs to the most excellent.

Baqillani now turns to the criteria for imamate. He argues that the imam must be the best (afdhal) person in the community, drawing on prophetic traditions and early Muslim consensus. This lesson examines his proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the multiple hadith quotes and the reference to consensus.
2
Identify the hadith evidence
Baqillani cites several hadith with different wordings all commanding precedence of the best. Note the key phrases like 'يؤم القوم أفضلهم' and 'أئمتكم شفعاؤكم'.
The hadith are considered mutawatir in meaning.
3
Understand the consensus argument
Baqillani invokes the practice of the early community, especially the Shura council, and Abd al-Rahman's statement about Uthman. This shows they sought the best.
The supreme imamate includes leading prayer, so the imam must be the best.
4
Consider the exception clause
Note the condition 'as long as there is no obstacle' (متى ما لم يكن هناك عارض). Reflect on what obstacles might justify not appointing the best.
This shows the rule is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه يجب أن يكون أفضلهم متى ما لم يكن هناك عارض يمنع من إقامة الأفضل فالأخبار المتظاهرة عن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم في وجوب تقدِمَةِ الأفضل ومنها قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «يوم القومَ أفضلهم»، وقوله: «أئمتـكـم شفعاؤكم ، فانظروا بمن تستشفعون ، وقوله في خبر آخر: «أئمتكم شفعاؤكم إلى الله، فقدموا خيركم وقوله: «من تقدم على قوم من المسلمين، يرى أن فيهم من هو أفضل منه فقد خان الله ورسوله والمسلمين في أمثال هذه الأخبار مما قد تواترت على المعنى وإن اختلفت ألفاظها . وقد اتفق المسلمون على أن أعظم الإمامة الإمامة الكبرى وأن إمام الأمة الأعظم له أن يتقدم في الصلاة فيجب لأجل ذلك أجمع أن يكون أفضلهم . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً إجماع الأمة في الصدر الأول على طلب الأفضل وتمثيلهم بين أهل الشورى وقول عبد الرحمن : «لم أرهم يعدلون بعثمان
As for what indicates that he must be the best among them, as long as there is no obstacle preventing the establishment of the best, there are abundant reports from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, on the obligation of giving precedence to the best. Among them is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Lead the people, the best of them.' And his saying: 'Your imams are your intercessors, so look to whom you seek intercession.' And his saying in another report: 'Your imams are your intercessors to God, so put forward your best.' And his saying: 'Whoever takes precedence over a group of Muslims while knowing that among them is someone better than him has betrayed God, His Messenger, and the Muslims.' And similar reports that have reached tawatur in meaning although their wording differs. And the Muslims have agreed that the greatest imamate is the supreme imamate, and that the greatest imam of the community has the right to lead the prayer, so for all that, he must be the best among them. And this is also indicated by the consensus of the community in the early period on seeking the best and their deliberation among the people of the shura (consultative council), and the statement of Abd al-Rahman: 'I did not see them equating anyone with Uthman...'
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents multiple proofs that the imam must be the most excellent (afdhal) in the community. The author cites several hadith from the Prophet that command giving precedence to the best in leadership, especially in prayer. The principle of the greater imamate includes leading prayer, so the imam must be the best. Additionally, the author invokes the consensus of the early community, exemplified by the Shura council after Umar's death, where they sought the best candidate, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's statement that they saw no one equal to Uthman. This reinforces that the imam should be afdhal, unless there is a valid obstacle preventing that.

Key Terms
أفضل
إمامة
شفاعة
إجماع
شورى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Political expediency overrides merit in choosing an imam.
Correction: The hadith and consensus show that the best person must be prioritized unless a valid obstacle prevents it.

Checkpoint
Which hadith does Baqillani use to support the imam being the best?
A) 'The best of you are those who learn the Quran.'
B) 'Lead the people, the best of them.'
C) 'The ruler is a shield.'
D) 'Obey your leaders even if they punish you.'

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that the imam must be the most excellent, supported by hadith and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you accepted a leadership role or a position of influence? Did you honestly assess whether someone else was more excellent in knowledge, character, or piety? How does the Prophet's warning about betraying God and His Messenger weigh on your conscience in that situation?
?
Think of someone you follow or admire. Is your admiration based on their worldly success or their closeness to Allah? If you were to seek the 'most excellent' among your acquaintances, who would it be and what would it cost you to acknowledge them?
?
The early community deliberated and sought the best even at the highest level of leadership. In your own circles—family, work, or community—do you actively seek out and promote the most excellent people, or do you prefer those who are convenient or agreeable? What step can you take this week to put the best person forward, even if it means stepping back?

Prophetic Injunctions on Preferring the Most Excellent
Baqillani cites Prophetic hadiths commanding precedence of the most excellent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the textual evidence from the Prophet's hadiths that obligate giving precedence to the most excellent in leadership positions.

Core Idea
The Prophet's commands demand the most excellent be placed in authority.

Imam Baqillani begins his argument for preferring the afḍal (most excellent) imam by citing multiple hadiths from the Prophet ﷺ. These narrations, though varying in wording, consistently command that the best person be put forward for leadership. Understanding this textual basis is essential before considering other evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths
Read the four hadiths cited by Baqillani. Notice how each one commands giving precedence to the best.
2
Observe the multi-transmission
Baqillani notes that these reports are mutawātir (massively transmitted) in meaning, even if wordings differ. This strengthens the evidence.
3
Connect to supreme imamate
These general commands apply to all leadership, but especially to the supreme imamate (imāma kubrā). This will be expanded in the next section.
The next part of the source discusses consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عارض يمنع من إقامة الأفضل فالأخبار المتظاهرة عن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم في وجوب تقدِمَةِ الأفضل ومنها قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «يوم القومَ أفضلهم»، وقوله: «أئمتـكـم شفعاؤكم ، فانظروا بمن تستشفعون ، وقوله في خبر آخر: «أئمتكم شفعاؤكم إلى الله، فقدموا خيركم وقوله: «من تقدم على قوم من المسلمين، يرى أن فيهم من هو أفضل منه فقد خان الله ورسوله والمسلمين في أمثال هذه الأخبار مما قد تواترت على المعنى وإن اختلفت ألفاظها . وقد اتفق المسلمون على أن أعظم الإمامة الإمامة الكبرى وأن إمام الأمة الأعظم له أن يتقدم في الصلاة فيجب لأجل ذلك أجمع أن يكون أفضلهم . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً إجماع الأمة في الصدر الأول على طلب الأفضل وتمثيلهم بين أهل الشورى وقول عبد الرحمن : «لم أرهم يعدلون بعثمان أحداً» ، وقول أبي عبيدة لعمر حين قال: «مُدَّ يدك أبايع لك»، «أتقول هذا وأبو بكر حاضر؟ والله ما كان لك في الإسلام فَهة غيرها، وترك الكافة الإنكار عليه، وقبولُ عُمَر لهذا منه وإضرابه عن مراجعته وإنما استجاز عُمَرُ قبول ذلك خشية الفتنة وأن لا تستقيم الأمة على أفضلها ولذلك قال: «وَقَى اللَّهُ شرها».
An obstacle prevents establishing the most excellent. The numerous reports from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, about the obligation of giving precedence to the most excellent include his saying: 'Appoint as leader of the people the most excellent among them,' and his saying: 'Your leaders are your intercessors, so see whom you take as intercessors,' and his saying in another report: 'Your leaders are your intercessors to God, so put forward the best of you,' and his saying: 'Whoever takes precedence over a group of Muslims while knowing that among them is one better than him has betrayed God, His Messenger, and the Muslims.' And similar reports are widespread in meaning even if their wordings differ. The Muslims have agreed that the greatest imamate is the supreme imamate, and that the supreme imam of the community is entitled to lead in prayer; therefore it is necessary that he be the most excellent among them. Also indicating this is the consensus of the community in the first generation on seeking the most excellent, and their example among the people of the Shura, and the saying of 'Abd al-Rahman: 'I have not seen them consider anyone equal to 'Uthman,' and the saying of Abu 'Ubayda to 'Umar when he said: 'Stretch out your hand that I may pledge allegiance to you,' 'Do you say this while Abu Bakr is present? By God, you had no (flaw of) foolishness in Islam other than this,' and the general abandonment of disapproval of him, and 'Umar's acceptance of this from him and refraining from replying; rather 'Umar accepted that for fear of discord (fitna) and that the community might not remain firm upon its most excellent, and therefore he said: 'May God protect us from its evil.'
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the requirement that the imam (leader) be the most excellent (afḍal) among the community. Baqillani begins by citing multiple Prophetic hadiths that explicitly command giving precedence to the best person. He notes that although the wordings differ, these reports are widely transmitted (mutawātir) in meaning. He then argues that the Muslims have agreed (ijmāʿ) that the greatest imamate (al-imāma al-kubrā) is the supreme leadership, and that such an imam leads prayer; thus he must be the most excellent. As further evidence, he recounts the consensus of the early community in seeking the most excellent during the shura (consultative council) after 'Umar. He mentions that 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf stated that they saw no one equal to 'Uthman. Then he narrates the incident when 'Umar offered to pledge allegiance to Abu 'Ubayda, but Abu 'Ubayda rebuked him, saying 'Do you say this while Abu Bakr is present?' and accused 'Umar of a lapse ('faha'). The fact that none of the Companions objected to this rebuke, and 'Umar himself accepted it without reply, indicates their agreement that the most excellent must be preferred. However, Baqillani notes that 'Umar only accepted this to avoid fitna (discord) and to ensure the community would remain upon its most excellent leader, showing that necessity can override the strict requirement.

Key Terms
الأفضل
الإمامة الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the multiple reports from the Prophet regarding preferring the most excellent?
A) They are weak and unreliable
B) They are widespread in meaning (mutawātir) even if wordings differ
C) They are only about prayer, not leadership
D) They are all authentic

Key Takeaway
Multiple Prophetic hadiths, transmitted widely, command that the most excellent person be given precedence in leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever knowingly allowed someone less qualified to lead you in a prayer or community affair because it was more convenient, while knowing someone more excellent was available? What does the Prophet's warning about betrayal mean for you today?
?
When you are in a position to choose a leader—whether in a study circle, a committee, or a community—do you genuinely seek the most excellent person, or do you prioritize personal comfort, familiarity, or status? How can you change that starting this week?
?
Consider the religious teachers or leaders you look up to: are they truly the most excellent in character and knowledge? If not, what responsibility do you have to seek out better examples or to become the most excellent yourself?

Consensus and the Exception of Necessity
Baqillani uses consensus and historical examples to argue for preferring the most excellent, with a concession for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how consensus of the early community and the Shura incident support preferring the most excellent, and how necessity (fitna) can override this requirement.

Core Idea
Consensus requires the most excellent, but necessity may permit deviation.

After presenting hadith evidence, Baqillani turns to consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Muslim community, particularly the first generation's practice during the Shura (consultative council) after ʿUmar. He also recounts the incident between ʿUmar and Abū ʿUbayda, which illustrates that while the most excellent is preferred, fear of discord (fitna) can sometimes necessitate a different choice. Understanding this nuance is crucial for a complete picture of Islamic leadership doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the consensus argument
Baqillani claims that Muslims have agreed that the supreme imam must be the most excellent because he leads prayer. Note how he derives the requirement from the practice of the early community.
2
Examine the Shura example
He cites the saying of ʿAbd al-Raḥmān that no one was considered equal to ʿUthmān, showing they sought the best. Also note the statement of Abū ʿUbayda to ʿUmar.
3
Understand the fitna exception
Baqillani explains that ʿUmar accepted Abū ʿUbayda's rebuke and did not insist on his own pledge because he feared fitna (discord) and wanted the community to remain upon its most excellent. This shows that necessity can override the strict requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

An obstacle prevents establishing the most excellent. The numerous reports from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, about the obligation of giving precedence to the most excellent include his saying: 'Appoint as leader of the people the most excellent among them,' and his saying: 'Your leaders are your intercessors, so see whom you take as intercessors,' and his saying in another report: 'Your leaders are your intercessors to God, so put forward the best of you,' and his saying: 'Whoever takes precedence over a group of Muslims while knowing that among them is one better than him has betrayed God, His Messenger, and the Muslims.' And similar reports are widespread in meaning even if their wordings differ. Also indicating this is the consensus of the community in the first generation on seeking the most excellent, and their example among the people of the Shura, and the saying of 'Abd al-Rahman: 'I have not seen them consider anyone equal to 'Uthman,' and the saying of Abu 'Ubayda to 'Umar when he said: 'Stretch out your hand that I may pledge allegiance to you,' 'Do you say this while Abu Bakr is present? Commentary
This passage is a proof for the requirement that the imam (leader) be the most excellent (afḍal) among the community. Baqillani begins by citing multiple Prophetic hadiths that explicitly command giving precedence to the best person. He notes that although the wordings differ, these reports are widely transmitted (mutawātir) in meaning. However, Baqillani notes that 'Umar only accepted this to avoid fitna (discord) and to ensure the community would remain upon its most excellent leader, showing that necessity can override the strict requirement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The requirement for the most excellent imam is absolute and cannot be overridden under any circumstance.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'Umar accepted a less excellent option to avoid fitna, indicating that necessity can permit deviation from the strict requirement.

A) He insisted on his pledge
B) He accepted the rebuke and refrained from replying due to fear of fitna
C) He argued that Abu Bakr was not present
D) He asked for a different candidate

Key Takeaway
Consensus and early practice require the most excellent imam, but necessity (like fear of fitna) can allow deviation to preserve unity.

?

?

?

When a Less Excellent Imam is Permissible
Baqillani permits a less excellent imam to avoid fitna, citing Umar's shura.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a less excellent (al-mafḍūl) imam is permissible when appointing the most excellent (al-afḍal) would lead to fitna and disorder, supported by the precedent of Umar's shura.

Core Idea
Avoiding greater harm justifies appointing a lesser imam.

Baqillani addresses a critical question: is it necessary to always appoint the most qualified person as imam? Drawing on the purpose of the imamate and the practical example of the Companions after Umar, he shows that preserving unity and preventing corruption can override the preference for the excellent. This lesson explores the reasoning and evidence for this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on purpose of imamate
Begin by reading the Arabic passage where Baqillani enumerates the duties of the imam: to repel enemies, protect the sanctuary, implement hudud, etc. Then read the translation.
Notice that the imam's role is functional, not merely ceremonial.
2
Identify the condition for deviation
Pay attention to the clause starting with 'فإذا خيف' (so if one fears). List the negative consequences that permit deviating from the most excellent. Understand that these are collective harms.
The phrase 'اختلاف السيوف' (conflict of swords) is a strong image of civil war.
3
Examine the shura precedent
Read Baqillani's reference to Umar's six-member council. Note that both excellent and less excellent were present, and Umar allowed any of them to be chosen if it led to unity and reform.
This historical example is used as tacit consensus (sukutiyya ijma).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على جواز العقد للمفضول وترك الأفضل لخوف الفتنة والتهارُج فهو أن الإمام إنما يُنْصَبُ لدفع العدو وحماية البيضة وسد الخلل وإقامة الحدود واستخراج الحقوق فإذا خيف بإقامة أفضلهم الهَرْجُ والفساد والتغالب وترك الطاعة واختلاف السيوف وتعطيل الأحكام والحقوق وطمع عدو المسلمين في اهتضامهم وتوهين أمرهم صار ذلك عذراً واضحاً في العدول عن الفاضل إلى المفضول ويدل على ذلك عِلْمُ عمر رضي الله عنه وسائر الصحابة والأمة بأن في الستة فاضلاً ومفضولاً وقد أجاز العقد لكل واحد منهم إذا أدى إلى صلاحهم وجَمْع كلمتهم من غير إنكار أحد عليه ذلك فثبت أيضاً ما قلناه . وأما ما يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون من بني هاشم دون غيرها من قبائل قريش فهو أن ظاهر الخبر لا يقتضي ذلك، ولا العقل يوجبه ؛ وظاهر قوله صلّى الله عليه: «الأئمة من قريش يوجب كونها شائعة في سائرهم. فإن قال قائل : هلا قلتم إنها تجوز في موالي قريش لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : موالي القوم منهم؟ قيل له : هذا إنما قاله مجازاً واتساعاً وتألفاً للموالي
As for what indicates the permissibility of contracting [the imamate] for the less excellent (al-mafḍūl) and leaving the most excellent for fear of discord (fitna) and turmoil (al-tahāruj), it is that the imam is only appointed to repel the enemy, protect the sanctuary, fill the gaps, establish legal punishments (ḥudūd), and extract rights. So if by establishing the most excellent among them one fears turmoil, corruption, domination, abandonment of obedience, conflict of swords, suspension of rulings and rights, and the enemy of the Muslims coveting their violation and weakening their affair, then this becomes a clear excuse for deviating from the excellent (al-fāḍil) to the less excellent. This is indicated by the knowledge of 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, and all the Companions and the community that among the six [of the shura] there was an excellent and a less excellent, yet he permitted the contract for each one of them if it led to their well-being and the unification of their word, without anyone disapproving of that. So also what we have said is established. As for what indicates that [the imam] does not have to be from Banu Hashim to the exclusion of other tribes of Quraysh, it is that the apparent meaning of the report does not require that, nor does reason necessitate it. The apparent meaning of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The imams are from Quraysh' requires that it be common among all of them. If someone says: 'Why do you not say that it is permissible among the clients (mawālī) of Quraysh, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: The clients of a people are among them?' It is said to him: 'This he said metaphorically, expansively, and to win over the clients.'
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument and refutation. First, Baqillani provides the evidence for the permissibility of appointing a less excellent (mafḍūl) imam when appointing the most excellent would cause fitna and turmoil. He states the purpose of the imamate: repelling enemies, protecting, maintaining order, implementing ḥudūd, and securing rights. If appointing the best leads to chaos, the less excellent may be chosen. He cites the precedent of the six-member shura (including Uthman, Ali, etc.), where both excellent and less excellent were present, yet all were considered eligible for the covenant if it served unity and reform. Since Umar and the Companions allowed that without objection, it establishes the ruling. Second, Baqillani argues that the imam must be from Quraysh but not specifically from Banu Hashim. He states that the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' implies a general inclusion of all Qurayshi tribes. Reason does not require limitation to one clan. He then addresses an objection: why not extend the imamate to the clients (mawālī) of Quraysh, based on the prophetic saying 'The clients of a people are among them'? Baqillani replies that this saying is metaphorical and for winning hearts, not a legal ruling on eligibility for the imamate.

Key Terms
المفضول
الفتنة
الهرج
الستة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The most excellent person must always be appointed as imam, regardless of consequences.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if appointing the most excellent causes societal harm (fitna), it is permissible—and even necessary—to appoint a less excellent person to prevent greater evil.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which of the following is NOT a valid reason to appoint a less excellent imam?
A) Fear of fitna and turmoil
B) Fear of the enemy of Muslims coveting their violation
C) The most excellent person is not from Quraysh
D) The fear of conflict of swords

Key Takeaway
The imamate is a practical office, and avoiding greater harm can override the preference for the most excellent candidate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on the 'most excellent' option in a decision, despite knowing it would cause division or harm to those around you? Are you willing to set aside your ideal for the sake of unity and avoiding greater evil?
?
Do you trust the collective wisdom of the community (like Umar and the Companions) over your own preference for the 'best' candidate? What would it cost you to submit to that wisdom?
?
If your insistence on the 'excellent' leads to chaos or the weakening of the Muslim community, what does that say about your priorities? Will you choose your standard or the well-being of the ummah?

The Imam Must Be from Quraysh, Not Banu Hashim Alone
Baqillani argues imam must be from Quraysh generally, not Banu Hashim specifically, and rejects extending to clients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the imam must be from Quraysh but not specifically from Banu Hashim, and that the hadith about clients is metaphorical, not a legal basis for imamate.

Core Idea
Quraysh includes all tribes for imamate, excluding Banu Hashim exclusivity.

Baqillani refutes the claim that the imam must be from the Banu Hashim clan, a view held by some Shi'a groups. He argues from the literal meaning of the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' that it applies to all Quraysh, not just one branch. Then he addresses a potential objection that might extend the imamate to clients (mawali) of Quraysh, explaining that the relevant hadith is metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Quraysh condition
Read the Arabic passage where Baqillani denies the necessity of Banu Hashim lineage. Note his use of 'zāhir al-khabar' and 'al-aql' as sources.
Baqillani uses both textual and rational arguments.
2
Understand the objection about clients
Read the hypothetical objection: why not include mawali of Quraysh based on the hadith 'Mawali al-qawm minhum'? Then read Baqillani's response.
The objection is from those who might expand Quraysh status too broadly.
3
Analyze the metaphorical response
Baqillani says the Prophet's statement about clients was metaphorical (majāzan) and meant to win hearts (ta'alluf), not to give legal equality. Reflect on how this distinguishes between metaphorical and legal statements.
This is an important methodological point in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على جواز العقد للمفضول وترك الأفضل لخوف الفتنة والتهارُج فهو أن الإمام إنما يُنْصَبُ لدفع العدو وحماية البيضة وسد الخلل وإقامة الحدود واستخراج الحقوق فإذا خيف بإقامة أفضلهم الهَرْجُ والفساد والتغالب وترك الطاعة واختلاف السيوف وتعطيل الأحكام والحقوق وطمع عدو المسلمين في اهتضامهم وتوهين أمرهم صار ذلك عذراً واضحاً في العدول عن الفاضل إلى المفضول ويدل على ذلك عِلْمُ عمر رضي الله عنه وسائر الصحابة والأمة بأن في الستة فاضلاً ومفضولاً وقد أجاز العقد لكل واحد منهم إذا أدى إلى صلاحهم وجَمْع كلمتهم من غير إنكار أحد عليه ذلك فثبت أيضاً ما قلناه . وأما ما يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون من بني هاشم دون غيرها من قبائل قريش فهو أن ظاهر الخبر لا يقتضي ذلك، ولا العقل يوجبه ؛ وظاهر قوله صلّى الله عليه: «الأئمة من قريش يوجب كونها شائعة في سائرهم. فإن قال قائل : هلا قلتم إنها تجوز في موالي قريش لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : موالي القوم منهم؟ قيل له : هذا إنما قاله مجازاً واتساعاً وتألفاً للموالي
As for what indicates the permissibility of contracting [the imamate] for the less excellent (al-mafḍūl) and leaving the most excellent for fear of discord (fitna) and turmoil (al-tahāruj), it is that the imam is only appointed to repel the enemy, protect the sanctuary, fill the gaps, establish legal punishments (ḥudūd), and extract rights. So if by establishing the most excellent among them one fears turmoil, corruption, domination, abandonment of obedience, conflict of swords, suspension of rulings and rights, and the enemy of the Muslims coveting their violation and weakening their affair, then this becomes a clear excuse for deviating from the excellent (al-fāḍil) to the less excellent. This is indicated by the knowledge of 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, and all the Companions and the community that among the six [of the shura] there was an excellent and a less excellent, yet he permitted the contract for each one of them if it led to their well-being and the unification of their word, without anyone disapproving of that. So also what we have said is established. As for what indicates that [the imam] does not have to be from Banu Hashim to the exclusion of other tribes of Quraysh, it is that the apparent meaning of the report does not require that, nor does reason necessitate it. The apparent meaning of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The imams are from Quraysh' requires that it be common among all of them. If someone says: 'Why do you not say that it is permissible among the clients (mawālī) of Quraysh, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: The clients of a people are among them?' It is said to him: 'This he said metaphorically, expansively, and to win over the clients.'
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument and refutation. First, Baqillani provides the evidence for the permissibility of appointing a less excellent (mafḍūl) imam when appointing the most excellent would cause fitna and turmoil. He states the purpose of the imamate: repelling enemies, protecting, maintaining order, implementing ḥudūd, and securing rights. If appointing the best leads to chaos, the less excellent may be chosen. He cites the precedent of the six-member shura (including Uthman, Ali, etc.), where both excellent and less excellent were present, yet all were considered eligible for the covenant if it served unity and reform. Since Umar and the Companions allowed that without objection, it establishes the ruling. Second, Baqillani argues that the imam must be from Quraysh but not specifically from Banu Hashim. He states that the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' implies a general inclusion of all Qurayshi tribes. Reason does not require limitation to one clan. He then addresses an objection: why not extend the imamate to the clients (mawālī) of Quraysh, based on the prophetic saying 'The clients of a people are among them'? Baqillani replies that this saying is metaphorical and for winning hearts, not a legal ruling on eligibility for the imamate.

Key Terms
قريش
بني هاشم
الموالي
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imam must be from Banu Hashim (the Prophet's clan) to the exclusion of other Quraysh.
Correction: Baqillani argues from the hadith's general wording that all Quraysh are eligible, and reason does not require Banu Hashim exclusivity.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the objection that clients (mawali) of Quraysh can be imams?
A) Because the Prophet never said that
B) Because the saying 'Mawali al-qawm minhum' is metaphorical, not legal
C) Because clients are not Muslims
D) Because clients are not from Quraysh

Key Takeaway
The imam must be from Quraysh but not necessarily from Banu Hashim; clients cannot be imams because the relevant hadith is metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith or an Islamic text, do you instinctively take it literally or do you pause to consider its context, purpose, and possible metaphorical meanings? How might this lesson change the way you approach prophetic statements?
?
Baqillani dismissed a well-known hadith about clients being 'among' the people because it was said for 'winning hearts,' not as a permanent ruling. Is there a teaching you have held onto that might have been said for a specific situation or purpose, rather than as a universal command? Will you re-examine it today?
?
This passage deals with lineage and privilege in leadership. Look at your own life: do you ever assume that certain people are 'naturally' more qualified for leadership based on their family, tribe, or social status? What would it cost you to judge by character and qualification instead?

Appointing a Less Virtuous Imam to Avoid Discord
Proof of permissibility to appoint a less virtuous imam for fear of fitna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale and evidence for the permissibility of appointing a less virtuous imam when the most virtuous would lead to fitna.

Core Idea
The imam's primary purpose is unity; avoiding fitna can justify choosing a less virtuous leader.

This passage from Imam al-Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il presents a key principle of Islamic political theology: the appointment of an imam is for practical purposes like defense and justice. When the most virtuous candidate would cause division and strife, it becomes permissible to choose a less virtuous one. Al-Baqillani supports this with the precedent of Caliph 'Umar's shura council.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the reference to historical precedent.
This passage is a proof (dalil) structured in two parts: rational justification and textual evidence.
2
Identify the purpose of the imam
Notice how al-Baqillani begins by stating the objectives of the imamate: defense, protection, enforcement of laws, and upholding rights. This sets the foundation for the argument.
3
Understand the condition for turning to the less virtuous
The passage explains that if appointing the most virtuous would cause turmoil, corruption, and vulnerability to enemies, then it is a clear excuse to choose a less virtuous candidate. This is a rational weighing of benefits and harms.
This shows that the primary goal is preserving the community's well-being, not merely virtue maximization.
4
Examine the evidence from 'Umar
Al-Baqillani cites the knowledge of 'Umar and the Companions that among the six candidates there were virtuous and less virtuous individuals, yet any of them could be chosen if it brought unity. No one objected, establishing consensus.
This is a historical precedent that reinforces the rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على جواز العقد للمفضول وترك الأفضل لخوف الفتنة والتهارُج فهو أن الإمام إنما يُنْصَبُ لدفع العدو وحماية البيضة وسد الخلل وإقامة الحدود واستخراج الحقوق فإذا خيف بإقامة أفضلهم الهَرْجُ والفساد والتغالب وترك الطاعة واختلاف السيوف وتعطيل الأحكام والحقوق وطمع عدو المسلمين في اهتضامهم وتوهين أمرهم صار ذلك عذراً واضحاً في العدول عن الفاضل إلى المفضول ويدل على ذلك عِلْمُ عمر رضي الله عنه وسائر الصحابة والأمة بأن في الستة فاضلاً ومفضولاً وقد أجاز العقد لكل واحد منهم إذا أدى إلى صلاحهم وجَمْع كلمتهم من غير إنكار أحد عليه ذلك فثبت أيضاً ما قلناه.
As for what indicates the permissibility of contracting (al-‘aqd) for the less virtuous and abandoning the more virtuous out of fear of discord (fitna) and turmoil (al-tahāruj), it is that the imam is only appointed to repel the enemy, protect the community (al-bayḍa), fill gaps, establish penalties (ḥudūd), and extract rights. If it is feared that appointing the best of them would cause turmoil (al-harj), corruption, mutual overpowering, abandonment of obedience, clash of swords, suspension of rulings and rights, and greed of the enemy of the Muslims to humble them and weaken their affair, then that becomes a clear excuse to turn away from the virtuous to the less virtuous. That is evidenced by the knowledge of ‘Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, and the rest of the Companions and the nation that among the six there are virtuous and less virtuous, and yet he permitted the contract for each of them if it led to their well-being and uniting their word, without anyone disapproving of that. Thus what we have said is also established.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) demonstrating the permissibility of appointing a less virtuous imam when necessary to avoid fitna. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, begins by stating the purpose of appointing an imam: to defend against enemies, protect the community, maintain order, enforce penal laws, and uphold rights. If appointing the most virtuous candidate would instead cause turmoil, corruption, strife, and vulnerability to external enemies, then it is a clear justification to choose a less virtuous person. The author then supports this with the precedent of Caliph ‘Umar, who knew that among the six candidates for the caliphate (the shūrā council) there were both more virtuous and less virtuous individuals, yet he permitted any of them to be chosen if it would unify the community. No Companion objected to this reasoning. Thus, the conclusion that appointing a less virtuous imam is permissible under necessity is firmly established.

Key Terms

الفتنة
التهارُج
الهَرْج
البيضة
الستة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The most virtuous person must always be chosen for the imamate regardless of consequences.
Correction: The passage shows that if appointing the most virtuous would cause widespread discord, it is permissible and even necessary to choose a less virtuous candidate to preserve unity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, what is a valid excuse for appointing a less virtuous imam over a more virtuous one?
A) The less virtuous candidate is wealthier.
B) Appointing the more virtuous would cause discord and weaken the community.
C) The more virtuous candidate declines the position.
D) The less virtuous candidate is more knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of appointing a less virtuous imam is grounded in the imamate's purpose of preserving unity and avoiding greater harm, as demonstrated by rational argument and the precedent of 'Umar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you insist on getting your way because you believe you have the best opinion, have you considered whether it might cause division among Muslims? What would it take for you to step back for the sake of unity, even if you are right?
?
Think of a situation where you or your community chose a leader who was not the most qualified, but it helped avoid conflict. How did that choice align with the purpose of the imamate as described in this lesson? What does that reveal about your priorities?
?
If you were among the shura council today, and the most virtuous candidate would cause fitna, would you have the courage to choose a less virtuous one for the sake of the community? What fears hold you back from making such a decision?

Imam Need Not Be from Banu Hashim Specifically
Proof that the imam need not be from Banu Hashim but must be from Quraysh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the requirement for the imam to be from Quraysh does not necessitate being from Banu Hashim specifically, based on the apparent meaning of the hadith and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
The imam must be from Quraysh, but not necessarily from Banu Hashim.

In this passage, al-Baqillani addresses a common sectarian claim that the imam must be from the Prophet’s clan, Banu Hashim. He presents a clear argument using the apparent meaning of the Prophetic hadith and rational reasoning. He also responds to an objection regarding non-Arab clients of Quraysh. This lesson unpacks his proof and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the main claim and the two key pieces of evidence.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Al-Baqillani uses the apparent meaning of the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and the absence of a rational requirement for restriction. Identify how each supports his conclusion.
The hadith is considered a primary source, and reason is used to confirm that no exclusive requirement exists.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised that mawali (clients) might be included based on another hadith. Note how al-Baqillani refutes this by distinguishing figurative from literal speech.
This shows the importance of interpreting hadith in context and according to legal principles.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in Islamic political theology. How does it affect the qualifications for leadership?
This argument reinforces the Quraysh condition while rejecting narrower clan-based claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون من بني هاشم دون غيرها من قبائل قريش فهو أن ظاهر الخبر لا يقتضي ذلك، ولا العقل يوجبه ؛ وظاهر قوله صلّى الله عليه: «الأئمة من قريش» يوجب كونها شائعة في سائرهم. فإن قال قائل : هلا قلتم إنها تجوز في موالي قريش لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : موالي القوم منهم؟ قيل له : هذا إنما قاله مجازاً واتساعاً وتألفاً للموالي وإكراماً لهم ومُطْلَقُ قوله من قريش يوجب أن يكون من الصميم دون الموالي.
As for what indicates that [the imam] must not be from the Banu Hashim to the exclusion of other tribes of Quraysh, it is that the apparent meaning of the report (al-khabar) does not necessitate that, nor does reason require it. The apparent meaning of his statement, peace be upon him, 'The imams are from Quraysh' necessitates that [the imamate] is widespread among all of them. If someone says, 'Why do you not say that it is permissible among the mawālī (clients) of Quraysh, due to the Prophet's statement, peace be upon him, 'The mawālī of a people are from them'?' It is said to him: This was only said figuratively, expansively, to win over the mawālī and honor them. The unqualified statement 'from Quraysh' necessitates being from the pure stock (al-ṣamīm), not the mawālī.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the view that the imam must be from the Banu Hashim clan specifically. Al-Baqillani argues that the apparent meaning of the Prophetic hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' does not restrict the imamate to one clan; rather it applies to all Quraysh tribes. Reason also does not demand such a restriction. Then he addresses an objection: Some might argue that the mawālī (non-Arab clients of Quraysh) could be included, given another hadith 'The mawālī of a people are from them.' He refutes this by explaining that the latter hadith is meant figuratively and as a gesture of honor, not as a legal inclusion. The unqualified phrase 'from Quraysh' means from the biological lineage (al-ṣamīm), not the clients. Thus, the imam must be a Qurayshī by descent, but not necessarily from Banu Hashim.

Key Terms
بني هاشم
قريش
الخبر
الموالي
الصميم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the imam must be from Banu Hashim specifically.
Correction: This passage shows it is sufficient to be from Quraysh in general; Banu Hashim is not required.

Checkpoint
What does al-Baqillani conclude regarding the requirement for an imam to be from Banu Hashim?
A) It is obligatory.
B) It is recommended but not obligatory.
C) It is not required; being from Quraysh is sufficient.
D) It is not required even if not from Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
The imam must be from Quraysh lineage, but not necessarily from Banu Hashim, and mawali are not included because the relevant hadith is figurative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you harbor a hidden bias that only those from a particular family or lineage are truly fit to lead you, even if they lack the qualities of justice and knowledge? This lesson calls you to submit to the revealed condition of Quraysh without adding your own preferences. Will you repent from any factionalism that divides the Ummah?
?
The Prophet's words about mawali being 'from them' were a kindness, not a legal inclusion. In your own life, do you use kind words to include others while truly keeping them at a distance? Reflect on whether your inclusivity is genuine or merely figurative. What will you change today to truly welcome those who are different from you?

Why the Imam Need Not Be Infallible
Proof that the imam is not required to be infallible or all-knowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the imam's role is limited to implementing revealed laws and that fallibility does not disqualify him, as shown by the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Core Idea
The imam is a deputy of the community, not a sinless prophet.

Al-Baqillani addresses the theological claim that the imam must be infallible and omniscient. He argues that the imam's function is to apply already-established rulings, and he is merely a representative of the community. This passage presents both logical analogies and historical examples from the early caliphs to refute the requirement of infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Baqillani defines the imam's role in the first sentence.
The imam is not a prophet but a deputy.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core reasoning: the imam is appointed to implement already-known laws, and the community supervises him. Therefore, infallibility is unnecessary.
The analogy with other officials reinforces this.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objector says the imam appoints deputies, so he must be infallible. Al-Baqillani responds by analogy: those deputies also appoint deputies, leading to infinite regress.
This exposes the flaw in the objection.
4
Review the caliphs' statements
Read the quotes from Abu Bakr, ‘Umar, and ‘Uthman. They openly admit fallibility. Note that the community did not object.
These are empirical proofs from the best generation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument shapes Sunni political theology: the imam is accountable to the community and can be removed if needed.
Infallibility is not a requirement for leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون معصوماً عالماً بالغيب ولا بجميع لا يَشِذَّ عليه منه شيء فهو أن الإمام إنما يُنصَبُ لإقامة الأحكام وحدود وأمور قد شرعها الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تقدم علم الأمة بها وهو في جميع ما يتولاه وكيل للأمة ونائب عنها وهي من ورائه في تسديده وتقويمه وإذكاره وتنبيهه وأخذ الحق منه إذا وجب عليه وخلعه والاستبدال به متى اقترف ما يوجب خلعه فليس يحتاج مع ذلك إلى أن يكون معصوماً كما لا يحتاج أميره وقاضيه وجابي خَرَاجه وصدقاته وأصحاب مسائله وحرسه إلى أن يكونوا معصومين وهو فليس يلي بنفسه شيئاً أكثر مما يليه خلفاؤه من الأمور. فإن قالوا : فهو المُولّي لخلفائه فيجب أن يكون لذلك معصوماً من الخطأ، قيل لهم : وكذلك أمراؤه وقضاته وعُمَّال خراجه يُوَلُّونَ خلفائهم، فيجب أن يكونوا لذلك معصومين . ويدل على هذا اعترافُ الخلفاء الراشدين بأنهم غير معصومين وترك إنكار الأمة أو واحد منهم تولى الأمر مع اعترافهم بنفي العصمة عنهم . هذا أبو بكر يقول: «أطيعوني ما أطعت الله ؛ فإذا عصيت الله فلا طاعة لي عليكم» إلى قوله : «لا أوثِرُ في أشعاركم وأبشاركُم» ؛ وهذا عمر يقول : «امرءاً أهدى إلينا عيوبنا» و «لولا على هلك عمر» و «لولا معاذ لهلك عمر» ؛ وهذا عثمان يقول : «أحلتهما آية وحرمتهما آية» يعني في الأختين
As for what indicates that it is not necessary for [the imam] to be infallible (ma‘ṣūm), knowledgeable of the unseen (al-ghayb), nor [knowledgeable] of everything from which nothing escapes him, it is that the imam is only appointed to establish the rulings (al-aḥkām) and limits (ḥudūd) and matters that the Messenger, peace be upon him, has legislated, and the knowledge of the nation (al-umma) has preceded him [in knowing] them. In all that he undertakes, he is a deputy (wakīl) of the nation and a representative (nā’ib) from it, and they are behind him in directing him, correcting him, reminding him, alerting him, taking rights from him if they are due, deposing him, and replacing him whenever he commits what necessitates his removal. So he does not need, despite that, to be infallible, just as his governor, judge, tax collector, alms collector, administrators of his inquiries, and guards do not need to be infallible. He does not handle by himself anything more than what his deputies handle. If they say: 'He is the one who appoints his deputies, so he must thus be infallible from error,' it is said to them: Likewise, his governors, judges, and tax collectors appoint their own deputies, so they would also have to be infallible. This is also supported by the acknowledgment of the Rightly Guided Caliphs (al-khulafā’ al-rāshidūn) that they are not infallible, and the nation's lack of disapproval of any of them who took charge while acknowledging the negation of infallibility from them. This is Abu Bakr saying: 'Obey me as long as I obey Allah; if I disobey Allah, then no obedience is due to me from you,' up to his saying: 'I do not prefer myself over your hair and skin.' And this is ‘Umar saying: 'One who shows us our faults' and 'Were it not for ‘Ali, ‘Umar would perish' and 'Were it not for Mu‘ādh, ‘Umar would perish.' And this is ‘Uthman saying: 'One verse permitted them and another forbade them,' meaning concerning two sisters [being married simultaneously].
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the imam is not required to be infallible or omniscient. Al-Baqillani argues that the imam's role is to implement already-revealed laws, and he acts as a deputy of the community, which supervises and corrects him. Therefore, he need not be infallible, just as subordinate officials (governors, judges, etc.) are not infallible. If the objector argues that the imam appoints these officials, and thus must be infallible to avoid error in appointment, the author responds with an analogy: those officials also appoint their own deputies, so they would also have to be infallible, leading to an infinite regress. The author then cites empirical evidence from the Rightly Guided Caliphs: Abu Bakr's statement that he must be obeyed only if he obeys God; ‘Umar's acknowledgment of his need for correction; and ‘Uthman's admission of uncertainty regarding the permissibility of marrying two sisters. These examples demonstrate that the early community accepted that caliphs were not infallible.

Key Terms
معصوم
العصمة
الغيب
الخلفاء الراشدين
وكيل
نائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the imam must be sinless and all-knowing like a prophet.
Correction: The imam is merely a fallible deputy of the community, as al-Baqillani proves through logic and the example of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Baqillani provide to show that caliphs are not infallible?
A) The Prophet said caliphs make mistakes.
B) The Consensus argued against infallibility.
C) The Rightly Guided Caliphs admitted their fallibility.
D) Reason proves that infallibility is impossible for humans.

Key Takeaway
The imam does not need to be infallible because he is a fallible deputy of the community, as affirmed by the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The caliphs openly admitted their fallibility and need for correction. When was the last time you acknowledged a mistake to someone you lead or care about? How does your pride prevent you from doing so today? Will you commit to admitting one specific error to someone this week?
?
The imam is a deputy of the community, and the community has a duty to direct and correct him. Are you fulfilling your responsibility to advise those in authority over you—in your family, workplace, or community—or do you remain silent out of fear or indifference? What one step will you take this week to speak up about something that needs correction?
?
‘Umar said, 'Were it not for ‘Ali, ‘Umar would perish.' Who in your life has corrected you and saved you from a mistake? Have you thanked them? More importantly, are you humble enough to seek out such people, or do you surround yourself with those who only agree with you? What will you do today to invite honest correction?

The Imam's Role and Why Infallibility Is Unnecessary
Baqillani argues that the Imam does not need to be infallible or omniscient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational argument that the Imam, as a deputy of the community, does not require infallibility.

Core Idea
The Imam is a fallible deputy, not a divine agent.

This passage from Imam Baqillani's Tamhid tackles a critical question: Must the leader of the Muslim community be infallible? Responding to sectarian claims, Baqillani offers a clear, rational proof that the Imam's role is limited to implementing already-known Sharia, and he is accountable to the community. Read on to see how he builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage is concise; each clause builds on the previous.
2
Identify the main argument
Notice how Baqillani defines the Imam's role: he is appointed to implement already-established rulings, and he acts as a deputy (وكيل) and representative (نائب) of the community.
The community's role is to correct and guide him.
3
Analyze the analogy with other officials
Baqillani compares the Imam to governors, judges, and tax collectors—none of whom are required to be infallible. This analogy reinforces the point that the Imam's position does not require infallibility.
Think about why these officials are not expected to be infallible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون معصوماً عالماً بالغيب ولا بجميع لا يَشِذَّ عليه منه شيء فهو أن الإمام إنما يُنصَبُ لإقامة الأحكام وحدود وأمور قد شرعها الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تقدم علم الأمة بها وهو في جميع ما يتولاه وكيل للأمة ونائب عنها وهي من ورائه في تسديده وتقويمه وإذكاره وتنبيهه وأخذ الحق منه إذا وجب عليه وخلعه والاستبدال به متى اقترف ما يوجب خلعه فليس يحتاج مع ذلك إلى أن يكون معصوماً كما لا يحتاج أميره وقاضيه وجابي خَرَاجه وصدقاته وأصحاب مسائله وحرسه إلى أن يكونوا معصومين وهو فليس يلي بنفسه شيئاً أكثر مما يليه خلفاؤه من الأمور.
As for what indicates that he does not have to be infallible, knowing the unseen, nor knowing everything from which nothing escapes him, it is that the Imam is only appointed to implement the rulings and punishments and matters that the Messenger (peace be upon him) legislated, and the knowledge of the community has already preceded them [i.e., these matters]. And he, in all that he undertakes, is a deputy of the community and a representative of them, and they are behind him in guiding him, correcting him, reminding him, alerting him, taking the truth from him when it is due upon him, deposing him and replacing him whenever he commits what necessitates his removal. So he does not need with that to be infallible, just as his governor, his judge, his tax collector of land tax and alms, his officials of his districts, and his guards do not need to be infallible. And he does not undertake himself anything more than what his deputies undertake of matters.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the necessity of the Imam's infallibility. Baqillani argues that the Imam's role is to implement already-known laws, and he is a representative of the community. The community corrects, guides, and even removes him if he errs. Therefore, there is no need for him to be infallible, just as his own officials (governors, judges, tax collectors, etc.) are not infallible. The argument establishes the Imam's fallibility by analogy with his deputies.

Key Terms
عصمة
إمام
وكيل
نائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam must be infallible because he leads the community.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the Imam is a fallible deputy, subject to correction and removal by the community, so infallibility is not required.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the Imam not need to be infallible?
A) Because he is appointed by God and therefore sinless.
B) Because he is a deputy of the community and subject to correction.
C) Because he is the most knowledgeable of the community.
D) Because he is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that the Imam, as a deputy of the community, does not need infallibility because the community guides and can depose him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may expect your leaders—in the mosque, at work, in your family—to be flawless. Baqillani says the Imam is just a fallible deputy. Is there a leader in your life whom you have silently demanded to be infallible? How does that expectation relieve you of your own responsibility to speak up? What will you do this week to actively guide or correct that person with kindness?
?
The community stands behind the Imam to 'take the truth from him' and 'depose him' if needed. Are you personally part of that collective oversight, or do you passively tolerate wrong in your community because it's easier? Name one error you have observed in a leader but remained silent about. Will you commit today to address it in a respectful way?
?
Baqillani compares the Imam to a governor, judge, or tax collector—all fallible. Do you treat some people in your life as if they were above correction, attributing a kind of infallibility to them? Is that a subtle form of shirk (giving created beings attributes only Allah has)? Search your heart and repent, resolving to see all humans as fallible deputies accountable to Allah and to one another.

Objection and Answer on the Imam's Appointments
Baqillani answers the objection that the Imam's appointment of deputies requires his infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the claim that the Imam must be infallible due to his appointment of deputies.

Core Idea
Baqillani uses a clever reductio to show the objection leads to absurdity.

This lesson presents a concise dialectical exchange. The opponent argues that since the Imam appoints deputies, he must be infallible. Baqillani responds by extending the same logic to all officials, showing the argument's flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of the objection and answer come through.
Notice the concise and parallel structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent states: 'He is the one who appoints his deputies, so he must be infallible from error.' Understand the reasoning: the Imam's role requires infallibility.
The opponent is likely from a sect that demands absolute infallibility for the Imam.
3
Identify Baqillani's response
Baqillani says: 'Similarly, his governors, judges, and tax collectors appoint their own deputies, so they must be infallible.' This extends the logic to all officials.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if the premise were true, absurd consequences follow.
4
Understand the logical structure
The argument is: If appointing deputies requires infallibility, then every official who appoints a deputy must be infallible. Since that is false, the premise is false.
This is a classic dialectical move in kalam.
5
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why this refutation is effective. It does not prove the Imam's sinlessness, but shows the opponent's argument is invalid.
Baqillani avoids conceding the opponent's frame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فهو المُولّي لخلفائه فيجب أن يكون لذلك معصوماً من الخطأ، قيل لهم : وكذلك أمراؤه وقضاته وعُمَّال خراجه يُوَلُّونَ خلفائهم، فيجب أن يكونوا لذلك معصومين .
If they say: 'He is the one who appoints his deputies, so he must be infallible from error for that purpose,' it is said to them: 'Similarly, his governors, judges, and tax collectors appoint their own deputies, so they must be infallible for that purpose.'
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection claims that since the Imam appoints deputies, he must be infallible in those appointments to avoid error. The answer uses a reductio ad absurdum: if that were true, then every official who appoints deputies would also need infallibility, which is clearly false. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
تولية
خليفة
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam must be infallible because he appoints deputies.
Correction: Infallibility is not required for appointing deputies; otherwise all officials who appoint deputies would need it, which is not the case.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond to the claim that the Imam must be infallible because he appoints deputies?
A) He agrees.
B) He argues that the Imam's deputies also appoint deputies, so they would also need infallibility.
C) He says the Imam does not appoint deputies.
D) He says the deputies are infallible.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by showing its logic would require all appointing officials to be infallible, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold someone in authority to a standard of perfection in one area, do you apply the same standard elsewhere? Where in your life have you been inconsistent in your expectations of leaders? This week, identify one such inconsistency and rectify it.
?
Baqillani's response forces us to examine the logic behind our assumptions. Think of a belief you hold about religious leadership—does the reasoning hold up when applied to other, less significant roles? If not, are you willing to let go of that assumption today?
?
The objection's flaw is that it demands infallibility for one appointment but not for similar ones. Where in your own thinking do you grant special privileges to certain people or groups without justification? Commit to challenging one such double standard this week.

Admissions of the Rashidun Caliphs of Non-Infallibility
Baqillani cites statements from the four caliphs acknowledging their fallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the statements of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali demonstrate that the early Muslim community did not believe the caliphs were infallible.

Core Idea
The caliphs themselves denied their own infallibility.

Baqillani provides a powerful argument from the actions and words of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs. Each one explicitly acknowledged that they could err and that their authority was conditional. This passage shows that the early community did not hold the imam to be infallible, contrary to some later claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence of four caliphs and their statements.
Each statement is a direct admission of fallibility.
2
Identify each caliph's admission
Go through each caliph: Abu Bakr conditions obedience on his own obedience to God; Umar acknowledges needing correction from Ali and Mu'adh; Uthman admits uncertainty on a legal ruling; Ali changes his opinion and admits a slip.
The key is that these are admissions from the highest authorities.
3
Understand the implication
If the caliphs themselves say they are not infallible, then the community's acceptance of their authority despite this shows that infallibility is not a condition for leadership.
Baqillani uses this to counter those who claim imams must be ma'sum (sinless).
4
Reflect on the argument
Why is this evidence strong? Because the caliphs were the leaders, and no one objected to their statements. This indicates consensus (ijma') that imams are not infallible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على هذا اعترافُ الخلفاء الراشدين بأنهم غير معصومين وترك إنكار الأمة أو واحد منهم تولى الأمر مع اعترافهم بنفي العصمة عنهم . هذا أبو بكر يقول: «أطيعوني ما أطعت الله ؛ فإذا عصيت الله فلا طاعة لي عليكم» (۱) إلى قوله : «لا أوثِرُ في أشعاركم وأبشاركُم» (۲) ؛ وهذا عمر يقول : «امرءاً أهدى إلينا عيوبنا» و «لولا على هلك عمر (۳) و «لولا معاذ لهلك عمر؛ وهذا عثمان يقول (۱) : «أحلتهما آية وحرمتهما آية (*) ، يعني في الجمع بين الأختين بملك اليمين؛ وهذا عليّ يرى الرأي ثم يرجع عنه كالذي قيل له في بيع أمهات الأولاد: أجمع رأبي ورأي عمر على ألا يُبعن وقد رأيت بيعهن) (۱) ويسأل عن مسائل في الأحكام ويطلب الروايات كطلبهم، ويقول فيما بُلِيَ به من الحرب والهرج وتشتت الآراء عليه : لقد زللتُ زلة لا أعتذر سوف أكيس بعدها وأنْشَمِرُ () وأجمع الرأي الشتيت المُنتَشِرُ ()
And this is indicated by the admission of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs that they are not infallible, and by the lack of denial by the community—or any one of them—(i.e., the caliphs) when they (i.e., the caliphs) assumed authority while acknowledging the negation of infallibility regarding themselves. This is Abu Bakr saying: 'Obey me as long as I obey God; if I disobey God, then there is no obedience to me from you' (1) up to his saying: 'I do not prefer myself over your hair and your skin' (2). And 'Umar says: '... a man who points out our faults to us' and 'Were it not for 'Ali, 'Umar would have perished' (3) and 'Were it not for Mu'adh, 'Umar would have perished.' And 'Uthman says (1): 'One verse made them lawful and another verse made them unlawful (*),' meaning regarding combining two sisters through right hand possession. And 'Ali would hold an opinion then retract from it, as when he was told about the sale of slave mothers of children: 'My opinion and the opinion of 'Umar agreed that they should not be sold, and I have seen them sold' (1). And he would ask about rulings in legal matters and request narrations just as they requested, and he said regarding the war and turmoil and the scatter of opinions that befell him: 'I have slipped a slip that I do not excuse; I will become wise after it and strive, and I will gather the scattered scattered opinion.' ()
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage provides evidence from the actions and statements of the four Rightly-Guided Caliphs. Each caliph explicitly admitted that they could err and were not infallible. Abu Bakr conditioned obedience on his own obedience to God. Umar acknowledged the need for correction from others ('Ali and Mu'adh). Uthman admitted uncertainty on a legal issue. 'Ali changed his opinions and admitted a slip (zalla) regarding the turmoil during his caliphate. These examples prove that the early community did not believe in the infallibility of the Imam.

Key Terms
خلافة
عصمة
الخلفاء الراشدين
زلّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sunnis believe the caliphs were sinless or infallible.
Correction: The passage shows the caliphs themselves denied their own infallibility, and the community accepted this.

Checkpoint
Which caliph said 'Obey me as long as I obey God; if I disobey God, then there is no obedience to me from you'?
A) Umar
B) Abu Bakr
C) Uthman
D) Ali

Key Takeaway
The Rightly-Guided Caliphs openly admitted they were not infallible, and the community upheld their leadership, proving that infallibility is not a requirement for the imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The caliphs openly admitted their mistakes. When was the last time you sincerely admitted a fault to someone you lead or respect? What held you back?
?
Abu Bakr said, 'Obey me as long as I obey God.' Do you follow anyone—a scholar, a leader, or a friend—unconditionally? How would your life change if you only followed them when they obey God?
?
Umar welcomed someone who 'points out our faults.' Who in your life has the permission to correct you? If no one, why not? What would it take to invite that?

Refuting Shi'ite Excuses for 'Ali's Actions
Baqillani refutes Shi'ite claims that 'Ali's actions were errors excused by taqiyya or foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that apparent facts (zahir) override Shi'ite claims of taqiyya in justifying 'Ali's actions.

Core Idea
Shi'ite taqiyya cannot hide what is manifestly wrong.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts Shi'ite attempts to excuse certain actions attributed to 'Ali, such as arbitration and appointing a traitor. The Shi'ites claim these were errors justified by taqiyya (dissimulation) or foreknowledge. Baqillani dismisses these claims as false, insisting that the apparent (zahir) facts are decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then consult the literal translation to understand the specific claims and refutation.
Pay attention to the list of actions mentioned.
2
Identify the three actions the Shi'ites consider errors
The text lists arbitration (tahkim), selling captives to Masqala, and appointing a traitor. Recognise how these are presented.
3
Understand the Shi'ite defense
The Shi'ites excuse these actions as taqiyya or as foretold by the Prophet. Note the phrase 'ادعائهم في ذلك التقية'.
4
Grasp Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani rejects these claims as false and rejected based on the apparent known facts (az-zahir al-ma'lum). See the phrase 'وهذا باطل متروك بالظاهر المعلوم'.
Consider why the zahir is decisive.
5
Note Baqillani's qualification
Baqillani adds that in his view these actions were not errors at all, as explained elsewhere. This nuances the refutation.
This shows Baqillani's broader theological stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي غير ذلك مما حكي عنه مما تقرَّ الشيعة أنه ليس بصواب في الدين. كفعل التحكيم وبيعه السَّبي على مَصْقَلَة بن هبيرة واحتماله المال وتوليته من خان الله والمسلمين وخانه ولحق بالمنابذين لـه والخارجين عليه وادعائهم في ذلك التقية ومع ما أعلمه النبيُّ صلّى الله عليه وسلم بما يؤول الأمر إليه. وهذا باطل متروك بالظاهر المعلوم وإن كان هذا أجمعُ ليس بخطأ من فعله عندنا لما قد بيناه في غير هذا الكتاب .
And besides that, among what is narrated about him that the Shi'ites consider not correct in religion, such as the act of arbitration, his sale of captives to Masqala ibn Hubayra, his acceptance of money, his appointment of one who betrayed God and the Muslims and betrayed him and joined those hostile to him and those rebelling against him, and their claim in that of taqiyya, and despite what the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed him of regarding the eventual outcome. And this is false and rejected based on the apparent known facts, even if this entire (conduct) is not an error on his part in our view due to what we have explained in another book.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This is a refutation of Shi'ite attempts to excuse 'Ali's actions. The Shi'ites claimed that actions like arbitration (tahkim), selling captives to Masqala, and appointing a traitor were errors, but they justified them as taqiyya (dissimulation) or because the Prophet had informed 'Ali of the outcome. Baqillani rejects these claims as false based on the apparent (zahir) facts, though he notes that in his own view these actions were not actually errors, as he has explained elsewhere.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taqiyya can justify apparent errors in the actions of a Companion like 'Ali.
Correction: Baqillani argues that manifest facts (zahir) override any claim of taqiyya; thus the Shi'ite defense is false.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani regard the Shi'ite claims that 'Ali's actions were excused by taqiyya?
A) He accepts them as valid.
B) He considers them false and rejected.
C) He says they are plausible.
D) He does not mention them.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes Shi'ite taqiyya excuses for 'Ali's actions by invoking the manifest facts (zahir) as the decisive criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used a 'valid excuse' to justify an action that the clear facts show was wrong? What would it take for you to admit the truth rather than hide behind rationalizations?
?
This lesson shows that even the actions of ‘Ali are judged by apparent facts, not hidden excuses. How often do you judge yourself by hidden intentions while judging others by their apparent actions? Will you today commit to applying the same standard to yourself?
?
Baqillani rejects taqiyya because the manifest truth overrides. Is there an area of your life where you have been living in 'taqiyya'—pretending something is acceptable when the manifest truth of the Qur’an and Sunnah says otherwise? What will you do about it after this lesson?
