The Imam's Role: Administration, Not Special Knowledge
Baqillani states that the Imam does not need special knowledge and defines his administrative duties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Imam's role is administrative and executive, not a source of hidden religious knowledge.

Core Idea
The Imam's authority is practical, not doctrinal.

Baqillani addresses two key questions about the Imam: does he possess special knowledge, and what is his actual purpose? The answers clarify that the Imam is not a revealer of hidden truths but a manager of the community's practical affairs. This passage distinguishes Sunni political theology from other views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
The passage has two distinct questions.
2
Identify the first question and answer
The first question asks if the community needs the Imam's special knowledge. Note the answer: no, because he and they are equal in knowledge of Sharia.
This refutes the idea of the Imam having exclusive divine knowledge.
3
Identify the second question and answer
The second question asks why the Imam is established. The answer lists practical duties: managing armies, guarding frontiers, justice, etc.
These duties are all administrative, not doctrinal.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Baqillani makes this distinction. It limits the Imam's role to execution of Sharia, not interpretation or revelation.
This counters sectarian claims of an infallible, knowledge-endowed Imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر ما أقيم الإمام لأجله فإن قالوا : فهل تحتاج الأمة إلى علم الإمام وبيان شيء خُص به دونهم وكشف ما ذهب علمه عنهم ؟ قيل لهم : لا لأنه هو وهم في علم الشريعة وحكمها سِيَّان فإن قالوا : فلماذا يُقامُ الإمام؟ قيل لهم : لأجل ما ذكرناه من قبل من تدبير الجيوش وسد الثغور وردع الظالم والأخذ للمظلوم وإقامة الحدود وقسم الفيء بين المسلمين والدفع بهم في حجهم وغزوهم فهذا الذي يليه
Chapter: Mention of what the Imam is established for. If they say: 'Does the community need the knowledge of the Imam and his explanation of something he is specialized in to the exclusion of them, and the revelation of what has escaped their knowledge?' It is said to them: 'No, because he and they are equal in knowledge of the Sharia and its rulings.' If they say: 'For what reason is the Imam established?' It is said to them: 'For what we mentioned before: managing the armies, guarding the frontiers, repressing the oppressor, taking for the oppressed, establishing the prescribed punishments, distributing the spoils among the Muslims, and pushing them to perform their Hajj and their military expeditions. That is what he undertakes.'
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a new section defining the purpose of the Imam. It begins with a question: does the community need the Imam's special knowledge? The answer is no, because all are equal in knowledge of Sharia. Then it asks why the Imam is established. The answer lists practical administrative duties: managing armies, guarding frontiers, justice, enforcing laws, distributing spoils, and organizing Hajj and military campaigns. Thus the Imam's role is executive, not doctrinal.

Key Terms
إمام
شريعة
فيء
حدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Imam is a source of hidden religious knowledge beyond the common understanding of Sharia.
Correction: Baqillani asserts that the Imam and the community are equal in knowledge of Sharia; the Imam's role is limited to practical administration.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the primary purpose of establishing the Imam?
A) To reveal hidden knowledge of the Sharia.
B) To administer practical affairs like armies, justice, and frontier defense.
C) To serve as a prophet after Muhammad.
D) To be infallible in all matters.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's authority is limited to executing the Sharia, not interpreting it beyond the community's common knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your religious leaders possess hidden knowledge of God's will that you cannot access? How does this passage confront that assumption in your heart? Will you now commit to seeking knowledge directly from the sources instead of relying passively on others?
?
If the Imam is only a manager of practical affairs, what does that mean for your personal responsibility to understand the Sharia? Where have you been lazy in learning what Allah has made accessible to all?
?
The Imam's duties include protecting the oppressed and restraining the oppressor. In your own family, workplace, or community, where are you failing to stand for justice? What specific action will you take this week to fulfill this responsibility?

Refuting Shiite Claims on Ali's Actions and Taqiyya
Baqillani refutes Shiite claims that Ali used taqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Shiite claim that Ali used taqiyya in his rulings and actions, emphasizing that apparent actions are the standard.

Core Idea
Ali's outward actions are the standard, not hidden reasons like taqiyya.

This passage from Baqillani addresses the Shiite claim that Ali practiced taqiyya (dissimulation) in certain rulings and actions. The author presents examples where Ali changed his opinions or made decisions that Shiites consider errors, asserting that these are not mistakes and that the claim of taqiyya is baseless. Let's examine the arguments and understand the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples given: combining sisters, sale of mothers, the slip poem.
2
Identify the specific examples
List the actions of Ali that Shiites claim were wrong or done under taqiyya. The passage mentions several: arbitration, selling captives, appointing a traitor, etc.
These examples are crucial for the refutation.
3
Understand Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani argues that the claim of taqiyya is false and that these actions are not considered mistakes in his school. The refutation relies on the apparent meaning of the events.
He also references that Ali had foreknowledge from the Prophet, but this does not justify taqiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجمع بين الأختين بملك اليمين ؛ وهذا عليّ يرى الرأي ثم يرجع عنه كالذي قيل له في بيع أمهات الأولاد: أجمع رأبي ورأي عمر على ألا يُبعن وقد رأيت بيعهن) ويسأل عن مسائل في الأحكام ويطلب الروايات كطلبهم، ويقول فيما بُلِيَ به من الحرب والهرج وتشتت الآراء عليه : لقد زللتُ زلة لا أعتذر سوف أكيس بعدها وأنْشَمِرْ وأجمع الرأي الشتيت المُنتَشِرُ وفي غير ذلك مما حكي عنه مما تقرَّ الشيعة أنه ليس بصواب في الدين. كفعل التحكيم وبيعه السَّبي على مَصْقَلَة بن هبيرة واحتماله المال وتوليته من خان الله والمسلمين وخانه ولحق بالمنابذين لـه والخارجين عليه وادعائهم في ذلك التقية ومع ما أعلمه النبيُّ صلّى الله عليه وسلم بما يؤول الأمر إليه. وهذا باطل متروك بالظاهر المعلوم وإن كان هذا أجمعُ ليس بخطأ من فعله عندنا لما قد بيناه في غير هذا الكتاب.
The combination of two sisters by right hand possession; and this Ali holds an opinion then retracts from it like what was said to him regarding the sale of mothers of children: 'Abu Bakr and Umar agreed that they should not be sold, and I have seen their sale.' And he asks about rulings and requests traditions like their request, and he says regarding what he was afflicted with of war, turmoil, and dispersal of opinions: 'I have indeed slipped a slip for which I do not apologize, I will become clever after it and be active, and I will gather the scattered dispersed opinion.' And in other things reported from him which the Shiites claim are not correct in religion, like the act of arbitration, his sale of captives to Masqalah ibn Hubayrah, his acceptance of wealth and appointing him over one who betrayed God and the Muslims and betrayed him, and joined those who opposed him and rebelled against him, and their claim in that of taqiyya (dissimulation), and with what the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed him of how the matter would end. This is false and rejected by the apparent known meaning, even if this is not a mistake in his action according to us, as we have explained in another book.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Shiite claims regarding certain actions of Ali ibn Abi Talib. The author begins by mentioning an example: Ali initially had an opinion about combining two sisters through right hand possession, then retracted. Similarly, regarding the sale of mothers of children (ummahat al-awlad), Ali said that Abu Bakr and Umar agreed that they should not be sold, but he himself later saw fit to sell them. The author then quotes a poem where Ali admits a slip in the turmoil of war. Then the author lists other actions that Shiites claim were wrong, such as the arbitration, selling captives to Masqalah, and appointing a traitor. Shiites claim Ali did these out of taqiyya (dissimulation) and that he had prior knowledge from the Prophet. The author refutes this, stating that these claims are false and are rejected by the apparent and clear meaning of the events. The author adds that even if these actions were done, they are not considered mistakes according to the author's school, as explained elsewhere.

Key Terms
التقية
التحكيم
أمهات الأولاد
المصقلة بن هبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that taqiyya (dissimulation) is a valid principle in Sunni Islam to explain away actions of Companions that seem inconsistent.
Correction: The author asserts that the apparent actions of the Companions are to be followed, not hidden intentions, and that the claim of taqiyya is false and rejected.

Checkpoint
What do Shiites claim about Ali's actions like the arbitration and selling captives?
A) They were correct and based on ijtihad.
B) They were done out of taqiyya.
C) They were mistakes.
D) They were obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes Shiite taqiyya claims by arguing that Ali's apparent actions are not errors and that outward actions, not hidden intentions, are the basis for judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a wrongful action to yourself by claiming a hidden good intention, much like the Shiites used taqiyya to excuse Ali's actions? How does Baqillani's insistence on the 'apparent known meaning' expose the deception in your own heart? What one action from this past week are you most tempted to rationalize, and what would it mean to honestly judge it by its outward reality?
?
Ali himself admitted a 'slip' without apology, yet Shiites try to explain it away. When was the last time you admitted a genuine mistake without any excuse or deflection? Could it be that you fear the vulnerability of being seen as fallible? Will you today confess a specific failing to someone you trust, without adding 'but'?
?
The Shiites claimed that the Prophet's foreknowledge validated Ali's actions under taqiyya. In your own life, do you ever hide behind 'God knows my intentions' to avoid taking responsibility for the harmful effects of your actions? If you were to be judged solely by the visible impact of your choices—not your hidden motives—what would you most regret? How will you change that starting this week?

The Imam's Knowledge: Not Special
Baqillani denies the imam's special knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the imam does not possess any special knowledge of Sharia beyond what the community has.

Core Idea
The imam is not a source of secret knowledge.

A common assumption is that the imam, as a leader, must have unique religious insight. Baqillani addresses this directly, stating that the imam and the community are equal in their knowledge of Sharia. This lesson explores that key point.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the objector's assumption that the imam might have special knowledge.
The passage is a dialogue format.
2
Identify the author's response
Baqillani's answer is clear: the imam and community are equal in Sharia knowledge. Find the phrase that states this equality.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this refutes the sectarian idea that the imam has a unique teaching role. How does this affect the community's responsibility?
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر ما أقيم الإمام لأجله فإن قالوا : فهل تحتاج الأمة إلى علم الإمام وبيان شيء خُص به دونهم وكشف ما ذهب علمه عنهم ؟ قيل لهم : لا لأنه هو وهم في علم الشريعة وحكمها سِيَّان فإن قالوا : فلماذا يُقامُ الإمام؟ قيل لهم : لأجل ما ذكرناه من قبل من تدبير الجيوش وسد الثغور وردع الظالم والأخذ للمظلوم وإقامة الحدود وقسم الفيء بين المسلمين والدفع بهم في حجهم وغزوهم فهذا الذي يليه ويُقام لأجله فإن غَلِطَ في شيء منه أو عدل به عن موضعه كانت الأمة من ورائه لتقويمه والأخذ له بواجبه.
Chapter on mentioning for what the imam is established. For if they say: Does the community need the knowledge of the imam and the exposition of something he is singled out with without them, and the revealing of what is lost from them? It is said to them: No, because he and they are equal in the knowledge of Sharia and its rulings. For if they say: Then why is the imam established? It is said to them: For the sake of what we mentioned before: managing the armies, guarding the frontiers, restraining the oppressor, taking [rights] for the oppressed, establishing the legal punishments, dividing the spoils (fay') among the Muslims, and urging them in their pilgrimage and warfare. This is what he undertakes and for what he is established. So if he errs in something of that or deviates from its proper place, the community is behind him to correct him and to take him to his duty.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the purpose of the imam. It takes the form of a question-and-answer. First, the objector asks whether the community needs the imam for his special knowledge or for unveiling hidden matters. The author answers no, because the imam and the community are equal in their knowledge of Sharia. Then the objector asks why the imam is established at all. The answer lists several executive duties: managing armies, guarding the frontiers, restraining the oppressor, taking rights for the oppressed, establishing hadd punishments, dividing fay' (spoils), and organizing pilgrimage and warfare. These are the duties for which the imam is established. If he errs in any of these or deviates from justice, the community has the responsibility to correct him and ensure he fulfills his duties.

Key Terms
الإمام
علم الشريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the imam must have special knowledge or authority to teach hidden truths.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the imam and community are equal in Sharia knowledge; the imam's role is not to teach but to administer.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, does the community need the imam for special knowledge of Sharia?
A) Yes, the imam has hidden knowledge.
B) No, they are equal in knowledge.
C) Only the imam can interpret the Quran.
D) The imam can reveal unseen things.

Key Takeaway
The imam and community are equal in knowledge of Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Duties of the Imam and Community Oversight
Baqillani enumerates the imam's duties and community oversight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific administrative duties of the imam and the community's responsibility to correct him if he errs.

Core Idea
The imam exists to manage affairs, not to teach.

If the imam does not have special knowledge, what is his purpose? Baqillani answers with a clear list of practical duties, from managing armies to dividing spoils. This lesson also highlights the community's role in keeping the imam accountable.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer about duties
Read the Arabic passage covering the question 'why is the imam established?' and the list that follows.
The list contains several distinct duties.
2
Understand each duty
Examine each duty: managing armies, guarding frontiers, restraining the oppressor, taking for the oppressed, establishing punishments, dividing fay', and urging pilgrimage and warfare.
These are all executive and judicial functions.
3
Note the community's corrective role
The passage ends with a crucial point: if the imam errs or deviates, the community must correct him and compel him to his duty.
This shows the imam is accountable to the community.

Deposing an Imam and the Obligation to Obey: The Sunni Position
Baqillani presents two views on what necessitates deposing an imam, then supports the majority view of patience and obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions on what necessitates deposing an imam and the Sunni stance that patience and obedience are required except in sin.

Core Idea
Rebellion is not permitted even against an unjust imam; only admonishment and disobedience in sin are allowed.

This passage addresses a critical question in Sunni political theology: when, if ever, is it permissible to depose a ruler? Baqillani presents the conditions that some scholars consider grounds for removal, then explains the majority view—backed by prophetic hadiths—that forbids rebellion and commands patient obedience. Understanding this balance of patience and defiance in sin is key.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani starts with a question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the two views on deposition
The passage presents an initial list of conditions for deposing an imam (apostasy, abandoning prayer, and according to many also sinfulness). Then it states the majority Sunni view that deposition is not necessitated by these. Find both views.
The first view is attributed to 'many people' (كثير من الناس), while the second is attributed to 'the majority of Ahl al-Ithbat and Ashab al-Hadith'.
3
Examine the evidence for the Sunni view
The majority supports their position with numerous hadiths. Note the specific hadith excerpts: 'Hear and obey even if a mutilated slave…' and 'Obey them even if they eat your wealth…'.
These hadiths emphasize obedience even under unjust rulers, with the only exception being commands to sin.
4
Reflect on the practical implication
Consider what this means for a Muslim living under an unjust ruler. The passage says it is obligatory to admonish, fear God, and disobey only in sinful commands. Rebellion is not an option.
This reflects the classical Sunni principle of patience (sabr) and avoiding fitna.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر ما يوجب خلع الإمام وسقوط فرض طاعته يوجب ذلك إن قال قائل : ما الذي يوجب خلع الإمام عندكم؟ قيل له : أمور منها كفر بعد الإيمان ومنها تركه إقامة الصلاة والدعاء إلى ذلك ومنها عند كثير من الناس فسقه وظُلْمُه بغضب الأموال وضرب الأبشار وتناول النفوس المحرمة وتضييع الحقوق وتعطيل الحدود. وقال الجمهور من أهل الإثبات وأصحاب الحديث : لا ينخلع بهذه الأمور ولا يجب الخروج عليه بل يجب وعظه وتخويفه وترك طاعته في شيء مما يدعو إليه من معاصي الله واحتجوا في ذلك بأخبار كثيرة متظاهرة عن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم وعن اصحابه في وجوب طاعة الأئمة وإن جاروا واستأثروا بالأموال وأنه قال عليه السلام : اسمعوا وأطيعوا ولو لعبد أجدع ولو لعبد حبشي وصلوا وراء كل بر وفاجر وروي أنه قال: أطعهم، وإن أكلوا مالك وضربوا ظهرك، وأطيعوهم ما أقاموا الصلاة في أخبار كثيرة وردت
Chapter on mentioning what necessitates deposing an imam and the fall of the obligation of obedience to him. That necessitates [deposing]. If a speaker says: What necessitates deposing the imam according to you? It is said to him: Matters, among them: apostasy after faith, and among them: his abandoning of establishing prayer and calling to that, and among them, according to many people: his sinfulness and his injustice by taking properties unjustly, striking skins, taking forbidden lives, wasting rights, and suspending legal punishments. And the majority of the people of affirmation (Ahl al-Ithbat) and the hadith folk (Ashab al-Hadith) said: He is not deposed by these matters, nor is rebellion against him obligatory. Rather, it is obligatory to admonish him, fear him, and disobey him in something to which he calls from acts of disobedience to God. And they argued for that with many mutawatir reports from the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his Companions regarding the obligation of obeying the imams even if they are unjust and take wealth exclusively, and that he said, peace be upon him: 'Hear and obey, even if [your leader is] a mutilated slave, and even if a black slave, and pray behind every righteous and sinful [leader].' And it is reported that he said: 'Obey them even if they eat your wealth and strike your back, and obey them as long as they establish prayer,' in many reports.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument about what necessitates the removal of an imam. It begins with a question: what necessitates deposing the imam? The answer lists three categories: first, apostasy; second, abandoning the prayer and calling to it; third, according to many people, sinfulness and injustice including taking property wrongfully, striking people, killing unlawfully, wasting rights, and neglecting punishments. Then the author presents the view of the majority (the people of affirmation and hadith scholars) that these issues do not necessitate deposing nor rebellion. Instead, the duty is to advise, fear God, and disobey only in sinful commands. The evidence is from many hadiths that command obedience even to unjust rulers, such as the hadith 'Hear and obey even if [the ruler] is a mutilated slave' and 'Obey them even if they eat your wealth and strike your back, as long as they establish prayer.' These ahadith support patient endurance of injustice without rebellion.

Key Terms
خلع الإمام
الجمهور
أهل الإثبات
أصحاب الحديث
الطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any major sin or injustice by the imam justifies rebellion to depose him.
Correction: The Sunni position is that only apostasy and abandoning prayer (and according to some, other grave sins) are grounds for deposition, but even then rebellion is not obligatory; rather, admonishment and disobedience in sin are required.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, what should be done if an imam is sinful and unjust?
A) Immediately depose him by force.
B) Admonish him and disobey only in sins.
C) Ignore him and pray separately.
D) Leave the community.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that obedience to the imam is obligatory even if he is unjust, except when he commands sin; rebellion is not permitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself feeling justified in speaking harshly against a leader because of their shortcomings? How does this lesson's command to obey even an unjust ruler challenge your heart's inclination?
?
Imagine a leader commanded you to do something clearly sinful. Would you disobey that specific command? Are you prepared to face the consequences of that disobedience, trusting God rather than fearing the ruler?
?
This lesson highlights the virtue of patience with injustice. Is there an area in your life where you are unwilling to be patient with authority, perhaps in your family or community? What would it cost you to obey God by being patient, as the hadiths teach?

The Majority View: Obedience Despite Injustice
Baqillani reports the majority view on unjust rulers with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Sunni position on obedience to unjust rulers and its scriptural basis.

Core Idea
Even unjust rulers must be obeyed except in sin, not overthrown.

This passage presents the mainstream Sunni view on a critical political theology question: what do we do when rulers become unjust? The majority consensus forbids rebellion and prescribes admonition and selective disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the key phrases: 'لا ينخلع', 'وعظ وتخويف', 'ترك طاعته في شيء مما يدعو إليه من معاصي الله'.
The passage is a report of the majority opinion, not necessarily Baqillani's own view.
2
Identify the three parts of the majority view
The passage gives three components: (1) The imam is not removed, (2) Rebellion is not obligatory, (3) Admonition and selective disobedience in sinful matters are required.
This is a balanced position between unconditional obedience and rebellion.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
Consider the hadith cited: 'Listen and obey even a mutilated slave...' and 'Obey them even if they eat your wealth...'. These emphasize obedience despite injustice, with the exception of sinful commands.
The author notes he has treated this in another book; we focus only on what is presented here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجمهور من أهل الإثبات وأصحاب الحديث : لا ينخلع بهذه الأمور ولا يجب الخروج عليه بل يجب وعظه وتخويفه وترك طاعته في شيء مما يدعو إليه من معاصي الله واحتجوا في ذلك بأخبار كثيرة متظاهرة عن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم وعن اصحابه في وجوب طاعة الأئمة وإن جاروا واستأثروا بالأموال وأنه قال عليه السلام : اسمعوا وأطيعوا ولو لعبد أجدع ولو لعبد حبشي وصلوا وراء كل بر وفاجر ) وروي أنه قال: «أطعهم، وإن أكلوا مالك وضربوا ظهرك، وأطيعوهم ما أقاموا الصلاة» في أخبار كثيرة وردت هذا الباب. وقد ذكرنا ما في هذا الباب في كتاب «إكفار المتأولين وذكرنا ما رُوِيَ في معارضتها، وقلنا في تأويلها بما يُغنى الناظر فيه إن شاء الله .
And the majority of the people of affirmation and the hadith scholars said: He is not removed by these matters, nor is rebellion against him obligatory, rather admonition and warning him are obligatory, and disobedience to him in anything he calls to from the disobedience of Allah. And they argued for that with many widespread reports from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and from his companions regarding the obligation of obeying the imams even if they are unjust and take wealth for themselves, and that he said, peace be upon him: 'Listen and obey even a mutilated slave, even a slave from Abyssinia, and pray behind every righteous and wicked person.' And it was narrated that he said: 'Obey them even if they eat your wealth and strike your back, and obey them as long as they establish prayer.' In many reports reported in this chapter. And we have mentioned what is in this chapter in the book 'Takfir al-Muta'awwilin' and mentioned what was narrated in opposition to them, and we said regarding their interpretation that which suffices the observer, God willing.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents the opposing view. The majority (al-jumhoor) hold that the imam is not removed by the grounds listed earlier. Instead, they must be admonished, but obedience is required in what is not sinful. They cite multiple hadith commanding obedience even to unjust leaders. The author notes he has treated this topic elsewhere.

Key Terms
جمهور أهل الإثبات
أصحاب الحديث
طاعة الأئمة
لا ينخلع
وعظ وتخويف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith cited require unconditional obedience even in sin.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'disobedience in anything he calls to from the disobedience of Allah', so sinful commands must not be obeyed.

Checkpoint
What does the majority view say about an unjust imam?
A) Must be removed
B) Must be obeyed unconditionally
C) Must be admonished and obeyed except in disobedience
D) Rebellion is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The majority of Sunni scholars hold that an unjust imam should not be removed but admonished and disobeyed only in sinful commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a leader or authority figure in your life treats you unjustly—whether at work, in your family, or in the community—is your first instinct to rebel, complain, or quietly obey? Which of the hadith in this lesson cuts against your natural reaction?
?
The Prophet commanded obedience even to a mutilated Abyssinian slave. What does this command reveal about the pride and craving for fairness in your heart? Are you willing to obey Allah's command even when the one over you seems undeserving of your respect?
?
You will face a situation where someone in authority asks you to do something wrong. Will you have the courage to disobey in that specific matter, while still honoring the general command to obey? Write down one sin you would refuse even if it cost you personally.

Grounds for Removing an Imam
Baqillani enumerates physical and mental causes for removing an imam and the aftermath of recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific conditions that necessitate the removal of an imam according to Baqillani, and the ruling if those conditions are later removed.

Core Idea
Removal of an imam requires a concrete, impairing condition; reversed conditions do not restore his authority.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani outlines the legal grounds that justify removing a sitting imam. He focuses on physical and mental incapacities that prevent him from fulfilling his duties, and also addresses the fate of an imam who is captured. Understanding these conditions helps clarify the stability of political authority in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage twice: first for overall flow, then for specific details. Note that Baqillani starts with 'Mimma yujibu khalʿ al-imām aydan' (And among what necessitates removal of the imam as well).
This passage continues from a previous discussion; 'aydan' connects to earlier points.
2
List the conditions for removal
Identify each cause mentioned: insanity with harm or hopelessness, deafness/dumbness, extreme old age, any condition preventing governance, and prolonged captivity.
Note that these are all physical or mental incapacities, not moral or comparative.
3
Understand the rule on recovery
Baqillani states that if the imam recovers (e.g., from insanity or captivity), he does not return to power but becomes a subject. Why? Because the contract was dissolved.
This emphasizes finality of removal once the condition is met and acted upon.
4
Reflect on the rationale
Ask yourself: Why are these specific conditions chosen? Because they directly prevent the imam from serving public interests. Removal is tied to the purpose of the imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوجب خلع الإمام أيضاً تطابق الجنونِ عليه وذهاب تمييزه وبلوغه في ذلك إلى مدةٍ يضر المسلمين زوال عقله فيها أو يُؤذن باليأس من صحته وكذلك القول فيه إذا صمَّ أو خرس وكبر وهرم أو عرض له أمر يقطع عن النظر في مصالح المسلمين والنهوض بما نصب لأجله أو عن بعضه لأنه إنما أقيم لهذه الأمور فإذا عطّل وجب خلعه ونصب غيره وكذلك إن حصل مأسوراً في يد العدو إلى مدة يخاف معها الضرر الداخل على الأمة ويوأس معها من الاستبدال به فإن فك أسره أو ثاب عقله أو برىء من مرضه وزمانَتِهِ لم يَعُدْ إلى أمره وكان رعية للوالي بعده لأنه عُقد له عند خلعه وخروجه من الحق فلا حق له فيه . وليس مما يوجب خلع الإمام حدوث فضل في غيره ويصير به أفضل منه وإن كان لو حصل مفضولاً عند ابتداء العقد لوجب العدول عنه إلى الفاضل لأن تزايد الفضل في غيره ليس بحدّث منه في الدين ولا في نفسه خلعه ومثل هذا ما حكيناه عن أصحابنا أن حدوث الفسق في الإمام بعد العقد له لا يوجب خلعه وإن كان مما لو حدث فيه عند ابتداء العقد لبطل العقد له ووجب العدول عنه وأمثال هذا في الشريعة كثيرة ألا ترى أنه لو وجد المتيمم الماء قبل دخوله في الصلاة لوجب عليه التوضؤ به ولو طرأ عليه وهو فيها، لم يلزمه ذلك؟ وكذلك لو وجبت عليه الرقبة في كفارته وهو موسر لم يجز غيرها ولو حدث اليسار بعد مضيه في شيء من الصيام لم يبطل حكم صيامه ولا يلزمه غير ما دخل فيه في أمثال لهذا كثيرة. وكذلك حكم القول في حدوث الفضل على الإمام بعد العقد له.
And among what necessitates the removal of the imam as well: the occurrence of insanity upon him, the loss of his discernment, and his reaching in that a period in which the loss of his intellect harms the Muslims or gives cause for despair of his recovery; and likewise the ruling concerning him if he becomes deaf or dumb, or grows old and senile, or there occurs to him an affliction that cuts him off from considering the interests of the Muslims and undertaking what he was appointed for—or from some of it—because he was only established for these matters, so if he is incapacitated, his removal is obligatory and the appointment of another. And likewise if he becomes a captive in the hands of the enemy for a period during which harm to the ummah is feared and despair of his replacement arises; then if his captivity ends, or his intellect returns, or he recovers from his illness and infirmity, he does not return to his authority, but becomes a subject of the ruler after him, because the contract was dissolved upon his removal and his exit from the right, so no right in it remains for him. And it is not among what necessitates the removal of the imam: the occurrence of superior virtue in another, whereby that other becomes better than him; even if he had been inferior at the beginning of the contract, it would have been necessary to turn away from him to the superior, because the increase of virtue in another is not an innovation in religion nor in himself that would remove him. And similar to this is what we related from our companions: that the occurrence of disobedience (fisq) in the imam after the contract for him does not necessitate his removal, even if it were that if it had occurred at the beginning of the contract, it would have invalidated the contract for him and made it necessary to turn away from him. And examples of this in the Sharia are many. Do you not see that if the one performing tayammum finds water before entering into prayer, it is obligatory for him to perform wudu with it; but if it occurs to him while he is in it, that is not required of him? And likewise, if a slave is obligatory upon him as an expiation and he is wealthy, it is not sufficient other than it; but if wealth occurs after he has proceeded into something of the fasting, the ruling of his fasting is not invalidated and nothing other than what he has entered into is required of him, in many such examples. And such is the ruling concerning the occurrence of virtue over the imam after the contract for him.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theological exposition on the conditions that necessitate the removal of an imam and a refutation of the idea that superior virtue in another person alone constitutes grounds for removal. Baqillani begins by enumerating clear incapacities that justify removal: insanity (with loss of intellect for a duration harmful to Muslims or giving no hope of recovery), deafness, dumbness, extreme old age and senility, any condition that prevents the imam from attending to the welfare of Muslims and fulfilling his duties (or even part of them), and captivity by the enemy for a period that endangers the ummah and prevents replacement. He then stipulates that if these conditions are later removed—captivity ends, sanity returns, illness cured—the imam does not regain his position; rather, he becomes a subject of the new ruler because the contract was legally dissolved upon his removal. Next, Baqillani addresses a potential objection: what if someone else becomes more virtuous after the imam's appointment? He rules that this does not require removal, even though if the imam had been inferior at the time of contract it would have been obligatory to choose the superior. The reason is that the increase of virtue in another is not a defect that occurs in the imam himself or a new development in religion. He supports this with parallel rulings from fiqh: if a person performing tayammum finds water before starting prayer, he must use water; but if he finds it during prayer, it is not required. Similarly, if a person must free a slave as expiation and he has wealth, only a slave suffices; but if he becomes wealthy after beginning the alternative of fasting, he does not need to switch. These examples show that the timing of a change in condition matters—what would invalidate at the outset does not necessarily invalidate after commencement. Thus, the occurrence of virtue in another after the imam's contract does not trigger removal.

Key Terms
خلع الإمام (khalʿ al-imām)
الجنون (al-junūn)
أهل الحل والعقد (ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that an imam can be removed simply because another person is more virtuous.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that removal is only for concrete incapacities, not for superior virtue, which is the subject of the next passage.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which of the following necessitates the removal of an imam?
A) Another person becoming more virtuous
B) The imam becoming deaf or dumb
C) A decrease in the imam's religious knowledge
D) The imam being disliked by some people

Key Takeaway
Removal of an imam requires a specific, incapacitating condition; once removed, recovery does not restore the position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you held a position of responsibility, would you have the honesty to step down when you become incapacitated, even if it meant losing your status permanently? What would your heart struggle with?
?
The imam's removal is for the sake of the community's welfare, not his own. Are there roles you cling to out of pride or fear, even when you are no longer able to serve those who depend on you?
?
After recovery, the former imam becomes a subject—a complete demotion. Could you accept such a transition with humility and support the one who replaced you? What would that require of your iman?

Superior Virtue and Fisq Do Not Require Removal
Baqillani argues that superior virtue or fisq in another does not necessitate removal, with analogy to tayammum and expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of superior virtue or disobedience in another person after an imam's appointment does not necessitate his removal, and how this parallels other Islamic legal principles.

Core Idea
A change in external circumstances after a contract does not undo what has been validly established.

After listing physical incapacities that justify removal, Baqillani turns to a potential objection: what if someone else becomes more virtuous or the imam becomes sinful? He argues that neither requires removal, drawing analogies from the rulings of tayammum and expiation. The key principle is that conditions that would invalidate a contract at its outset do not necessarily invalidate it if they arise later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and Baqillani's response
The passage begins 'Wa laysa mimmā yūjibu khalʿ al-imām ḥudūthu faḍlin fī ghayrihī' (And it is not among what necessitates removal...). Read the initial statement and then the reason given.
Baqillani contrasts the scenario if inferiority existed at the time of contract (would require choosing the superior) vs. after (does not).
2
Understand the reasoning
The increase of virtue in another is 'not an innovation in religion nor in himself that would remove him.' In other words, it's not a defect in the imam's person or his religious standing.
3
Examine the parallel with fisq (disobedience)
Baqillani supports the same rule for fisq: occurrence after the contract does not necessitate removal, even if it would have at the start. He notes 'Amthāl hādhā fī al-sharīʿa kathīrah' (examples in Sharia are many).
4
Analyze the examples: tayammum and expiation
The tayammum example: if water is found before prayer, wudu is required; if during prayer, it is not. Similarly for expiation: if wealth comes after starting fasting, the fast is still valid. These show that timing matters—what would invalidate at the start may not disrupt what has begun.
Note the principle: contract validity is based on conditions at inception; later changes do not retroactively invalidate.
5
Apply the principle to the imamate
Thus, the occurrence of virtue or sin in another person after the imam's appointment does not require removal, because the contract was valid at its time and is not undone by subsequent external developments.

Source Text

Twofold Evidence for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Baqillani presents the evidence for Abu Bakr's imamah via candidate and electors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold evidence Baqillani presents for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate: the qualifications of the candidate and the credibility of the electors.

Core Idea
Legitimate leadership requires both a qualified candidate and credible electors.

In this passage, Baqillani begins his defense of Abu Bakr's caliphate by addressing a hypothetical skeptic. Instead of appealing to explicit texts, he grounds his argument in the objective qualifications of the leader and the integrity of those who elected him. This approach reflects the Sunni emphasis on the community's role in selecting the caliph.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
The passage presents two separate lines of evidence for Abu Bakr's imamah. Find them both. Note how Baqillani introduces the objection first.
2
Understand the concept of ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd
Baqillani refers to the electors as among the most excellent of the people of binding and loosening. This term denotes those qualified to appoint or depose a leader in Sunni political theory.
3
Notice the historical context: the Saqifa
The passage mentions the day of the Saqifa among the Ansar. This was the gathering where Abu Bakr was elected. Baqillani specifies that the electors were Umar and Abu Ubayda to emphasize their credibility.
4
Consider the implication: validity through qualification
Baqillani does not claim that the election was unanimous. Rather, he argues it was valid because the key electors were themselves qualified and the candidate was supremely qualified.
This argument is a standard Sunni defense against Shi'a claims of explicit appointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه السقيفة إن قال قائل : ما الدليل على إثبات إمامة أبي بكر وأن العقد له وقع موقعاً صحيحاً قيل له : الدليل على ذلك أنه بصفة من يصلح للإمامة وزيادة عليها بما سنصفه فيما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى وان العاقدين له الأمر يوم من أفاضل أهل الحل والعقد ممن يصلح أيضاً لإمامة المسلمين والتقدم عليهم . وهم عمر بن الخطاب وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح بمحضر من بشير بن ... يوم السقيفة من الأنصار.
Chapter: The discourse on the imamah of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, and the Saqifa [incident]. If someone says: What is the evidence for establishing the imamah of Abu Bakr and that the contract for him occurred in a valid manner? It is said to him: The evidence for that is that he was characterized by who is suitable for the imamah and more, as we will describe later, God willing; and that those who contracted for him the authority on that day were among the most excellent of the people of binding and loosening (ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd), such that they are also suitable for leading the Muslims and being put forward over them. They were Umar ibn al-Khattab and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah in the presence of Bashir ibn ... on the day of the Saqifa among the Ansar.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a new chapter that introduces the topic of Abu Bakr's caliphate. Baqillani presents a hypothetical objection: 'What is the evidence that Abu Bakr's imamah is valid?' The answer is twofold. First, Abu Bakr himself possessed the qualifications necessary for leadership (and even surpassed them, as will be detailed later). Second, those who conducted the contract of allegiance (bay'ah) on that day at the Saqifa were among the most excellent of the people of authority (ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd)—specifically Umar ibn al-Khattab and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah—and they were themselves fit to lead. Moreover, the bay'ah took place in the presence of Bashir (likely Bashir ibn Sa'd) among the Ansar, implying the consent of the community. This argument establishes the validity of Abu Bakr's appointment by appealing to the fitness of the candidate and the credibility of the electors, which are standard criteria for legitimate leadership according to Sunni political theory.

Key Terms
إمامة
أهل الحل والعقد
السقيفة
البيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sunni evidence for Abu Bakr's caliphate is based solely on explicit hadith.
Correction: Baqillani instead argues from the qualifications of the candidate and the credibility of the electors, which are rational and historical proofs.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what are the two lines of evidence for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's imamah?
A) Abu Bakr's seniority and the Prophet's explicit appointment
B) Abu Bakr's personal fitness and the qualifications of the electors
C) The unanimous agreement of the entire community and the absence of opposition
D) The superiority of the Quraysh and the Ansar's submission

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Abu Bakr's caliphate by arguing that the candidate was supremely qualified and the electors were among the most credible authorities, making the contract valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that valid leadership requires both a qualified candidate and credible electors. Think of a leader you follow in your community or online: have you honestly assessed both their qualifications and the credibility of those who put them in that position? What if you have been following someone based only on popularity or surface qualities? Are you ready to re-evaluate that choice?
?
Baqillani grounds his argument in rational evidence rather than blind tradition. When it comes to matters of your faith or life decisions, do you demand the same level of reasoning, or do you accept things because 'that's how it's always been'? What is one area where you need to move from blind acceptance to evidence-based conviction?
?
The electors (Umar and Abu Ubayda) were themselves fit to lead, which gave weight to their decision. In your own life, whose advice do you trust? Have you ever followed someone who was not themselves qualified in the matter you sought guidance on? Reflect on a time when you relied on an unqualified advisor. How can you ensure that the people you listen to are themselves credible?

The Proof for Abu Bakr's Imamate: Qualifications and Contract
Baqillani presents the proof for Abu Bakr's imamate in response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two-part proof for Abu Bakr's imamate: his personal qualifications and the authority of those who performed the contract.

Core Idea
The imamate of Abu Bakr was validly contracted by the most qualified representatives.

Baqillani opens this chapter by anticipating a skeptical question: what is the evidence that Abu Bakr's imamate is valid? He presents a structured answer. The proof rests on two pillars: Abu Bakr's own fitness for leadership, and the fact that the contract was made by the most eminent members of the community (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). This lesson unpacks that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Locate the questioner's challenge and Baqillani's opening response. Notice the structure: the evidence is given in two parts.
The objection is introduced with 'إن قال قائل'.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
First, Abu Bakr's own qualifications. Second, the qualifications of those who contracted for him. The text says 'الدليل على ذلك أنه بصفة من يصلح للإمامة... وأن العاقدين له الأمر...'
The second part is introduced by 'وأن'.
3
Analyze the list of contractors
Note the names: Umar, Abu Ubayda, and the prominent Ansar present. They are described as 'أفاضل أهل الحل والعقد'. Reflect on why their role is central to the proof.
This emphasizes that the contract was made by qualified individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه. السقيفة. إن قال قائل: ما الدليل على إثبات إمامة أبي بكر وأن العقد له وقع موقعاً صحيحاً؟ قيل له: الدليل على ذلك أنه بصفة من يصلح للإمامة وزيادة عليها بما سنصفه فيما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى، وأن العاقدين له الأمر يوم هم من أفاضل أهل الحل والعقد ممن يصلح أيضاً لإمامة المسلمين والتقدم عليهم. وهم عمر بن الخطاب وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح بمحضر من بشير بن سعد وأسيد بن الحضير وعمران بن الحصين وغيرهم من الأنصار ومن حضر من المهاجرين، وأن هذا العقد وقع بمحضر من جمهور الأمة وأهل القدوة منهم، ولم ينكره منكر ولا قدح فيه قادح، بل تتابعوا على البيعة من ساعتهم وبقية يومهم، وأذعنت له الأنصار وانقادت بعد خلافها وغلطها فيه المتفق عليه؛ لأنها أرادت إخراج الأمر عن قريش ونصب إمامين في وقت واحد. وقال الحباب بن المنذر منهم: «منا أمير ومنكم أمير»، وهذا غلط حاولوه باتفاق المسلمين، فلو أقاموا عليه وخالفوا أبا بكر بعد عَقْدٍ من عقد له الأمر، لوجب أن يكونوا في ذلك آثمين ولوجب حربهم وقتالهم إلى أن يرجعوا عن البغي وشق العصا؛ لأن العقد قد تم لمن حضر وعقد، وقد دللنا على ذلك من قبل، فلا نحتاج في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر إلى وقوع الإجماع عليها، ولا نستضر ولا نستوحش من خلاف مخالف فيها؛ غير أن الله قد وقى كل أحد من ذلك الوقت مواقعة هذه المعصية ووفَّرَ دواعي الصحابة على ذلك في المسلمين وجمع.
Chapter on the imamate of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him. The Saqifa. If a questioner says: What is the evidence for establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr and that the contract for him occurred in a valid manner? It is said to him: The evidence for that is that he was of the quality of one fit for the imamate and more, as we will describe later, God willing, and that those who contracted the affair for him on that day were among the eminent people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) who are also fit for the imamate of the Muslims and leadership over them. They were Umar ibn al-Khattab and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah in the presence of Bashir ibn Sa'd, Usayd ibn Hudayr, Imran ibn Husayn, and others of the Ansar and those present among the Muhajirun. And this contract occurred in the presence of the majority of the community and the people of example among them, and no denier denied it, nor did any critic criticize it. Rather, they rushed to the bay'ah from that hour and the rest of their day, and the Ansar submitted and yielded after their disagreement and their acknowledged error in it; because they wanted to remove the affair from Quraysh and appoint two leaders at the same time. And al-Hubab ibn al-Mundhir among them said: 'From us a commander and from you a commander.' This was an error they attempted by the consensus of the Muslims. If they had persisted in it and opposed Abu Bakr after a contract had been made by one who contracted the affair for him, they would have been sinful in that, and it would have been obligatory to fight them and wage war against them until they returned from transgression and breaking the staff (of unity); because the contract had been completed by those present who contracted it. And we have demonstrated that before, so we do not need for establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr the occurrence of consensus upon it, and we are not harmed nor uneasy about the opposition of an opponent in it. However, God has protected everyone from that time from committing this sin, and He made the motives of the Companions abundant for that among the Muslims, and He united.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This is a proof passage establishing the validity of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author begins by stating the chapter heading and subheading 'The Saqifa', then presents an objection: what is the evidence that Abu Bakr's imamate is correct? The answer provides the proof. First, Abu Bakr himself possessed the qualifications for leadership and more. Second, those who contracted the bay'ah for him were among the most qualified people (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd), specifically Umar and Abu Ubayda, and the contract occurred in the presence of prominent Ansar and Muhajirun. The contract was witnessed by the majority of the community, and no one opposed it. The Ansar initially disagreed but then submitted after realizing their error. The author mentions that the Ansar wanted to appoint two leaders, one from them and one from Quraysh, but this was unanimously rejected. If they had persisted, they would have been sinful and it would have been obligatory to fight them. The author emphasizes that the contract was already completed by those present, so universal consensus (ijma') is not required for establishing the imamate. He concludes by noting that God protected everyone from committing that sin and united the Companions.

Key Terms
إمامة
عقد
أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, who were the two main individuals who contracted the imamate for Abu Bakr?
A) Umar ibn al-Khattab and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah
B) Abu Bakr and Umar
C) Bashir ibn Sa'd and Usayd ibn Hudayr
D) The Ansar and the Muhajirun

Key Takeaway
The validity of Abu Bakr's imamate is established by the contract of qualified representatives, not by universal acclaim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the leadership of your own community, do you trust that the qualified representatives (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) are capable of making the right choice, or do you insist on your own opinion? What prevents you from submitting to their decision?
?
If you had been present at Saqifa, would you have recognized Abu Bakr's qualifications and accepted the contract, or would you have objected based on personal bias? What does your answer reveal about your own humility before God's decree?
?
The passage shows that the validity of Abu Bakr's imamate rested on a contract by those who were fit for leadership. In your life, are you seeking counsel from those qualified in matters of faith, or are you relying solely on your own understanding? How will you change this starting today?

The Saqifa: Answering Opposition and the Sufficiency of the Contract
Baqillani explains the Ansar's opposition and concludes that universal consensus is not required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's response to the Ansar's initial opposition and why he concludes that universal consensus is not required for Abu Bakr's imamate.

Core Idea
The contract by qualified representatives suffices; opposition after the fact does not invalidate it.

This lesson completes Baqillani's argument by addressing the dissent of the Ansar at the Saqifa. He explains that their objection was based on a mistaken desire to split leadership, and that once the contract was completed, any persistent opposition would have been sinful rebellion. He concludes that the imamate is valid without requiring universal consensus, as God protected the community from such schism.

Guided Path
1
Read the event description
Read how the contract occurred in the presence of the majority and how the Ansar initially opposed but later submitted.
Note the phrase 'بمحر من جمهور الأمة'.
2
Understand the Ansar's error
Identify the Ansar's claim (two leaders) and Baqillani's description of it as a unanimous error.
The quote from al-Hubab is key.
3
Grasp the conditional consequence
If they had persisted, they would be sinful and it would be obligatory to fight them. Understand why: the contract was already completed.
This shows the seriousness of opposing a valid contract.
4
Learn the conclusion
Baqillani states that universal consensus is not needed; the contract by those present is sufficient. He ends with gratitude that God protected unity.
This directly refutes the misconception that ijma' is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the imamate of Abu Bakr requires universal consensus (ijma') from all Muslims at the time of the contract.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly says: 'لَا نَحْتَاجُ فِي إِثْبَاتِ إِمَامَةِ أَبِي بَكْرٍ إِلَى وُقُوعِ الْإِجْمَاعِ عَلَيْهَا' – we do not need universal consensus; the contract by qualified individuals suffices.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would have been required if the Ansar had persisted in their opposition after the contract?
A) They would be forgiven if they repented.
B) It would be obligatory to fight them until they returned from transgression.
C) The contract would be invalidated.
D) A new election would be held.

Key Takeaway
The contract of the qualified suffices; subsequent opposition does not invalidate it, and universal consensus is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in the minority opposing a decision that has been legitimately made by qualified people, do you submit like the Ansar, or do you persist in your error? What stops you from accepting the truth when it comes through others?
?
The author says 'we are not harmed nor uneasy about the opposition of an opponent.' Is your faith in the truth strong enough that you feel secure even when many oppose it? Where in your life do you let opposition shake your confidence in what is right?
?
The Ansar's initial error was rooted in a desire to keep authority within their group. In your own life, do you sometimes reject valid leadership or truth simply because it comes from outside your circle? How will you actively guard against that bias starting today?

The Contract and Consensus for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
The author defends Abu Bakr's caliphate using the contract and consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani defends Abu Bakr's caliphate based on the contract of allegiance and the lack of valid opposition, and why explicit consensus is not required.

Core Idea
The contract of allegiance itself suffices; consensus is not needed for Abu Bakr's imamate.

Imam al-Baqillani presents a historical argument for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. He points to the immediate pledge of allegiance by the majority of the Companions, the absence of valid opposition, and the recognition that the Ansar's initial proposal was an error. This passage shows that the contract completed in the presence of many is enough to establish the imamate, without needing explicit consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic text once, noting the names and key terms. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
The passage is dense with references to historical figures and events.
2
Identify the main claim
The author's central claim is that the contract of allegiance for Abu Bakr took place in the presence of many and was not denied. Identify how he argues that even potential opposition does not undermine it.
3
Examine the argument about the Ansar
Notice how the author addresses the initial disagreement of the Ansar. He calls it an error and explains that if they had persisted, they would be sinful and subject to fighting. This shows the seriousness of opposing the established contract.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that consensus is not required to prove the imamate; the contract itself suffices. Allah protected the Companions from persisting in error and united them in obedience.
This is a key point in Sunni political theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سعد وأسيد بن الحضير؛ وعمران بن الحصين وغيرهم من الأنصار ومن حضر من المهاجرين وأن هذا العقد وقع بمحضر من جمهور الأمة وأهل القدوة منهم ولم ينكره منكر ولا قدح فيه قادح بل تتابعوا على البيعة من ساعتهم وبقية يومهم، وأذعنت له الأنصار وانقادت بعد خلافها وغلطها فيه المتفق عليه؛ لأنها أرادت إخراج الأمر عن قريش ونصب إمامين في وقت واحد. وقال الحباب بن المنذر منهم : «منا أمير ومنكم أمير»، وهذا غلط حاولوه باتفاق المسلمين فلو أقاموا عليه وخالفوا أبا بكر بعد عَقْدِ من عقد له الأمر، لوجب أن يكونوا في ذلك آثمين ولوجب حربهم وقتالهم إلى أن يرجعوا عن البغي وشق العصا؛ لأن العقد قد تم لمن حضر وعقد، وقد دللنا على ذلك من قبل فلا نحتاج في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر إلى وقوع الإجماع عليها، ولا نستضـر ولا نستوحش من خلاف مخالف فيها ; غير أن الله قد وقى كل أحد من ذلك الوقت مواقعة هذه المعصية ووفَّرَ دواعي الصحابة على ذلك في المسلمين وجمع همتهم على طاعته والإذعان للحق الذي لزمهم الانقياد له.
Sa'd and Usayd ibn al-Hudayr, and 'Imran ibn al-Husayn, and others from the Ansar and those present from the Muhajirun, and that this contract took place in the presence of the majority of the Ummah and the people of example among them, and no denier denied it nor any critic criticized it. Rather, they proceeded with the pledge of allegiance from that very hour and the rest of their day, and the Ansar submitted and complied after their disagreement and error in it were agreed upon; because they wanted to remove the matter from Quraysh and appoint two imams at once. And al-Hubab ibn al-Mundhir said among them: 'From us an amir and from you an amir.' This was an error they attempted according to the consensus of the Muslims. If they had persisted in it and opposed Abu Bakr after the contract of one to whom the matter was contracted, they would have been sinful in that, and it would have been obligatory to fight them until they returned from transgression and splitting the staff; because the contract had been completed for those who were present and contracted. And we have already demonstrated that before, so we do not need, in proving the imamate of Abu Bakr, for consensus to have occurred upon it, nor are we harmed nor frightened by the opposition of an opposer in it; but Allah has protected everyone from that time from committing this disobedience and facilitated the motives of the Companions towards that in the Muslims and united their determination upon his obedience and submission to the truth which they were obligated to follow.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument defending the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author begins by listing witnesses from both the Ansar and Muhajirun who were present at the pledge. He emphasizes that no one denied or criticized the contract. The Ansar initially disagreed wanting to appoint an amir from themselves and one from Quraysh, but this was a recognized error. The author then states that if they had persisted, they would be sinful and fighting them would be obligatory. He argues that the contract was already completed by those present, so consensus is not needed to prove Abu Bakr's imamate. Opposition does not harm it. He concludes by saying that Allah protected everyone from that sin and united the Companions in obedience.

Key Terms
البيعة
الإمامة
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Sunni doctrine requires explicit consensus of all Muslims to validate a caliph's imamate.
Correction: The author argues that the contract (al-‘aqd) concluded by those present and the lack of valid opposition is sufficient; consensus is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would have been required if the Ansar had persisted in their opposition after the contract?
A) Negotiation and dialogue
B) They would be sinful and must be fought until they return from rebellion
C) A new pledge of allegiance
D) They would be forgiven as ijtihad error

Key Takeaway
The contract of allegiance for Abu Bakr, witnessed by many and accepted by the Ummah, is sufficient to establish his imamate without needing formal consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a lawful authority (in religion or society) that you initially disagree with, do you submit as the Ansar did, or do you persist in your error? What would it take for you to recognize a legitimate contract of allegiance in your own life?
?
The author says Allah protected the Companions from committing disobedience by accepting Abu Bakr. Is there an area in your life where you are resisting submission to truth, fearing that if you persist Allah might need to humble you? Are you ready to submit before that?
?
If you were in the position of the Ansar, with a strong opinion that could split the community, would you have the humility to admit error and follow the majority's contract? What prevents you from that humility today?

Refuting Claims of Delayed Allegiance by Weak Reports
The author refutes claims of delayed allegiance by Ali using weakness of reports and necessity of widespread transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why weak isolated reports cannot be used to accuse the Sahaba of sin, using the principles of their established justice and the necessity of widespread transmission for grave matters.

Core Idea
The justice of the Sahaba cannot be overridden by weak isolated reports.

Imam Baqillani addresses a historical claim that certain Companions delayed pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr. He argues that such claims rest on weak, isolated reports that contradict the established justice of the Sahaba. This lesson unpacks his logical and methodological rejection, grounding it in principles of hadith criticism and communal memory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the strong opening language: 'It is not permissible...'
2
Identify the two main arguments
The author presents two key arguments: (1) the established justice of the Sahaba cannot be undermined by weak reports, and (2) such a grave matter would have required widespread transmission.
The first is a principle of adalat al-sahaba; the second is a historical reasoning based on tawatur.
3
Reflect on the principle of adalat al-sahaba
The author asserts that once a Companion's faith, piety, and justice are established, they cannot be accused of sin based on isolated reports. This is a foundational principle in Sunni theology.
This principle is extended to other reports like Ibn Mas'ud's removal of the mu'awwidhatayn.
4
Understand the argument from widespread transmission
If 'Ali's delay had truly occurred, it would have been widely known and transmitted. The fact that only weak, contradictory reports exist shows the claim is false.
This is a powerful methodological argument: the absence of widespread transmission for a claim that would necessarily be famous indicates fabrication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس يجوز لمسلم اتقى الله أن يضيف إلى علي بن أبي طالب عليه السلام والزبير بن العوام التأخر عن بيعته بأخبار آحاد واهية مجيئها من ناحية متهومة; لأن تأخرهم عن البيعة مع ما وصفناه من صحة وثبوتها ضرب من الإثم والعصيان وليس يمكن إضافة معصية إلى الصحابة بمثل هذا الطريق, لا سيما إذا رووا مع ذلك أن أبا بكر عليه السلام كان يدعوهم إلى الطاعة ولزوم عليهم تأخرهم ولا يسوغهم ذلك, وكذلك يجب أن ينفى عن مسعود إخراجه المُعَوِّذَتَين من المصحف ومخالفته الجماعة. وكل أمر روي عن الصحابة فيه تأثيم وقذف بعصيان, فيجب أن نبطله وننفيه إذا ورد ورود الآحاد لأن من ثبت إيمانه وبره وعدالته لا يُفَسِّقُ بأخبار الآحاد . وعلى أن نعلم بواضح النظر كذب من ادعى تأخر علي والعباس والزبير لأن مثل هذا الخطب الجسيم في مثل هذا الأمر العظيم. يجب إشهاره وظهوره, وأن ينقل نقل مثله ; فكيف حفظت الأمة بأسرها وعلمت مخالفة علي لأبي بكر وغيره من الصحابة في حكم أم الولد والتوريث الذي إنما تعلمه الخاصة وذهب عنها عِلْمُ تأخره وتأخر الزبير عن البيعة حتى لا يَرِدُ إلا ورداً شاذاً ضعيفاً, وتكون الأخبار الكثيرة في معارضته ومناقضته والعادة جارية بلزوم مثل هذا للقلوب وإطلاق الألسن بذكره واشتهاره وإظهاره دون طيه وكتمانه
It is not permissible for a Muslim who fears Allah to attribute to 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, peace be upon him, and al-Zubayr ibn al-'Awwam, delay in his pledge of allegiance, based on weak isolated reports whose transmission is from a suspect source; because their delay in the pledge of allegiance, given what we have described of its validity and establishment, is a type of sin and disobedience, and it is not possible to attribute sin to the Companions through such a path, especially when they also narrate that Abu Bakr, peace be upon him, was calling them to obedience and that their delay was incumbent upon them and he did not permit it for them. Likewise, it must be denied of Mas'ud his removal of the Mu'awwidhatayn from the Mushaf and his opposition to the congregation. And every matter narrated from the Companions involving accusation of sin and defamation of disobedience, we must invalidate and deny it if it comes as isolated reports, because one whose faith, piety, and justice are established is not declared sinful by isolated reports. Moreover, we know by clear reasoning the falsehood of the claim of delay of 'Ali, al-'Abbas, and al-Zubayr, because such a grave matter in such a great affair must be publicized and known, and transmitted as similar events are transmitted. So how can the entire Ummah preserve and know the opposition of 'Ali to Abu Bakr and other Companions regarding the ruling of the mother of a child and inheritance, which only the elite know, while knowledge of his delay and the delay of al-Zubayr from the pledge of allegiance escaped them, so that it only comes as a weak, isolated report, while there are many reports contradicting and opposing it, and the norm would be for such to stick in hearts and tongues become loose with its mention and prominence and manifestation, rather than being concealed and hidden?
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage refutes claims that 'Ali, al-'Abbas, and al-Zubayr delayed in pledging allegiance. The author states that such claims are based on weak isolated reports (akhbar ahad) from suspect sources. Accusing the Sahaba of sin cannot be done through such reports, especially when there are reports that Abu Bakr called them to obey. He extends this principle to other reports like Ibn Mas'ud removing the mu'awwidhatayn from the Quran—such reports must be denied if based on weak reports because the Sahaba are known for their justice. He then uses a reasoning based on the necessity of widespread transmission: if such a grave matter as 'Ali's delay had occurred, it would have been widely known, not just through a weak isolated report. The fact that only weak reports exist while many contradict them shows the falsity of the claim.

Key Terms
أخبار آحاد
عدالة الصحابة
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any historical report, even if weak, can be used to establish facts about the Sahaba.
Correction: The author argues that the established justice of the Sahaba means they cannot be accused of sin based on weak isolated reports; moreover, grave matters require widespread transmission, and the weakness of the reports indicates fabrication.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject reports that 'Ali delayed in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr?
A) Because the reports are contradicted by the Quran
B) Because they are weak isolated reports that cannot override the established justice of the Sahaba
C) Because 'Ali himself denied it
D) Because Abu Bakr forbade such reports

Key Takeaway
Weak isolated reports cannot override the established justice of the Sahaba, and grave historical claims require widespread transmission to be credible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed a negative report about someone you knew to be pious, without demanding strong evidence? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate the standards you use before accepting accusations against others?
?
Think of a time when you spread or shared a weak report about someone. What would it cost you to verify such reports before acting on them? Are you willing to adopt a principle of requiring widespread proof before believing grave allegations against a person of established character?
?
This lesson teaches that one whose faith and justice are established cannot be declared sinful by weak reports. Who in your life do you consider to have established justice? Would you defend them against weak accusations as the author defends the Sahaba? What practical step will you take the next time you hear such a report?
